# Someone Else's Mouth

# Chapter 1

The software kept turning "public housing" into "pubic housing," and by the third correction Lena decided the man in the training video deserved whatever humiliation it tried on him next. Sweat kept sliding down the side of her ribcage and sticking her tank top to the chair. The box fan in the window shoved hot air from Moore Street straight at her face, then clicked on the turn and shoved it back again. It did not cool anything. It only moved the heat around so she could feel it in shifts.

Below her, the tailor shop rolled toward closing. Steam hissed once through the floorboards. Somebody laughed on the sidewalk. A SEPTA bus dragged itself up Broad with that worn metal complaint city buses had when August had baked them too long. Lena kept her headphones on and listened to the councilman in the file say community investment with a mouth full of saliva. The waveform swelled blue and green on her monitor. She backed up four seconds, fixed a dropped not, put the period outside the quotation marks because the client wanted that for some insane reason, and drank coffee that had gone sour and skin-warm in the mug.

The quarterly estimate sat on the table behind her under a saucer with a crack through it. She did not need to turn around to know the number. She had written it twice in black pen and once again in red as if color might make it smaller.

$4,200 by October 15.

That gave her six weeks, if she was being generous about what counted as a week.

Her phone lit once beside the keyboard and then again. They were not calls. It was app noise. She had shoved Hinge into a folder with banking, parking tickets, and the PECO app, as if romance and utilities belonged under the same little square of forced optimism. The screen flashed a partial message from a man named Chris who had opened with Nice smile. He had no chance of a response, and she resented him anyway for existing in the same minute as the tax bill.

There was a knock that was not really a knock. June hit the door twice with the side of her fist, then once with her heel.

"If you're dead, I'm eating both orders," she called.

Lena paused the file. "Come in, then, vulture."

June came through the door backward with a sweating plastic takeout bag hanging from one wrist and two fountain sodas cradled against her chest. She was compact and fast even when the heat should have slowed her, dark hair frizzing loose at the temples, work boots unlaced at the top. Her fingers were yellowed from cigarettes in a way she never bothered to hide. She set the drinks down, shook out the takeout containers, and pocketed the extra chopsticks without looking at them. June stole spare chopsticks from every restaurant in South Philly because, according to her, forks vanished in a one-bedroom but chopsticks respected the home.

She sniffed once. "Jesus, Len. It smells like hot dust and burnt headphones in here."

"That's the smell of literacy."

"That's the smell of your fan trying to sue you."

Lena pushed the headphones down around her neck. The skin beneath them felt tender, dented. "I've got twenty-eight more minutes on this file."

"Then you'll eat while I judge you." June slid one container toward her with a plastic fork anyway. Cold sesame noodles, cucumbers gone slick in the heat, dumplings already sweating through their wrappers. They were better than cooking and better than pretending she was going to cook.

Lena took one bite standing up. Peanut, vinegar, too much garlic. It made her realize how hungry she had let herself get.

June leaned over the desk, reading the waveform like she could make sense of it by spite alone. "What's it today?"

"Municipal broadband training."

"Bleak."

"He said nonprofit like he was chewing on a wet sock."

June barked out a laugh. She had a good laugh when she forgot to aim it. "You couldn't pay me enough to hear men explain the public good."

"You literally work in property tech."

"Exactly. I hear worse men for more hours."

She said it lightly, but there was the little edge under it, the work edge, the one that had gotten sharper over the past year whenever KeyNest came up. Lena knew the outline: after-hours tickets, fake emergencies, clients who thought every locked-out tenant was a crisis God had assigned to June personally. She also knew June did not want pity unless it came disguised as mockery, so she only tipped her chin toward the soda.

"Diet," Lena said.

"You're welcome."

They ate with the fan chopping the room into warm gusts. Lena stood to keep herself from sinking into the chair and losing another hour. June wandered the apartment the way she always did, not nosy exactly, just impossible to keep still. There was not much to wander. The bed took up one wall, the table another, and the stacked storage bins against the closet had turned the place into a careful version of crowded. June checked the freezer, made a face at the tray of old ice, picked up the tax paper from the table, and looked over at Lena.

"You still owe the feds forty-two hundred."

"Federal and state," Lena said. "Pennsylvania likes to feel included."

"Right. And the card?"

"Still there."

June tapped the paper against her thigh. "You still have the Wildwood money."

Lena swallowed before answering. Even the phrase made her think of fiberglass walls heating up under sun, her mother's old floral towels turning stiff with salt, the sour-sweet smell of sunscreen that had lived in that trailer long after Rosa stopped going to the beach. The sale money sat in her head like the last object in a room after somebody died, except the object was a bank balance and people treated you like you were lucky.

"I've got eighteen-six that already belongs to other things," she said. "Taxes, rent if work gets weird, maybe the luxury item of not panicking every time a client takes three extra days to pay."

June went still for one beat, thumb pinning the tax sheet against her thigh as if she were already sorting columns in her head.

"Okay," she said. "So. We don't let you light any of that on fire."

"We don't let me do what, exactly?"

June was already reaching for the phone on the desk. "Give me this."

Lena caught it first. "No."

"Yes."

"I'm not doing profile triage with you right now."

"You absolutely are. Look at this place. You're one tax notice away from marrying your own fan."

"At least it shows up."

June's mouth twitched. "That's exactly why I need to intervene."

Lena should have said no again. She knew it. Hinge had been living on her phone for months in the same dead zone as meditation apps and dentist reminders, an idea of action that let her avoid actual action. Sometimes she scrolled it while waiting for microwave rice and felt nothing except a low, stale meanness. There were men holding fish. There were men holding children who were not theirs. There were men who used the phrase fluent in sarcasm and looked like they sweated cologne. It all made her feel both superior and already rejected, which was efficient if nothing else.

June held out her hand. "Len."

"You get ten minutes."

"I need twelve."

"Ten."

June took the phone and sat on the edge of the bed as if she had been invited to do this by a panel of experts. She squinted at Lena's existing profile and let out a long disgusted breath.

"Oh, this is grim."

"Fuck you."

"No, listen. I'm helping. First problem, these pictures all look like you're apologizing for being visible."

"That's my face."

"That's your divorced face. Different thing."

Lena leaned against the desk with her fork in one hand and felt the old irritation rise, familiar enough to be almost companionable. June had known her since twelve, since plaid skirts and cafeteria pizza and those years when every humiliation arrived in public and then kept arriving. There were things June could say to her that would have sounded unforgivable from anybody else.

"Delete the black dress one," June said. "You look like you volunteered for a fundraiser you hate."

"It was a fundraiser I hated."

"Exactly."

She swiped through Lena's camera roll with unnerving speed, every flick carrying the clean finality of somebody sorting keep from trash. She threw out the church-step picture where Lena's ex-husband's shoulder still remained at the edge like an unfinished amputation. She tossed the blurry beach shot from Wildwood because grief sat in it too plainly. She dismissed a selfie from the winter because Lena looked thin-lipped and careful. Then she stopped at a photo Farah had taken in the bottle-shop stockroom, Lena laughing with a case of cava balanced against her hip, her mother's signet ring catching the fluorescent light.

"This one."

Lena looked over. "I'm shiny."

"It's August. Everybody's shiny. You look strong."

That hit harder than pretty would have. June seemed to know that too.

"Also this one," June said, selecting a picture from Gabe's backyard where Lena had one knee tucked under her on a folding chair, head thrown back at something off-camera, hair half fallen from its knot. "And one face-forward picture where a man can verify you're not an AI-generated aunt."

"Beautiful. Exactly the dream."

"Shut up."

June changed the prompts next. Lena had written hers like disclaimers. June deleted almost all of it and started over with two thumbs moving so fast the screen blurred.

"What are you putting?"

"Better things."

"That's not an answer."

June read aloud as she typed. "'Freelance captioner. If you eat chips into a microphone, I will subtitle you as a hate crime.'"

Lena laughed despite herself. "That's aggressive."

"That's memorable."

"I'm pretty sure Hinge has community standards."

"Good. You'll look rebellious."

She flicked to the next prompt. "'A fact about me that surprises people: I can carry a case of wine and hold a grudge at the same time.'"

"Jesus."

"What?"

"You make me sound competent."

"You're competent. That's half your problem."

June looked up then, quick and intent. The teasing had thinned out. "The rest of the problem is you keep offering men your caution like it's a prize. Nobody wants to date your caution."

Lena went still around the fork. Heat pressed in through the window screen. Down below, somebody slammed a car door and two dogs answered from different blocks.

"I wasn't aware we were doing a seminar," she said.

"We're not." June's tone stayed even. "I'm saying if you want somebody to actually notice you, you have to let them notice the part that's alive. Not the part that already filed the paperwork."

For a second Lena hated how close that came to true. She hated it more because it came from June, who still moved through the world as if every room had been built a little wrong and she could fix it by shoving harder on the correct hinge.

June finished the last prompt and handed the phone over. "Read."

Lena read.

The profile did sound like her, only with the volume pushed up. It belonged to a version of herself still alive under the hot apartment, the peanut sauce on her wrist, the estimated tax sheet under a cracked saucer, the account balances and freelance revisions and the years since the divorce had hardened into a story nobody asked follow-up questions about anymore. It was a sharper self, one less interested in preemptive defeat.

"It's obnoxious," she said.

"It's a dating app. That's the native language."

"The hate-crime line might get me arrested."

"Then you'll finally meet people."

Lena sat back down at the desk. The chair seat was hot through her shorts. "I hate that you're good at this."

June shrugged, pleased with herself. "Systems are systems."

She said it like a joke and like a rule she trusted.

"You're doing this for Mateo too," Lena asked, "or only for the elderly."

"Mateo's sixteen. He doesn't need help meeting terrible people."

"Fair."

June snatched up her soda, took a swallow, and winced. "Christ. Warm already."

"Welcome to my climate."

She reached over Lena's shoulder before Lena realized what she was doing and hit the final button.

The profile went live.

"June."

"What, you were going to pray on it?" She stood, gathering containers with the efficiency of somebody who never believed in leaving one mess when she could start managing the next. "Leave it up for a week. If a man says he likes hiking, ask him where. If he says everywhere, he's lying. If he sends a fish picture, block him on behalf of women. If he opens with hey beautiful, the Lord has spared you."

"That's ten different instructions."

"I contain multitudes."

At the door she paused, one hand on the frame, unreadable in that tired way she got when work and nicotine and unspoken things had thinned her down to essentials.

"Len," she said. "Try not to act like this is a dare. Let it be nice if it's nice."

Then she was gone, her steps quick on the stairs, the building swallowing her into its usual evening noises.

Lena looked at the phone in her hand. Her own face looked back from the app, brighter than she felt, the caption joke sitting there with stupid confidence, visible and available. The word available made her skin itch. She set the phone facedown on the desk and put the headphones back on too fast, as if work could reseal the room around her.

It almost did.

For fourteen minutes she managed to stay inside the file. The councilman became a woman from procurement. The woman from procurement became a slide deck voice-over with a hard g in strategic. Lena fixed a dropped article, moved a comma, trimmed dead air. Then the phone buzzed once against the wood and kept buzzing, two short pulses in a row, and the sound went straight into the back of her teeth.

She took the headphones off again.

* * *

There were three new likes, one rose from a man named Darren whose entire profile seemed organized around smoked meats, and one match.

The match was Eli Ward, twenty-nine.

His first picture was on a loading dock, black cable looped over one shoulder, one forearm braced against a road case. Dark hair that needed cutting, two days of stubble, no visible gym mirror in sight. The second was less flattering in a way Lena trusted more: him in a cramped booth beside a lighting console, looking down at something out of frame with the kind of concentration that rearranged a face. The third was at what looked like somebody else's wedding, thrift-store suit, crooked tie, paper cup in hand, smiling like whoever had taken the picture had interrupted him midway through a bad opinion.

One prompt said he worked in live sound and spent too much of his life in rooms where everybody blamed the speakers for human choices. Another said he kept motel sewing kits in his backpack because buttons never came off on days when stores were open. That detail stopped her longer than the pictures did. It felt stolen from a real life in the way most profiles tried and failed to imitate.

The message under his name read: "Be honest. When somebody chews directly into the mic, do you caption the chewing or let God handle it."

Lena sat down slowly.

Outside, a scooter went by with music clipping through cheap speakers. She could hear the tailor shop grate rattle down all the way now. The room had darkened without permission. On her monitor the paused transcript waited with the blinking cursor of a patient enemy.

She typed back before she could decide whether she should.

"God is busy. I bill by the minute."

The typing dots appeared almost at once. They came too fast, she thought, and then she only thought that because she was already interested.

"That feels ethical," he wrote. "I do sound for a living and I still think panel moderators should have to pass a licensing exam."

She smiled despite herself.

"What kind of sound?"

"Mostly theater, some touring, some random civic nightmare gigs when rent gets personal. You really caption stuff like that all day?"

"I caption whatever lands in the portal. Today I spent fifteen minutes deciding whether a city employee said 'broadband corridor' or 'broadband warrior.'"

"Which was it?"

"Corridor. Sadly."

"Warrior would've justified the taxes."

That was the first thing. Anybody could make a tax joke. What caught her was the inch he'd moved past the caption prompt and into the life under it without sounding like he was collecting data with a clipboard. Most men on the app either asked nothing or asked questions so nakedly strategic they might as well have come with checkboxes. Here is where I pretend to care about your work. Here is where I flatter your intelligence. Here is where I suggest drinks.

Eli wrote like he had actually heard the room she was in.

She let herself look back at his pictures. In one, a strip of green tape was looped around two fingers like a ring. In another, there was a coffee stain on the cuff of his shirt. He was handsome, yes, but not in the plasticky way that made her back away before she was even done being impressed. He was handsome in a way that still had laundry and cords and bad fluorescent light in it.

"You don't look old enough to be this disgusted with your profession," she typed.

"I started young and have range."

Then, after a beat:

"Also the wine-case picture is unfair."

She stared at the screen.

"Unfair how?"

"You look like you could carry me and my road case up two flights without losing the argument."

Lena laughed out loud, alone in her apartment, and immediately looked around as if somebody might have caught her. The line was flirty. Of course it was flirty. But it had landed in a place generic handsome did not know existed. It had gone straight to competence, to the body she used for work and lifting and getting through a life nobody was carrying for her. June had chosen that picture because June knew what men saw. Or knew what Lena wanted seen.

The thought should have made the whole thing feel staged. Instead it made her thumb hover harder over the keyboard.

"This is a very elaborate way to ask me to help you move."

"Absolutely not. I would never waste forearm privileges on furniture."

She sent back a laughing face and hated herself a little for it. Then she hated the hate. It was exhausting, the way every small pleasure in her life seemed to arrive already carrying its own rebuttal.

He asked what kind of headphones she used.

"Sony MDR-7506," she wrote. "Because I like looking like a divorced air-traffic controller."

"That's a serious answer."

"You asked a serious question."

"Fair. What do you put on after work when you've spent ten hours listening to other people breathe into microphones?"

That one took her a second.

She looked toward the fan. She looked at the half-finished file on the monitor. She realized she had not added a caption in eleven minutes.

"Depends how bad the day was. Sometimes silence. Sometimes old salsa. Sometimes I leave the fan on and let it impersonate a helicopter until I hate myself."

"Old salsa like cooking salsa or old salsa like lights off, one song on repeat, let the room sweat itself out?"

She stared at that longer than she meant to.

"Second one if I'm being honest."

"Which song buys you back from the dead?"

The question hit lower than flirtation. Lena set her fork down on the desk.

"Rubén Blades if I need the apartment to remember it has a pulse. Héctor Lavoe if I want to feel sorry for myself with standards."

"That's a real answer."

"You asked."

"I did. I'm picturing you giving the fan attitude while Lavoe tries to save the room."

She snorted. Then, before she could decide whether this counted as encouraging him, she opened Spotify and put on "Periódico de Ayer" low enough that the buses and the fan still came through.

"Fan doesn't deserve Héctor."

"Nothing does. But I'm relieved you didn't say Imagine Dragons."

She laughed, an actual laugh this time, and the room changed around it. She did go back to work, technically. She hit play, corrected a sentence, set an inaudible marker where the speaker coughed. But every time the phone lit she reached for it before she had even formed the thought to reach. The app began dividing the room into before-message and after-message. The file kept losing to a stranger.

At seven-thirty June texted her.

"Any disasters yet."

Lena looked at the message, then back at the open Hinge thread. She typed, "Nope," and did not mention Eli. She was not sure why. Maybe because naming him to June would make him feel like a skit. Maybe because she wanted one thing that had not already passed through June's capable hands.

Eli asked what her neighborhood sounded like through the open window.

"Buses, scooters, somebody's aunt yelling up a stoop, a kid bouncing a basketball too long after dinner, the tailor shop gate, the old couple next door whose television believes in maximum volume."

"That's a whole percussion section."

"It's Moore Street. We don't do subtle."

"No, I can hear it. Gate scream on the offbeat, basketball in the middle, aunt on lead vocals. Your fan's still trying to unionize over all of it."

Annoyed at how instantly she could hear the same mix once he named it, Lena got up and shoved the window a little higher. The basketball came back first, then the television through the wall, then a woman laughing hard enough to cough.

"You forgot the TV through plaster."

"Never. Every block has one apartment auditioning for hell."

She stood there longer than she needed to, phone warm in her hand, listening to her own street as if he'd miked it for her.

By eight-fifteen the file was still open and the sky outside the window had gone the color of dirty dishwater. Lena ate the last dumpling cold and did not remember doing it. Her apartment had shrunk all summer into a place where work happened and bills waited. Now, with the phone in her hand, it kept making new little doors.

He sent: "I'm about to get yelled at by a stage manager. Do you hate voice notes from strangers?"

Lena sat very still.

Voice notes from men often arrived already convinced of themselves. They came in too loud, too casual, too eager to make her host them.

She typed, deleted, typed again.

"I hate bad ones."

"Reasonable standard."

"You think you're offering a good one?"

"I can try. No legal guarantees."

She looked at the message long enough for the screen to dim.

If she said yes, it would be a small thing. Smaller than a date, smaller than a number, smaller than those first stupid overthought drinks where two people asked each other what neighborhoods they'd almost been priced out of. It would also change the scale of the room. A voice was different. A voice put a body on a sentence whether the body deserved to be there or not.

The cursor blinked inside the text field.

"Okay," she sent.

Three dots appeared. Then nothing for almost a full minute, long enough for her to feel mildly stupid, long enough to reopen the work file and stare at the word corridor as if it had become profound.

Then the Hinge notification came through: 1 voice note.

* * *

She did not play it right away.

That was pride, mostly. It was also caution dressed up as routine. She saved the open file, closed the laptop on the unfinished transcript, took her soda to the sink, and ran water until it came up only lukewarm instead of hot. She washed peanut sauce off her fingers. She tied her hair back again because it had fallen loose. She moved around the room with the phone faceup on the table, each step arranged to prove to nobody that she was not waiting.

Outside, the block had entered its night shift. The corner store bell kept dinging. Somebody somewhere was frying garlic. A couple argued in short furious bursts on the sidewalk and then stopped so abruptly it was worse than if they had kept going. The fan clicked and turned. Clicked and turned.

Lena took the phone to bed because the chair had become hostile. She sat with one leg folded under her, sheet sticking to the backs of her thighs, and tapped play.

His voice came in close, closer than anything about the app had any right to be.

"Hey, Lena. This is me proving I'm a person and not a customer-support team. I'm on a loading dock in Wilmington, so if you hear metal crashing, that's just three guys losing an argument with a ramp."

There was metal in the background, a hollow bang, somebody swearing, his laugh under it, low and rough and half-buried in the sentence like he had turned his head before it could fully happen.

"Your fan sounds exactly like an old follow spot trying not to die," he went on. "I mean that respectfully. Also, anybody who eats chips into a microphone should have to pay you directly. You don't have to answer in voice if that feels cursed. I just wanted to say hi like a person."

It ended there. There was no extra chuckle, no push.

Lena looked at the screen for a second, then hit play again.

The second listen was worse.

Or maybe it was better. It was worse for her, better for him.

The speaker sat against the groove her headphones had pressed into the side of her head all day, and his voice fit there too easily. The fan kept clicking. A siren passed somewhere farther off. The whole apartment stayed exactly what it had been ten seconds earlier, hot, cramped, smelling faintly of dust and cold noodles and the tailor's starch downstairs. But the scale of it changed. His voice made the room feel occupied in a new way, acoustically and intimately, as if a second set of attention had leaned in under the ordinary noise and stayed.

She played it a third time because at that point she was already committed to losing the argument with herself.

There were other men she had spoken to since the divorce. A contractor from Fishtown who wanted to discuss crypto at brunch. A divorced dad from Jersey who cried into a second beer before she had finished the fries. Two coffee dates so polite they felt like paperwork. None of them had made her body recognize anything before her mind could distrust it. None of them had sounded amused and patient and a little tired in a way that made tiredness feel like a shared language instead of a private failure.

Nothing he said was extraordinary. That was part of it. He had said hello, joked about the fan, let the note stop. There was no soulmate garbage in it, no You seem different, no gross confidence. Just a voice pitched low and warm enough that when he laughed again on the replay, Lena felt the sound almost physically, a small brush at the tender place under her ear where the headphones had rubbed all day.

Hinge was not built for this kind of waiting. The app sat there bright and marketlike, still full of little icons and options and the implication that everyone remained replaceable until proven otherwise.

She typed: "Okay, you do in fact sound like a person."

He answered fast enough to feel eager, not fast enough to feel on top of her.

"Huge relief on my end."

"Wilmington is random."

"Wilmington is where regional theater sends you to learn humility."

"I'm from South Philly. Humility is available locally."

"I believe that instantly."

She smiled again. The room kept smiling with her, which was embarrassing. She set the phone on the pillow, then picked it back up before another message could come in and pretend she had not been about to read it anyway.

"This app is going to eat the note if I keep replaying it," he wrote a minute later. "If you want, I'm better on Signal."

There it was.

It was the exact little threshold she had known was coming.

Lena stared at the message until the fan completed three more clicking rotations. Off-app sat in the middle, more intimate than flirtation and still short of trust. It meant a number in a contact list, a thread that could appear in daylight beside Gabe and Farah and PECO fraud alerts. It meant waking up and maybe seeing him there before coffee. It meant the possibility of continuity, which was what made it dangerous.

From the apartment next door came the old couple's television, game-show applause spilling through the wall in a tinny burst. Her tax estimate still sat on the table with the cracked saucer. The unfinished file still waited on the laptop. All the practical parts of her life remained exactly where she had left them, lined up with their mouths open.

She thought of June saying, Let it be nice if it's nice.

She thought of the voice note again, that brief low laugh under the metal crash, the respectful joke about the fan. She thought of how quickly she had wanted to hear it a second time.

Then she typed, before caution could redraft the sentence into something safer.

"Yeah. Signal's easier."

She added her number.

Once sent, it sat there in the thread looking more intimate than a whole naked photograph would have, a strip of digits, a gate she had unlatched herself.

For one terrible second nothing happened.

Then Signal lit up at the top of her screen with a new message request, his name and the same half-smiling picture from the wedding.

"Hey," he wrote there. "Less cursed app. Still me."

Her stomach did a small unhelpful turn, recognition arriving ahead of fear. Anticipation had texture. It had weight. It took up space in the room with the fan and the hot window and the bill on the table. It made the apartment feel less like a sealed container for work and debt and more like the first minute before somebody arrived.

Lena set the phone on the pillow beside her instead of on the table with the tax paper. When it buzzed again through the thin cotton case, the sound went straight into her thigh.

She let it.


# Chapter 2

By the third morning on Signal, his name was already doing rude things to the room.

At 6:54 her phone rattled hard enough to tap the spoon in yesterday's mug. The box fan in the window clicked on its bad bearing every ninth turn, a cheap stubborn metronome. She had told him about the click the night before because he had asked what kept waking her. His message said, "You up or did that fan finally throw itself into traffic?"

She smiled before she was fully awake, which annoyed her. Thirty-five, divorced, behind on captions, six weeks out from a tax bill with teeth, and there she was smiling at a screen before she had even peed. She let the phone sit for what she hoped counted as normal-person timing. Then she wrote back, "Still here. Fan's all talk."

His reply came while she was standing at the sink with a dry mouth and one hand under cold water for no reason except the apartment had already started heating up.

"I don't trust it. If you disappear I'm blaming appliance negligence."

Then a photo: coffee in a paper cup balanced on a black road case, the lip of a loading dock behind it still blue with morning. His thumb cut into one corner of the shot. A strip of fluorescent tape crossed the case like a small emergency.

"Today's office."

Not a shirtless mirror picture. Not a gym selfie with a caption about earning it. A paper cup, a road case, dawn.

She took the phone into the bathroom with her, which was pathetic, but she did it anyway.

By seven-thirty she was at her desk in a sports bra and old shorts, hair knotted up, left heel hooked on the chair rung, cleaning captions on a condo-board Zoom from Cherry Hill. The auto transcript had turned reserve study into deserve slutty three times and the treasurer had a wet whistle on every s. Below her screen a folded IRS notice held down a cable that would not stay in place. The tailor shop downstairs hauled up its metal gate with the sound of a train dragging itself out of sleep. Somewhere on the block a scooter coughed awake, failed, then tried again.

Her phone lit.

"Did deserve slutty pass, or did they table it?"

She laughed out loud, alone in the room. She had mentioned that error once, quick, while brushing her teeth. Most people heard the general category of what she said and answered the outline of it. He kept the nuisance, the throwaway piece, the exact stupid phrasing.

"Still under debate," she typed. "The condo future hangs in the balance."

"Thoughts and prayers."

She put the phone face down, made herself work twelve minutes straight, and then turned it back over just to see if there was more. There was.

"Eat something."

"Bossy."

"You told me coffee on an empty stomach turns you into a fugitive."

She had said that, too, as a joke the night before while she was explaining why she had once left an ex in a Target because he wanted to browse decorative baskets at eleven in the morning. She stood up, went to the kitchenette, and ate half a peach over the sink while the juice ran down her wrist and the fan pushed hot dust against the back of her knees.

Fast answers were cheap in the first week. Plenty of men sent them because they were working a slot machine, pushing the same bright buttons in six windows and waiting to see where the bells went off. Eli's attention came in at an angle. He didn't come back with "how's your day going" every two hours like a customer service script. He returned to the crooked little things she had tossed off without ceremony and handed them back with his thumbprint on them.

At nine he sent a voice note from somewhere loud enough that his first word blurred.

"Hang on."

She heard him move. A door banged. The noise changed shape, generator to alley, broad metallic roar thinning down into a truck beep and somebody swearing about a lift gate.

"There. Better. I was gonna say, if that condo board approves deserve slutty as a budget line item, I need you to steal the minutes."

His voice had the same near-the-ear patience as the first note, but now there was sleep roughing the bottom of it. He sounded like somebody who had worked late and still had to show up again, which she trusted more than if he had sounded glossy. He laughed once at his own joke, quietly, like he was trying not to carry into the phone as performance.

She listened to it twice. The second time with the left earbud pinched just right between finger and thumb because the cable had started cutting out again at the split.

"You're bad for productivity," she wrote.

"I contain multitudes."

"No, you contain OSHA violations and a paper cup."

"Rude. Also accurate."

The condo board argued about roofing bids until ten-thirteen. She cleaned their stumbles, marked their crosstalk, turned their human mess back into respectable text. Every time the phone buzzed she told herself she would finish the current speaker first. Most of the time she did not. He sent a picture of a coil of XLR cable like a sleeping snake and another of a road case with a caster blown clean off. He sent one line about a drummer who had gotten barbecue chips on a monitor console and sounded actually annoyed, not trying to seem charming. Ten minutes later, after a gap full of work noise, another message came in: "If one more guy calls every cable in this building 'the aux,' I'm walking into the river." Only then did he ask if the tailor downstairs still played boleros on Saturdays when pressing wedding shirts. She had mentioned that on Hinge, back when it still felt like a game, because she could hear the music through the floorboards and it made her feel as if other people's ceremonies were stored directly under her bed.

"He only does that when he's in a good mood," she wrote.

"How do you know?"

"Because he sings along and he can't sing."

"Devastating. I respect him more now."

At eleven-thirty her client sent an email marked gentle reminder and she felt the bottom drop out of the flirtation for a second. Gentle reminder always meant where the fuck is my file. She answered with a lie about final formatting, then worked hard enough to make the lie true. The room got hotter. Her thighs stuck to the chair. Sweat gathered under her bra band and along the inside of her elbows. She turned the fan higher and it answered with a louder click.

The phone buzzed again.

"Go stand in the freezer aisle for five minutes before you pass out."

She stared at the screen.

Two nights ago, laughing at herself for how broke and overheated August could make a grown woman, she had told him that when the apartment got unbearable she walked to the Acme and loitered by the frozen peas pretending to compare store brands until her skin stopped feeling rented. She had not said it as a meaningful thing. It had embarrassed her a little as soon as it was out. Now he had reached back for it, and the line snagged for a beat on the command buried inside it. Her ex had been good at dressing instructions up as concern and then acting wounded if she heard the leash.

"I'm not gonna faint in produce," she typed.

"Fair. That came out bossier than I meant."

"Little bit."

"Go get cold anyway."

She should have left the phone where it was. Instead she carried it with her to the closet, put on a shirt decent enough for daylight, and went outside.

The block was all white glare and hot brick. A SEPTA rumble passed somewhere deeper in the city, too low to separate from the traffic. At the corner a man in slides was hosing blood and soap from a butcher's back step into the gutter. By the time she got to the Acme her scalp was damp under her hair knot. She stood with the peas anyway, one hand on the freezer handle, and texted him a photo of frost crusting the inside glass.

"Medicinal," she wrote.

His answer came fast enough that she pictured the phone already in his hand.

"You look hotter than the peas."

She stared at that one long enough for an elderly woman reaching for waffles to give her a look. What got her was the way he had attached the line to the actual small indignity of her standing red-faced in a grocery freezer, not to some fantasy version of her. Men said prettier things than that all the time, usually before disappointing her in some bigger and more boring way. He had let the stupid scene stay stupid and reached for her inside it anyway.

She bought yogurt, two limes, a discounted rotisserie chicken, and the cheapest seltzer. On the walk home he sent another voice note.

"Okay, one actual useful thing. Our monitor guy killed two cables today by yanking them at the jack. Don't buy new earbuds yet. If you show me yours later, I can probably tell whether it's the same stupid break."

She stopped at the crosswalk with a bag cutting into her fingers.

This one was not memory. It had come straight out of whatever mess he was standing in, a piece of his own day handed over still warm.

"You're offering remote triage now?" she typed.

"Against my better judgment."

"I wasn't abusing them."

"That's what every cable killer says."

Back upstairs she delivered the condo-board file nine minutes past the lie she'd told. The client replied with thanks and a reminder about tomorrow's rush job. She should have felt only irritation. Instead there was a strange second track running under the day now, bright and private. She ate chicken standing at the sink with one foot on the cool strip of tile by the radiator and listened for his next message like listening for weather.

At four-seventeen he called without warning.

Her whole body moved before her head did. She wiped her hand on a dish towel, saw grease on the screen, wiped that too, then answered voice-only because the camera icon felt too naked at first.

"Hey," she said, trying to sound as if she always picked up on the first ring.

"Hey. You busy?"

Behind him there was a hiss, then the unmistakable shriek of somebody checking a mic through a PA.

"Supposed to be," she said.

"I can call later."

"No. It's fine."

"You sure?"

"Yeah."

He was quiet for a beat, not suspicious, just listening.

"You hiding from me on purpose with the voice thing, or are you in a hostage situation?"

She sat down on the edge of the bed because her knees had gone treacherous. "I look rough."

"Lena. I'm standing next to a dumpster in a loading dock. I promise we're not doing glamour."

She laughed in spite of herself.

"Okay," he said, softer. "Come on."

She hit the camera button before she could decide not to.

For a second they both had the wrong angle. She saw the lower half of his face, stubble darker than she'd pictured, throat, black shirt damp at the collar. He saw probably one eyebrow and her ceiling. Then the images corrected.

He was handsome with the day still on him. The loading dock light hit him greenish from above and made the hollows under his eyes visible. Sweat had flattened a piece of dark hair across his forehead. Behind him a cinderblock wall held a dented EXIT sign, and beyond his shoulder she could see the open mouth of the dock, a strip of late afternoon white and the black ribbed side of a truck. Somebody in the distance rolled a case over a metal threshold and the clatter came clean through the speaker. He looked down once, off-camera, and said, "Give me one sec," to somebody else with the absent courtesy of a man in the middle of work. Then he looked back at her.

"Okay," he said. "Hi."

She felt the stupid heat rise up her neck. "You have a face."

He smiled. It changed him more than the profile photos had, pulling him off center into something more crooked, more alive.

"Bad news?"

"No. Just weird. I had you filed as ear-only."

"Sounds intimate."

"I didn't mean it that way."

"I know. I'm still taking it."

He tipped the phone farther back and she got the full picture at once: black T-shirt, ink on the inside of one forearm, a strip of white spike tape wound twice around his ring finger and hanging loose because he'd been fidgeting it to death. It was such an ordinary, specific thing to have in his hand that something inside her settled. Men who did not exist in rooms did not absentmindedly destroy stage tape while waiting for a woman to turn on her camera.

"You weren't lying about the heat," he said. "You look flushed."

"That's hot-girl branding. Very strategic."

"Sure. And the sweat?"

"Also branding."

He laughed, but he did not do the gross thing where a man acted as if he had caught her performing womanhood badly. He just leaned his shoulder against the cinderblock and looked at her like the fact of her in a hot apartment was enough.

"Show me the earbud," he said.

"What?"

"The cable. The left one that keeps cutting out. Hold it up."

She almost said no on reflex, because the request was so practical it felt more intimate than if he had asked to see her collarbone. Instead she reached for the earbuds where they'd been lying on the bedspread in a snarl and held the split near the camera.

"There," he said immediately. "See that bend right above the jack? You're pulling the stress there every time you wrap it. Tape it two inches up, not right on the split. And stop winding it around your hand like you're taking revenge on it."

She stared at him. "Are you spying on me?"

"No. I just know exactly what people do to cables."

"People?"

"You, mostly."

That mostly landed low in her, almost embarrassingly low.

"I don't even have tape," she said.

He lifted his free hand and showed her the loose white strip on his finger. "You live above a tailor. Steal some. Or use a Band-Aid until tomorrow."

"That sounds sanitary."

"You're not eating it."

Behind him a voice shouted, "Eli, line check in two."

He turned half away. "Yep."

Then, back to her: "I gotta go in a second."

The disappointment crossed her too nakedly because he smiled again, smaller this time.

"Hey," he said. "I'm gonna call you later, okay? After load-out if you're still up."

"That depends."

"On?"

"Whether my glamour schedule allows it."

"Please pencil me in between the peas and deserve slutty."

She was still smiling when the call ended.

For a few seconds she sat with the dead screen in her hand and the room felt altered, not romantic exactly. Reweighted. There had been a truck, a shouted line check, sweat at the collar, tape on a finger, somebody else's schedule cutting through his attention. He had occupied space. He had looked down and answered another human being and then returned to her mid-breath. She had not realized how much of her doubt had been waiting for that.

Her phone buzzed again.

"And drink water. You always forget when you're working."

No flourish. No wink. Just that.

She went downstairs, knocked on the tailor's half-open door, and borrowed a strip of electrical tape off a shelf crowded with thread cones and garment bags. The tailor, who was sixty if he was a day and still ironed in an undershirt through August, handed it over without asking questions. Back upstairs she bound the earbud cable where Eli had said. The left side came back clean, no static, no cutting. She stood in the middle of the room with one bud in, listening to the condo-board treasurer whistle his way through a final agenda correction, and felt something close to gratitude that had nothing to do with audio.

At six she opened Hinge because habit was stronger than intention. Three new messages waited there. One man whose profile picture was just a beard in a bar mirror had written, "How's your week beautiful." Another wanted to know if she liked spontaneous road trips, which in app language usually meant he owned a Subaru and a secret grievance. A third had simply sent, "Trouble."

She looked at them long enough to become irritated by their existence, then closed the app without answering.

At ten-forty-eight, after a shower and two false starts on tomorrow's rush transcript, Eli sent a single line.

"You awake?"

She looked at the time, looked at the open file, looked back at his name, and wrote, "Yeah."

She moved the laptop aside to clear space on the bed before the phone even rang.

* * *

By the next night she had moved her entire day around three possible windows in which he might be free, which was humiliating and also true.

She did the grocery run before noon because he had a load-in at one. She sent a file early rather than better because he said he might have twenty minutes before soundcheck. She showered at seven instead of waiting for the air to cool because she did not want to be standing half wet in a towel if he called. She told herself this was temporary, first-week nonsense, the kind of thing adults noticed and corrected. Then her phone lit and she answered before the correction could happen.

The long call came late enough that the neighborhood had changed registers. The tailor was dark downstairs. The block voices had thinned to two men arguing softly at the corner and the occasional scooter ripping too fast down a side street. Somewhere nearby a television laugh track kept arriving in short trapped bursts through somebody's wall. Lena had one lamp on, low, and the sheet only over her shins because the apartment still held the day.

He called voice-only from what sounded like a bathroom.

"Please tell me that's a prison break," she said by way of hello.

He laughed, tired. "Hotel ventilation. If I stay in the room you won't hear me over it."

She could hear the room around him in pieces anyway: a distant ice machine dropping a tray, a muffled door somewhere down the hall, the hard slick echo of cheap tile.

"Glamour schedule," she said.

"You were warned."

"How bad is it?"

"The pillows smell like bleach and some guy's scalp. The towels could exfoliate a boat. I think the carpet's damp on purpose."

That made her laugh hard enough that the sheet slipped off one knee.

"You're impossible," she said.

"I'm right."

"You're probably right."

"Thank you. Record this, because it won't happen again."

At night he sounded looser, less polished in the right ways. Words bumped into each other. He left little silences where she could hear him breathe and shift the phone from one hand to the other. Once he said, "Hang on," and she heard him spit toothpaste into a sink. The uglier parts of being in a body did more for his reality than any smooth line could have.

They talked about bad venue coffee, about whether captioning other people's arguments ever left residue in her own head, about the woman in the apartment behind his who practiced trumpet badly enough that he could identify her scales through the wall. He spent five annoyed minutes describing a stage manager who had labeled six power strips with the same strip of tape and then blamed everybody else when half the backline died. He said the hotel vending machine had only neon cookies and a protein bar the color of drywall, and she could hear him tear into one anyway, disgust and wrapper crackle together. Only once did he reach back for her: a catering tray had tried to serve him pale melon and he had thought of her mother's rule about salting cantaloupe in August because that was how real sweetness worked.

Some of it was her. Some of it was simply the shape of the day he was actually living, and that made him feel more occupied, not less. It felt like he was building a version of her day inside his own and letting his own day crowd back in around it.

At some point after midnight she lay all the way down on the bed and put the phone on her sternum. The heat of it seeped through the thin shirt she slept in. She had turned off the lamp. The room was fan noise and the occasional long rolling groan of a bus. His voice came straight into the dark.

"You getting tired?" he asked.

"A little."

"You should sleep."

"You should stop talking, then."

"Rude."

"You're the one in a bathroom."

"That's fair."

He was quiet a moment. Then: "Can I ask you something that might annoy you?"

Every stupid caution she'd managed not to feel all day arrived at once and took a seat on the edge of the bed.

"Probably," she said.

"You still on Hinge?"

She stared at the ceiling, though there was nothing to see. The question should not have felt loaded. They had matched there less than a week ago. Normal people did not appoint one another to anything this fast. Normal people especially did not do it when one of them existed mostly as a warm rectangle in the hand.

"Technically," she said.

"Technically is a very evil answer."

"It's an honest one."

"Fair."

He did not rush in to claim ground. He did not go hurt or sulky. That almost made it worse.

"I paused mine tonight," he said after a second.

She rolled onto her side. "Why?"

"Because it started feeling gross."

"Gross how?"

"Like I was checking in on you and then flipping over to a stranger asking if I wanted to rank condiments. It made me feel like an asshole."

She laughed despite herself. "That's most of the app."

"I know."

"You could just not answer people."

"I could. I did. Then the whole thing started feeling noisy, and I don't want noise right now."

He paused, then added, "And yeah, maybe this is selfish, but I don't like picturing your phone lighting up with trout guys while I'm in a hotel bathroom talking to you."

She went still. There it was: appetite with a little grab in it.

"That's a lot for week one," she said.

"I know."

Across the room the laptop still threw a thin blue square over the desk, the rush transcript she'd promised by eight stalled in the ugliest section. Another late rush meant a revision penalty, and the $4,200 tax notice under the cable was not going to shrink because a man with a good voice wanted clarity. At the same time another, older alarm tripped: the marriage came back to her in its early form, practical preferences and gentle instructions, the kind you could mistake for devotion until the whole room belonged to them.

"I'm not giving you a speech," he said. "I'm telling you where my head's at."

She let the silence sit.

"This is exactly how people get stupid," she said.

"Probably."

"It's how I got divorced."

He breathed once on the line, steady, neither backing off nor pressing.

"I'm not him," he said.

"No shit."

"Okay."

"It's been five minutes," she said, and immediately hated how thin it sounded.

"I know."

"You don't know me."

"A little."

"A little isn't enough."

"Then don't pause anything for me," he said. "But don't keep me in one ear and a parade of dentists in the other and call it casual. I can't do that part."

She expected reassurance there. Instead he had made himself inconvenient.

"Jesus," she said.

"You asked."

"I'm not asking you for a promise you don't mean," he said. "I'm saying I don't want to act casual if I'm not casual. I'd rather sound stupid than pretend this doesn't have teeth."

The sentence landed differently now, with some grab in it, some want that did not care about being perfectly fair.

"You're making this weird," she said, but there was no edge in it now.

"It was already weird."

"Helpful."

"Come on, Lena. You know what I mean."

She did. The weirdness was not just that they had gotten attached too fast. It was that he had already started taking up room inside decisions that should have belonged to work, sleep, groceries, air. She had sent a file early for him. She had walked to Acme because he told her to get cold. The speed should have read as false. Instead it kept showing up as leverage.

"Do you ask everybody this in week one?" she said.

"No."

"How noble of you."

"I'm serious."

"Why me?"

He did not answer immediately. Somewhere in the hotel hallway a door opened, voices passed, then shut away again.

"I keep thinking about your day when I'm in mine," he said. "You tell me one thing about yourself and then I hear the rest of the day around it. And I don't want to be half paying attention to you while somebody named Chris sends me a fish picture."

She laughed with her hand over her mouth. "Chris?"

"It's always Chris."

"Sometimes it's Matt."

"Matt's bad, too."

"Agreed."

"See? That's intimacy."

She could feel herself giving in the small hinge-like way a body gives when weight is added at the right angle. The sheet had twisted around one ankle. The phone was warm against her palm. Outside, a bus exhaled at the corner and moved on.

"What if this is just first-week brain damage?" she said.

"Then we'll know later."

"That's not comforting."

"It's honest."

He was infuriating for not trying to sell her anything. She had braced for a claim and gotten a clear space instead. The choice sat there between them, uglier and cleaner for being hers.

Her phone buzzed against her hand. Another app notification. Reflex made her glance down.

Hinge: 2 new messages.

The bright little flame icon looked suddenly ridiculous, like a cheap sign outside a bad bar. She could see the queue waiting behind it without even opening it. Beard-in-mirror. Condiment guy. Trouble. Men with carefully cultivated opinions about vinyl and tacos and women who "didn't take themselves too seriously." Men who asked what she was looking for as if they were staffing a role. All of them together had not produced the exact low physical certainty of Eli rinsing toothpaste into a hotel sink while talking to her about caption lag.

"You still there?" he asked.

"Yeah."

She opened the app.

Faces came up in orderly little cards. A civil engineer in Manayunk holding a trout like evidence. A divorced dentist in East Passyunk whose bio used the word adventure twice. A teacher in South Jersey who had sent a paragraph so polite it already felt like homework. She had been leaving them there, half out of caution, half out of vanity, wanting the possibility without having to touch it.

Now they looked prewritten because Eli had made attention feel more exact than possibility.

"You don't have to do anything tonight," he said, misreading her silence in a way she liked. "I'm not timing you with a stopwatch."

"I know."

Her thumb hovered over Pause Profile.

The last of her common sense tried once more. This was still a man in a phone. A man with a body somewhere else, maybe not as continuous and knowable as the phone made him feel. A man she had not watched walk into a room she occupied. A man whose hand she knew only through pixels and an absent strip of stage tape. She should have wanted more proof before she narrowed the field this completely.

Instead she thought about the whole day he had already lived at her shoulder. The peas. The earbud cable. The pale melon on the hotel tray. The way he had said he'd rather sound stupid than pretend this had no teeth. The way her apartment had changed weight when his face appeared in that ugly loading-dock light.

She hit pause.

The app asked if she was sure.

She hit yes.

The bright flame dimmed to a neutral little outline, the whole decision reduced to one setting changed in a hot room.

"Lena?" he said.

"I paused it."

There was a small sound on his end, not triumph exactly. Air leaving him.

"Okay," he said.

"Don't make that noise."

"What noise?"

"That satisfied one."

"I'm not satisfied. I'm trying not to be weirdly happy."

"That's the same thing in a different shirt."

"Fine. I'm weirdly happy."

She smiled into the dark. What felt dangerous was how fiercely she wanted to keep it to herself for now. June would have a joke ready before Lena finished the sentence. Farah would narrow her eyes and ask the practical question. Gabe would turn it into teasing. She did not want the room of other people's reactions around this thing. She wanted it left where it was, close to the body, lit only by the phone.

"You don't have to pause yours just because I did," she said, because she heard herself sounding more exposed than she wanted.

"I know," he said. "I already did."

"Right."

"Also, for the record, I wasn't talking to any Chrises worth saving."

"That's noble."

"Thank you."

She should have gone to sleep then. Instead they stayed on another forty minutes talking about nothing that would look important in a transcript. The exact shape of the dent in his rental-car seat. Whether the frozen chicken pot pies at Acme were edible or just structurally interesting. The trumpet woman behind his apartment and the kid on Lena's block who kept practicing wheelies badly enough that every attempt ended with a metallic slam and an adult swearing. He asked her to put the phone near the window for a second so he could hear the buses and scooters. She did. He said he liked cities best at the hour when everybody who meant trouble was already either committed to it or asleep.

When he yawned she heard it all the way through, not polite, jaw-cracking, body-first. It made her tenderness for him feel indecently domestic.

"Go to bed," she said.

"You first."

"I'm already in bed."

"Then close your eyes."

"That's too intimate."

"You paused the app."

"You're insufferable."

"And yet."

She did close her eyes because it let his voice move closer.

When they finally hung up, the room did not widen back out the way it should have. It stayed shaped around him. The rush transcript was still open on her laptop, untouched for an hour. The time in the corner said 1:43. The ugliest section was still half-marked, and her client wanted the cleaned file by eight.

She got out of bed, crossed to the desk, and read three lines without taking in a word. Her body was still on the call. She opened email instead.

"Running into an export glitch on my end. I'll have the rush file to you by nine," she wrote, and sent it before she could revise the lie into something nobler. It might cost her part of the invoice. It might cost her the next rush if the client got tired of excuses. Relief came first anyway.

The IRS notice was still under the cable on her desk. The fan still clicked every ninth turn. Her life had not improved in any durable respectable sense. It had simply reorganized itself, almost invisibly, around one person's availability.

She picked the phone back up, turned the volume to loud, and opened her alarm app.

He had said he would be awake before call time and probably free for ten minutes around seven.

She set the alarm twenty minutes earlier than work required and left the phone on the pillow beside her head, one more small rearrangement she was already calling temporary.


# Chapter 3

Sunday dinner at Gabe's announced itself half a block out. The foil pan under Lena's arm had warmed through the dish towel and her phone had buzzed six times against the back pocket of her jeans, quick trapped-bee hits she could feel all the way into her hip. The street already had enough noise without Eli adding his own private frequency to it. Somebody was arguing through a first-floor screen door about the Phillies bullpen. A scooter tore past too fast for the block and made a mother on a stoop yell after it. From half a block away Lena could hear one of Gabe's girls doing the long theatrical cry that meant she was not hurt at all, only offended in public.

She stopped under the sliver of shade from a sycamore and checked the phone anyway.

`You there yet?`

Then, thirty seconds later:

`Tell me what the house smells like.`

Then:

`Actually don't. I'll go insane and eat venue almonds for dinner.`

It was not even a real joke and it still got her. The smile came before she could manage it. She had changed her shirt twice for Sunday dinner at her brother's house, which was humiliating enough, and put on mascara like Gabe's twins were going to file a report if she looked tired. June would have called her on it. Gabe would have called her on it louder.

She typed, `Almost there. Sauce. Child sweat. Lemon Pledge probably.`

The three dots came back so fast it made something warm and cheap unspool under her ribs.

`Perfect. I want the full census later.`

She shoved the phone away before she could reread that into meaning and mounted Gabe's stoop with the pan on one hip. The rowhouse door was open to the metal security gate. Heat and tomato and child soap came straight out at her. The house looked the way it always did on Sundays, as if it had been losing a fight with objects for hours and was somehow winning anyway. Tiny sneakers under the radiator cover. A pink plastic doctor kit on the floor by the umbrella stand. A damp beach towel hanging over the banister because one of the girls had insisted on making a fort with it and then abandoned the project halfway through. There was never a clean blank stretch of floor in Gabe's place. Something always got there first.

"If that's another vegetable," Gabe called from the kitchen, "turn around and go home."

Lena stepped inside and kicked the door nearly shut with her heel. "It's a vegetable. I brought it because I love your children and want them to live long enough to resent you."

Gabe leaned out from the kitchen in a gray SEPTA T-shirt gone darker at the chest and collar with sweat. He had a wooden spoon in one hand and a pencil behind one ear, like he had been taking notes on the stove. His face had the permanently tired drag it always got by Sunday afternoon, but he was grinning. "My children eat red sauce. That's a vegetable. Ask a court."

"Ask a cardiologist."

"I don't trust rich people doctors."

"You say that every time and then you take their prescriptions."

"That's because my body's a union shop. Nobody agrees and everybody still clocks in."

He took the pan from her anyway, peered under the foil, and made a show of disappointment. "Zucchini. Jesus Christ."

"You can say no thank you and die younger."

From the dining room archway, Marisol said, "Put it down before you drip on my floor, Gabe."

Marisol sat cross-legged on one of the dining chairs with a cordless trimmer in her hand, shaving a snag off the edge of a bright orange press-on nail. She had done a box color on her hair and wrapped it in a silk scarf printed with chains and lemons, though damp strands had already escaped at the nape of her neck. Her reading glasses were on top of her head for no reason Lena had ever understood. She never read in them. Tati leaned against her knee in a bathing suit top and shorts, solemnly applying a sticker to her own elbow. Mina had red rain boots on bare feet and the wet shine of recent hand-washing all over her cheeks.

"I mopped twice," Marisol said. "Today I refuse to watch tomato footprints happen in real time."

"That's fair," Lena said.

The booted twin looked up at her with the grave authority only six-year-olds and judges possessed. "Tati cheated."

"I didn't cheat," said Tati, still working the sticker onto her elbow. "You cried first."

"That's not about cheating."

"It's about winning."

Lena bent to kiss both heads because not doing it would have started another argument. The girls smelled like Johnson's shampoo and the faint sweet rot of dried juice. They hit her on the way back up with the full soft weight of children who assumed her body was a public utility. It landed somewhere low in her chest, that casual claim. Gabe's whole house did that. It pressed people into one another until privacy seemed like an expensive taste.

"Where's June?" she asked, because the air felt wrong without the extra noise June usually brought.

Gabe rolled his eyes and went back into the kitchen. "Late. As always. She texted Marisol something about Staples and ice and Mateo needing sneakers apparently this minute."

"His foot grew again," Marisol said. "That kid is built like a weed. Also your brother killed the fan in the front room."

"The fan was making a death noise at me," Gabe called.

"Because you hit it."

"I adjusted it."

"With your foot."

The box fan in the front window sat at an angle, unmoving, one side of the plastic grille caved in. The room without it felt close and wet. Sweat gathered under Lena's bra line, in the bend of her elbows, under the cheap metal of her ring. The radiator cover was warm from trapped sunlight. Somewhere upstairs a cartoon shrieked from a television nobody seemed to be watching.

Her phone buzzed again.

This time it was a photo from Eli: his hand holding a paper cup of coffee in front of what looked like a black-painted backstage hallway. No face. Just wrist, cup, a strip of silver rings on a case handle in the background, and the caption: `Ten more minutes and I'm yours.`

She looked at it long enough for Marisol to catch her.

Marisol tipped her head. "There he is."

"Shut up."

"I'm not saying anything."

"Your whole face is saying something."

"My whole face says lots of things. I'm very expressive."

One of the girls climbed into Lena's lap before she had actually committed to sitting down. The other followed because being left out of somebody else's body was intolerable. Lena let herself get pinned there on the dining chair, both small knees digging into her thighs, and tried not to check the phone again while the house worked around her. Gabe stirred sauce and cursed at the oven door. Marisol shouted upstairs for nobody to jump on a bed, even though both twins were visibly downstairs. Somebody in the alley behind the house dropped a glass bottle into recycling with a sound like a snapped bell.

This was the part Lena missed without admitting she missed it. Not motherhood, not exactly, though sometimes that too in ugly little flashes she never looked at straight. She missed being expected somewhere noisy. She missed the way Gabe's place never asked whether she wanted touching before it handed her three different kinds. Her apartment had room to think in, and room to think in could become its own kind of sickness if she let it.

The security gate banged. June came in backward with two bags looped around one wrist and a twenty-pound bag of ice balanced against her hip. Mateo followed with a shoe box under one arm and a case of seltzer hugged to his chest. He had his school hoodie on despite the heat, hood down, beat-up headphones hanging around his neck with one earcup patched in silver tape. Acne had broken out hard across his forehead and he seemed newly angry at his own height, as if the extra inches had arrived without asking. A black composition notebook stuck out of the back pocket of his shorts, pages warped from use.

"Nobody helps around here?" June said. "Love that for you all."

"You're late," Marisol said.

"Correct. And still carrying civilization in both hands."

June dropped the bags on the table, took one look at the dead fan, and clicked her tongue. "Who kicked it?"

"It was already dying," Gabe said.

"That means you kicked it while it was dying."

Mateo put the seltzer down carefully, as if he did not trust glass near this family, and said, "She made me buy a screwdriver set at Staples because she said grown men shouldn't be allowed near electrical items if they're related to us."

"I said some grown men," June said.

"You looked right at Gabe."

"Because visual aids help."

Even Marisol laughed at that. June set the ice by the sink and fished a tiny yellow-handled screwdriver out of the Staples bag. She crouched in front of the fan with her knees apart and the silk of her blouse sticking to her spine, moving with the quick irritated economy Lena had watched since they were thirteen and June had been the only girl in class who could fix the chain on a bike with a house key. Her nicotine-stained fingers worked the bent grille loose. She leaned her head close to the blades, blew away a clot of gray lint, and said, "Teo, freezer. Then wash your hands and rinse that basil. Gabe, get me a butter knife. Not that one. The one you did not already put in your mouth."

"Bossy," Gabe muttered, but he handed her the clean knife.

Mateo paused on his way to the freezer. "Do I also get paid in vibes or is that just family policy?"

"You get dinner," June said.

"Same thing, basically."

He disappeared into the kitchen. A beat later Lena heard the freezer door thump, then Mateo saying to one of the twins, "If you lick the bag of ice again, you're drinking hot juice like a pioneer."

The box fan coughed once, rattled with offended drama, and then spun into life. A strip of cooler air came off it thin as mercy but real. It moved the damp hair off Lena's neck. Marisol put a hand over her heart as if June had performed surgery.

"Marry me instead," Marisol said.

"Tempting," June said without looking up.

Then she stood, wiped her hands on the sides of her jeans, and finally took Lena in properly. Her eyes flicked once over the mascara, the shirt, the phone clenched a little too hard in Lena's hand.

"You look nice," June said.

"Don't start."

"I didn't start. I said you look nice."

"Which in your mouth means I obviously tried."

"You obviously did try. That's not illegal."

The girls had migrated toward the revived fan and were holding their mouths in front of it to make robot voices. Gabe barked at them not to stick crayons in the grille. Marisol got up to rescue the salad from whatever was happening in the kitchen. In the temporary cover of everybody moving, June hooked two fingers in Lena's elbow and steered her toward the narrow strip of space between the fridge and the back door.

"Breathe," June said quietly. "It's dinner, not a parole hearing."

"I'm breathing."

"You're auditing your own breathing. Different thing."

Lena huffed once through her nose. Up close June smelled like cigarettes ghosted under drugstore jasmine body spray and the freezer-cold damp of the ice bag. Familiar enough to knock Lena down into herself a little. "Is it weird if he calls?"

"At a table full of people who already know you have a phone?" June said. "No. It's only weird if you make it weird."

"Helpful."

"Len. Gabe would have mocked any man you brought home even if he came with a deed and a saint's blessing. Let him have somebody to bounce off. He'll live."

It was ridiculous how much that helped. June had always been able to tune a room for her, take the shriek out of it and leave the useful noise. When they were teenagers she could walk Lena into a party and tell her in under ten seconds who was safe, who was lying, who was so drunk they would cry in half an hour. She did it now with one dry sentence and a thumb over the tomato stain Lena had not noticed on the side of her pan towel, rubbing it off against the sink.

"Also," June said, stepping back, "if he sucks on speaker, you'll know faster."

"Jesus."

"That was me being practical."

"You're enjoying this."

"A little," June said. "You're cute when you pretend you aren't losing your mind."

Before Lena could answer, Mateo came back from the sink carrying a fistful of basil and the shoe box under his other arm.

"Do I put these anywhere," he asked, lifting the sneakers, "or are they ceremonial."

"Front closet," June said.

"That closet's a war crime," Marisol called from the kitchen.

"Then kick it in and make room," June called back.

The house answered with laughter, spoon clatter, the fan's steady chop. Lena's phone buzzed again. Eli this time: `Outside now. Call whenever. No rush.`

No rush was one of his better tricks. He never said do you miss me right now, do you want me enough, are you thinking about me instead of whoever is in front of you. He made room and let her hurry into it herself.

She locked the screen and slid the phone facedown onto the counter.

For ten whole seconds she felt almost normal.

***

They ate at a table technically built for six and currently holding eight bodies, three serving bowls, a sweating pitcher of tap water, the dead and then resurrected fan moving one corner of the room, and two coloring books nobody had succeeded in confiscating from the twins. Gabe's sauce had gone deep and dark from the pot, sweet first and then the sausage heat after. Marisol had made a salad with shaved fennel because she got on seasonal kicks and liked converting people against their will. The zucchini Lena brought sat mostly untouched except by June, who put a disapproving amount on Gabe's plate whenever he looked away.

Lena spent the first ten minutes pretending she could eat normally while Eli's last text waited in her pocket like a held hand. One of the girls kept kicking the table leg in an absent rhythm. Mateo, seated sideways at the end because there was no formal place left for him, ate with one headphone on the table beside his plate and the other still around his neck. He had a habit of straightening the fork and knife so they lay parallel after every few bites, then messing them up again without noticing. Marisol pointed out twice that he was hunching and twice he said he was not.

"You want another meatball, Teo?" Gabe asked.

"I want school to get canceled forever," Mateo said.

"Not what I asked."

"Then no."

"He'll eat half a loaf of bread in secret in an hour," June said.

"That's slander," Mateo said.

"That's eyewitness testimony."

Gabe wiped his mouth with the back of his hand, saw Lena glance toward her pocket, and grinned. "All right. Enough of this. Is Phone Romeo calling or not?"

Both girls lit up instantly.

"Phone Romeo," one of them repeated, delighted.

"Phone Romeo!" said the other, because nothing mattered more to twins than synchronized mockery.

"Can you not call him that," Lena said.

"You bring a man to Sunday dinner by Bluetooth, you get what you get," Gabe said. "Call him."

Marisol snorted into her water. "If you don't call him soon she's going to grind a groove in the table with her knee."

June, chewing, said, "That's true."

"Everybody shut up," Lena said, which only made the girls laugh harder.

Her hand was damp when she pulled the phone out. She did not want to examine why. Not here, with the sauce smell sitting thick in the curtains and Gabe reaching for more bread and one twin using a carrot stick as a wand. She hit call before she could rethink the whole idea.

Eli picked up on the second ring.

"Hey," he said, and even through the speaker the low warmth of his voice changed the room for her. "Am I interrupting crimes?"

"Only dinner," Lena said.

"Worse. I should have worn a tie."

Gabe pointed at the phone. "This Gabe?"

"That's me," Eli said. In the background there was a hollow bang, maybe a loading door, maybe a case getting dropped. He raised his voice a little over it without losing the easy tone. "Hi, Gabe. Sorry to crash the meal."

"Before you crash it," Gabe said, "you know any prayers not written by dead Irishmen?"

Eli laughed. "I know one for overcooked chicken cutlets and weak gin."

Both girls cackled. Gabe smacked the table once, delighted. "Good enough."

"You're not crashing till you do it in person," Gabe said. "Right now you're a podcast."

Eli laughed, not too hard. "Fair. Then I'm honored to be your least educational one."

June lowered her eyes to her plate to hide a smile. Mateo did not hide his.

"What are you feeding her," Eli asked. "She already sent me the smell report but I don't trust her to classify sausage accurately."

"Excuse me," Lena said.

"That's slander against my Italian side," Gabe said, though Lena had never once heard him claim an Italian side before. "We're having gravy. Meatballs, sausage, salad nobody asked for, and Lena brought zucchini because she hates joy."

"Zucchini is joy if you do it right," Eli said.

Marisol leaned toward the phone. "Finally, a civilized person."

"Marisol, right?" Eli said. "Thank you for taking my side before you've even seen me."

"I respect anybody who'll eat a vegetable in this house."

"Then I'll eat two and make eye contact."

He had a clean touch for this, light enough that nobody bristled. He did not come in swinging. He matched the room's volume, quiet enough that people leaned a fraction toward him instead of shrinking back. Gabe kept waiting for the obvious line of bullshit and could not find it.

The twin in the red boots had abandoned her chair and was now standing with both palms on the table, speaking straight at the phone. "Do you have tattoos?"

Lena nearly dropped her fork. "Mina."

"What?" Mina said. "I'm asking."

Eli did not miss a beat. "Yeah, I do."

The other twin popped up too. "How many?"

"Two good ones and one I got at nineteen because I thought being stupid made me interesting."

Gabe barked a laugh. "Now that's honest."

"What's the bad one," Mateo asked, before Lena could process that he had chosen to join the conversation at all.

"A compass that points west no matter what," Eli said. "Which would be poetic if I had any idea where west was."

"That doesn't even make sense," Mateo said.

"Exactly."

One of the girls shrieked with delight. "I want a mermaid tattoo."

"No tattoos till you're thirty," Marisol said.

"Thirty is old."

"Good," Gabe said. "Stay scared."

The questions came loose after that the way they always did once children decided an adult was worth the effort. Did Eli have a dog. No, but he kept trying to befriend every ugly venue cat available to him. Did he play guitar. Not well enough to make anybody suffer through it at dinner. Did he know Taylor Swift. Personally, no; spiritually, against his will, yes. Could he hear the fan. Absolutely, and it sounded like it had survived battle.

"June fixed it," Lena said before she could stop herself, sudden and proud the way people got about tiny domestic competence.

"Of course she did," Eli said. "June saves another household."

"You hear that?" June said, lifting her glass. "Public record."

"Write it down for tax purposes," Gabe said.

Lena watched the pleasure move around the table in small ordinary ways. Marisol smiling despite herself when Eli complimented the fennel. Mateo actually looking at the phone when Eli asked what he was dreading most about school and Mateo, after pretending not to care, said, "Group projects," with a level of disgust that made Eli say, "Correct answer," like he had won something. The girls abandoning interrogation long enough to yell over each other about whose birthday came first and insisting Eli guess. Gabe leaning back in his chair, arms folded, face easing from brotherly suspicion into wary amusement that was close enough to tempt fate.

"So what do you actually do," Gabe said eventually. "Lena says sound designer, which means nothing to me."

"Mostly I make other people's messes less embarrassing," Eli said. "Live shows, recorded stuff, whatever pays. If a singer breathes like he's dying into a mic, I'm there. If a room hums like it hates God, I'm there."

Gabe tipped his head. "That's brochure talk."
