The room did not go quiet, exactly, but Lena felt it draw tighter around the table. Heat climbed the back of her neck. She had felt this snag coming all the way up Gabe's stoop: one polished answer too many and Gabe would hear performance, not person.

Mateo turned his fork between his fingers. "Yeah, but what do you actually touch. Like if the sound dies do you fix it or just wear black and look stressed."

June made a sound into her water glass that might have been a laugh.

Eli took a beat. Long enough for Lena to feel every half second of it.

"I tape cables so drunk uncles stop trying to kill themselves on them," he said. "I swap dead XLRs, ring out monitors, chase feedback, and tell wedding singers the mic isn't broken, they're just swallowing it. If an outlet's actually fucked, I go find somebody like Gabe before I light a ballroom up and make the local news."

Gabe snorted. "All right. That's a job."

"Electrician-adjacent solidarity?" Eli asked.

"SEPTA. Which means everybody blames me for power, delays, weather, and their own bad life choices."

"Then yes. Solidarity. You get the real electrocution risk. I just get blamed when a DJ plugs a fog machine into the wrong thing and suddenly it's my religion."

"He comes home mad at wires," Marisol said.

"Wires deserve it."

"Tell him about the guy who microwaved foil at the depot," Lena said.

Gabe groaned. "Why are you giving away my set?"

Eli said, "No, I need that story. It sounds healing."

And Gabe told it, because of course he did, with hand gestures and the exact proud disgust he gave every story in which another man had behaved like an idiot in a professional setting. The table leaned with him. Even the girls listened for almost twenty seconds, which in that house counted as reverence. Eli laughed in the right places, then asked one follow-up about whether the guy at least had the shame to deny it. Not too much. Enough.

Lena let herself watch instead of performing not-watching. Her fork hung halfway to her mouth while the table did the dangerous work for her, sliding him into later without asking permission first. Eli on speaker now, Eli maybe on a folding chair in this same room later, paper plate in hand, Gabe giving him shit over beer, the girls climbing him like public stairs, Marisol deciding in one look whether he was useful or decorative. It arrived whole enough that her body believed it before her head got to vote.

"When are we meeting you for real?" Marisol asked, because she had no patience for fake-casual social choreography.

Lena's face went hot.

Eli answered before she could. "Soon, I hope. I owe Gabe a proper union story and I owe your daughters evidence that I'm not just a voice in a rectangle."

"And the tattoo," Mina said.

"No tattoo," Marisol said.

"Maybe one glimpse of the stupid compass," Eli said. "Educational purposes."

"I support education," Gabe said.

"Traitor," Marisol told him.

One of the girls had somehow ended up with a meatball in her fist. The other had drawn a green spiral over half a coloring-book page and onto the table. Mateo took the phone from Lena without asking and angled it so Eli could see the drawing over the rim of the water pitcher.

"You should know what you're signing up for," he said.

"That's important consumer information," Eli said.

"She did a horse," Mateo said.

"That's not a horse," said the artist twin, outraged.

"It's emotionally a horse."

Eli laughed again, that soft quick breath of it. "I can tell. The horse has seen things."

The girls exploded. Even Lena laughed hard enough to bend. She had heard him be funny before, but alone with her, in the sealed little tunnel the two of them kept making. Here it landed differently. It had witnesses. It bounced off the walls and other people's mouths and came back altered, proven. Gabe was still smiling when he reached for the phone to hand it back.

"All right, podcast," he said. "You can stay on probation."

"High honor," Eli said. "Thank you for letting me interrupt."

"Next time you come hungry," Marisol said.

"Gladly."

Then, a fraction quieter, for Lena only though everyone could hear it if they wanted, he said, "Tell me later how the room sounded once everybody calmed down."

The word caught under her breastbone. Sounded. Not looked, not went. A private note tucked inside the public one, the reminder that he knew where her mind lived. She swallowed and said, "Okay."

When the call ended, the room did not deflate. Gabe was already reaching for more bread; one twin was licking sauce off her knuckles; Marisol kept her elbow on the table like nobody had just made space for a disembodied man. The warmth held as if a real person had only stepped outside to take another call and might come back in any minute. Gabe tore bread in half and tossed Lena one piece.

"Annoying," he said.

"Who?"

"Him. In a good way."

Marisol nodded. "Normal enough to be dangerous."

"What does that mean?" Lena asked.

"It means you like him more now because I said that."

The girls had moved on entirely. "Phone Romeo has a stupid tattoo," one of them sang.

"Phone Romeo doesn't know west," the other added.

Mateo, deadpan, said, "That part honestly tracks."

June caught Lena's eye across the table. She did not say I told you so. She did not have to. She lifted her brows once, small and private, and Lena felt herself answering with a look that probably gave away too much.

She ate the rest of her plate with the loose dizzy happiness of somebody who had gotten away with bringing a fragile thing into public and watched it survive.

***

The neighborhood had gone from white-hot to merely sticky by the time they left. Gabe pressed leftovers on them in mismatched containers because he always cooked as if weather and appetite were theoretical. Marisol sent half the fennel salad with Lena whether she wanted it or not. The girls tried to follow everyone onto the stoop barefoot until Gabe hauled them back by the elbows and threatened a bath with no cartoons. The fan could still be heard from the sidewalk, chopping away in the front room like proof of June's usefulness.

Mateo carried the seltzer June had somehow forgotten and the empty shoe box flattened under one arm. At the corner he stopped and pushed one earcup up.

"I'm going to Lopez because she wants cilantro too now," June said.

"You're going now?"

"It's two blocks."

"It was supposed to be before dinner."

"And then your uncle killed a fan."

Mateo looked at the sky as if requesting patience from a higher office. "Fine. But if she only has the sad cilantro left, I'm not taking blame."

"Nobody is prosecuting you over herbs."

"That's not how this neighborhood works."

June snorted. "Take twenty."

He took the bill, hesitated, then said to Lena, "Your guy was okay."

It sounded, from Mateo, almost extravagant. Before she could answer he added, "The compass tattoo is stupid, though," and headed toward the bodega with his shoulders folded in, composition notebook thumping lightly against his back pocket.

Lena watched him go. "He likes Eli."

"Mateo likes very few people on purpose," June said. "Take the win."

They turned toward Moore Street together, leftovers swinging between them. Warm air rose off the pavement in slow dirty breaths. People were back out on stoops now that the sun had dropped: old men in sleeveless undershirts, three teenage girls sharing one bag of chips, a woman in rollers smoking and yelling at somebody inside without real malice. A SEPTA bus groaned at the light. Somewhere close, somebody was frying garlic. Lena's skin still held the house on it, sauce and kid shampoo and the metallic cold brush of the revived fan.

June nudged her with one shoulder. "Your boy did good."

"He's not my boy."

"No? Whose is he?"

"Don't be gross."

"I'm being exact."

Lena laughed in spite of herself. The leftovers thumped against her leg. "You heard the call. He was just being normal."

"That's what I said. He was normal in a very efficient way."

"What does that mean?"

"It means Gabe handed him a test and he didn't act like he was taking one."

Lena heard the small barb in it and felt herself go instantly defensive. "Maybe because he wasn't. Maybe he's just easy with people."

"Maybe," June said.

"Why are you saying it like that?"

"Like what?"

"Like there's a hidden camera and he's secretly an actor."

June lit a cigarette off the stub of another one cupped in her palm, the flame briefly showing the tiredness in her face. "Len, relax. I'm complimenting him. A lot of men would've come on too strong. He didn't. That's good."

"Okay."

"Also he laughed at Gabe's depot moron story at the right places, which is advanced work."

"That's because it was funny."

"It was barely medium funny."

Lena stopped at the corner while a car nosed too far into the crosswalk. She could feel herself smiling again anyway, unable not to. The whole walk seemed to have taken on a thin lifted quality, as if the night had been aired out from inside. "He asked about the room after," she said.

"I heard."

"No, I mean he got what I'd want to tell. Not just what happened. The sound of it."

June looked sideways at her. "That did seem to hit you pretty hard."

Lena stared ahead at the bodega awning flapping once in the warm wind. There it was, the tiny opening, and because it was June she stepped through before she had sorted whether she should. "It did."

They crossed. Halfway down the block a sprinkler somebody had rigged to a hose leaked over the sidewalk in a fine slant, not enough to cool anything, just enough to freckle their shins. June hopped the wet patch without breaking stride. Lena walked through it. The water smelled faintly of rubber and old pennies.

"Why?" June asked.

Not nosy. Not gentle either. June at her best always asked like she expected a usable answer.

Lena shifted the containers in her arms. "Because nobody braced."

"Groundbreaking."

"Shut up."

"I'm listening."

Lena blew out a breath. "No, seriously. Gabe didn't do the face."

"What face?"

"The face where he thinks I'm about to hand the family a fresh idiot and he has to act like a comedian so I keep my dignity."

June's mouth flattened around the cigarette. She knew the face. She had seen it through the divorce, through the stupid months after when everybody handled Lena like a box with cracked dishes inside.

"Tonight he actually listened," Lena said. "He pushed him. Mateo pushed him. Eli answered and Gabe relaxed. I felt that all the way in my teeth."

June said nothing.

"And once that happened, the whole thing stopped feeling private in a way I liked too much," Lena said. "Mateo took my phone like it belonged on the table. Marisol told him to come hungry. It made Eli feel less like some voice I've been feeding in my apartment and more like somebody the room had already started clearing space for."

There. Ugly enough to be true.

June exhaled smoke through her nose. "You didn't make him up."

Lena gave her a look. "I know that."

"Do you."

"I hate you."

"No you don't."

She did not. The corner of her mouth twitched before she could stop it. June could say the meaner version and it still felt like care because Lena knew how much of her life June had already carried with no audience. Rides home from bad jobs. Advil after migraines. Three straight nights on Lena's couch after the divorce when Lena kept waking up angry and ashamed and unable to stop talking. June had seen the least flattering shapes of her and stayed anyway. That kind of history made certain kinds of danger almost impossible to imagine.

They turned onto Lena's block. Her building sat above the dark tailor shop, second-floor windows open because the heat left no good choices. The night smelled like old brick, hot trash, basil from somebody's container garden, and the sugar leak of a busted fruit bag in the alley. June flicked ash into the gutter.

"How serious is this getting," she asked, casual enough that it nearly passed.

Lena looked at her. "What do you mean."

"I mean is this fun, or are you already reorganizing the furniture in your head."

The question landed against Lena's ribs like a knuckle finding the bruise on the first try. June was good at naming the motion before Lena herself finished making it. She could have laughed it off. She could have said obviously not, it has been what, a week and change, get a grip. Instead she heard herself say, "I don't know."

June waited.

"No, that's a lie," Lena said. The truth was in her mouth before she had sanded it down. "I know exactly how it might be bad."

"Bad how."

"Bad like..." She shifted the foil container against her ribs, felt sauce warmth still trapped in it. "Bad like I'm already moving things around for him and he's still mostly a voice."

June's face did not change much. It never had to. Tired dark eyes, cigarette ember, the unreadable set her mouth took when she was listening hardest. Lena pressed on because the words had broken loose now.

"Tonight made it worse," she said. "In a good way. I know how that sounds."

"It sounds normal."

"It does not feel normal."

"Why not."

Lena laughed once, no humor in it. "Because for two years everybody has politely stopped asking if there's anybody. Gabe asks about work. Marisol asks if I'm eating. Farah asks if I'm sleeping. Nobody asks if there's anybody because the answer kept being no and then it got rude to keep poking it. Tonight there was actually someone to hand them, and I could feel my brain sprinting ahead of me. A chair at dinner. A holiday. Some stupid Tuesday where his charger is on my counter. Do you know how sick that made me feel, how much I liked it?"

June took the cigarette out of her mouth. "That isn't sick."

"It felt a little sick."

"That is because wanting things always embarrasses you right before you get them."

Lena looked up at her apartment windows. Dark except for the small blue pulse of her router on the desk by the front room window, visible from the street if she leaned right. Inside it would still be too hot. The bedsheet would still cling. The sink would still have the coffee mug from this morning with the spoon welded to the bottom by dried sugar. But now Gabe and Marisol and the girls and even Mateo had heard Eli's voice. He existed in other people's ears. The feeling of that moved through her slow and strong, closer to relief than giddiness, though it had some of that too.

"I think I could get in real trouble with him," she said quietly.

June's gaze held on her a beat too long for a joke. Then she smiled, small and crooked, and bumped Lena's arm with the back of her hand.

"Then maybe don't tell him about tonight like you're filing wedding banns," she said. "Make him miss a little."

"You are such an asshole."

"And yet I'm right."

Lena smiled despite herself. "Maybe."

"Maybe nothing. Go upstairs. Text me when Lopez only has corpse cilantro so I can blame Mateo."

"You are sending a sixteen-year-old out for herbs at nine-thirty."

"I bought him sneakers. That's called governance."

Lena snorted. June started back toward the corner, smoke trailing behind her in the damp air. Halfway down the block she lifted a hand without turning around.

Upstairs, Lena let herself into the apartment and stood for a minute in the stale heat, leftovers in her arms, listening to the window fan click every ninth turn as it shoved hot air from one side of the room to the other. The place felt smaller after Gabe's house, as it always did, more deliberate in its loneliness. She set the containers on the table, kicked off her shoes, and checked her phone.

There was a text from Gabe already.

`okay fine. podcast can come around`

And under it, from Eli:

`You sounded happy.`

Then:

`I liked hearing you in there.`

Lena sat on the edge of her bed without meaning to and read both messages twice. Sauce and fennel and other people's detergent still clung to her shirt. Outside, a bus sighed at the corner and somebody laughed loud enough to bounce off the brick opposite her window. She typed back to Eli, deleted it, typed again.

For once the apartment did not feel like the only place where he existed.


# Chapter 4

She checked the phone again and caught her own blurred face in the black screen: damp hair, old T-shirt, the strip of space between the bed and the stacked storage bins behind her. The fan in the window pushed hot air across Lena's desk instead of out, clicking every ninth turn. She had showered anyway because there was only so much of other people's sauce and the walk home and her own skin she could stand carrying. The brick had stopped radiating and started merely holding the day's heat. The apartment still felt used up by it.

She knew her angles in the divorced-woman way that had nothing to do with vanity and everything to do with damage control. Chin tipped. Window light if she could steal it. Nothing from below. Nothing full-length unless the dress had structure enough to lie for her. Her Hinge pictures gave Eli a good face, good collarbones, one wedding-guest dress from two years ago that still suggested a waist if you caught it from the right side. They did not give him the soft lower belly that folded when she sat, or the pale pressure marks a bra left under her breasts, or the little scar on her left knee that looked harsher on camera than it did in life. They did not give him the body the last year of marriage had turned into a lighting problem.

Signal lit up with his name.

You home?

Then, before she could answer:

Call me when you're alone.

And then, because he had a good sense for exactly how much to push:

I've been thinking about you since your brother made me say grace over speakerphone.

She smiled before she meant to. Gabe had not actually made him say grace. Gabe had asked if theater boys knew any prayers not written by dead Irishmen, and Eli had laughed and said he knew one for overcooked chicken. The twins had screamed into the phone. She stood in front of the mirror wedged between the kitchenette and the closet and tried to see what he would see when the screen was gone and the whole body had to arrive at once. On video she had angles. On video she had the mercy of framing, the ability to prop the phone, lean a shoulder, decide where light should hit. In person there would be the walk toward each other, the first second of recognition, the moment his face either did or did not brighten. She had thought about that second enough to hate herself a little.

Her black wrap dress lay on the bed, rejected. Too obvious. Too much like admitting she had changed the sheets for a man who had not even had his hands on her yet. The dark green sleeveless top stayed. Jeans, too. She chose the soft black bra with the underwire that no longer stabbed her in the left side and underwear that matched only in the forgiving sense of both being black. She put lotion on her knees. She put it on her elbows. She even rubbed a little on the scar on her left knee, as if he would notice it first thing and decide what kind of woman she was from there.

At five-thirty she stripped the bed again and remade one corner because the fitted sheet had come loose. That embarrassed her more than shaving had. She wasn't bringing him home. She knew that. She knew exactly how adult women were supposed to act about first dates after thirty-five, after divorce, after three weeks of hearing a man's voice in your ear and one explicit night on video that had ended with your thighs sticky and your whole body shocked by its own willingness. She knew all of it. Still, the clean towels were folded at the foot of the bed. Still, she had moved the laundry basket into the closet and shut the door on it with her hip.

At six-ten she sat at the tiny table with one sandal on and one sandal off, eating salted almonds she could not taste because she had read somewhere that drinking on an empty stomach made you look older by midnight. She was about to put on the second earring when Eli called.

Not a text. Not a voice note. A call, his face there immediately, jostling in and out of frame.

"Hey," she said, already smiling at the mess of him, because he was outside somewhere loud and his hair looked flattened on one side. "You alive?"

The smile on his face was wrong. It arrived and then fell apart before it was fully built. Behind him she saw cinder block, a wedge of white van, light too hard to be evening. He had one earbud in and one hanging loose against his collarbone, the way he always wore them when he wanted to hear both her and the room. His thumb was scraping the torn skin beside one fingernail.

"I'm so sorry," he said.

The room changed temperature. Not literally. That would have been easier. The fan still pushed hot air across her shins. A kid outside still screamed something about a foul. But inside the call a new pressure came down, clean and heavy.

"What happened?"

He tipped the phone and for a second all she got was glittering safety glass across a van seat and a black rubber weather strip hanging loose like torn licorice. Then his face again. "We were loading out. Ten minutes, maybe twelve. Somebody smashed the side window and took one of the cases. Not the whole van, thank God. Just the Pelican with the wireless packs and my interface and one stupid iLok that apparently runs half my week. I came back and it looked like a craft project in there."

He gave a little laugh at the end, automatic and bad. There was a red line across the heel of his hand where he'd cut himself on glass.

"Jesus." She was on her feet without deciding to stand. "Are you okay?"

"I'm fine. Pissed off, but fine. Nobody got hit. It just..." He dragged the back of his wrist over his mouth. "I'm at this garbage auto-glass place off Columbus now. And I can't make tonight. I hate even saying it like that. I hate it."

She looked down and saw the single earring still in her fingers. One sandal. Bare left foot on the peeling linoleum. Her whole body had been pointed toward seven-thirty for the last six hours and now had nowhere to go.

"Okay," she said, too quickly. Then, because that sounded like somebody giving him permission to disappoint her, "I mean, fuck, Eli, okay. Obviously. We can do another night."

"Len." His voice dropped, closer to the mic. "Don't do the nice voice with me. I know what tonight was."

That almost undid her. She sat back down because otherwise she would have had to brace herself on the table. "It's not a fake nice voice."

"Maybe not fake. Generous, then." He looked away from the screen, toward somebody yelling in the distance, then back. "I was really looking forward to seeing your actual face in three dimensions. I had a whole plan to pretend I wasn't nervous and then probably fail in under a minute."

She laughed once. The sound felt exposed. "You could still come later."

He shut his eyes for half a second. "I can't. They've got to vacuum out the whole door and tape it if the glass guy can't do it tonight, and I'm trying to figure out what I can borrow for tomorrow so I don't eat the job. I smell like hot wires and windshield glue. This is not my best debut."

"I don't care how you smell."

"I do." He smiled again, thinner this time. "I don't want our first real meeting to be me picking tempered glass out of my ass in a parking lot."

The line was gross enough that it should have broken the tension. Instead the parking lot rushed in around him: cut hand, hot-wire smell, tempered glass in his ass. He stopped seeming curated and started seeming stupidly real. Only an actual man with an actual body in an actual bad night would say ass to a woman he wanted and still sound embarrassed about disappointing her.

She asked what had been taken, and he told her, slower now, as if naming it made the loss solid: two receiver packs, the interface, cables he could replace without crying, the key with the software license on it that would make him want to strangle himself. The nouns meant enough to her by now. He had spent so many calls narrating the petty mechanics of load-ins and bad monitors and the particular misery of untangling cable somebody else had wrapped wrong. She could picture the case foam with the neat cutouts. She could picture his hands laying things in.

"Did you call the police?"

"Yeah. Guy took a statement like I was interrupting his smoke break. Venue manager filed one too." Somebody shouted behind him and he turned his head. "Hang on."

He looked back at her immediately, almost guilty for the second of inattention. "I gotta grab this. Len, I'm sorry."

"I know," she said.

The screen went black.

***

When the screen went black, the apartment got louder. She stood in the middle of it with her phone in one hand and the second earring in the other and felt foolish in a way that was not clean enough to call humiliation. Humiliation would have meant the story was already over. This was worse. This still wanted something.

She put on the other earring.

She left both on for maybe six minutes, standing at the sink with the tap running for no reason. Then she took them off, lined them up on the table, and read the two messages he had sent in the meantime.

need ten. auto-glass guy talking like i'm made of money

and hey. eat actual food. i'm serious

She should have let the second one soften her. Instead it irritated her enough that she called him back.

This time he answered from inside the van. Clear plastic had been taped over the missing window and breathed in and out with the night air. He looked grayer, the adrenaline burned off.

Outside, a SEPTA bus sighed at the corner. Somebody thumped down her stairs, then back up, then laughed. She looked at the clean line of her top in the dark window, at her own shoulder hovering over his face on the screen. "So what, you just need the window fixed? Or what?"

He let out a breath through his nose. Not a performance. Or if it was, it was beautiful work. "The window, yeah. And I can borrow one pack for tomorrow if I put down money. That's the part that's making me want to head-butt a wall. I can cover some of it. I'm not in rags over here. It's just a really ugly week for it."

"How ugly?"

"Lena."

"What?"

He watched her for a beat, the way he did when he wanted to make silence feel gentle instead of loaded. "Eat something, okay? You sound shaky."

It was such a him move she almost smiled. Caretake first, deflect second, answer third if the room required it. Usually she loved that about him. Right now it made her want to reach through the phone and grab the front of his shirt.

"I ate almonds."

"That's not food."

"Eli."

He glanced offscreen again. A forklift beeped somewhere behind him. When he looked back he looked tired in a way she had not seen before. Not deeper, just less arranged. "If the glass gets done tonight and I can charm the rental guy, I'm short three-fifty till Friday. Which sounds disgusting when I say it out loud, so I would love to rewind the last five seconds of my life."

There it was. Not an ask. Worse, in a way. A number with a date attached, hanging between them like a damp towel.

She thought of her checking account. Rent already gone. Electric due next week. A client invoice still outstanding because one producer believed paying on time was a moral weakness. Three hundred and fifty dollars was two long Saturdays at Farah's and one emergency evening restocking Rioja while her feet swelled in her shoes. It was also, if she let herself look at it that way, less than two percent of the trailer money sitting in savings with her mother's name still ghosting the paperwork in her head.

"I could send that," she said.

He stared at her.

"Len, no."

"I'm serious."

"So am I." The joke dropped out of his voice completely. "Absolutely not. I'm not having our first in-person night turn into you bailing me out because my week went to shit."

"You wouldn't be turning it into that. I would."

"That's not better."

She stood again, because sitting made her feel pinned. "It's a bridge. You just said till Friday."

"Yeah, and that means I can survive till Friday like every other idiot freelancer in America."

"You don't have to perform being noble for me."

His face changed at that, not much, but enough. Shame and pride crossed each other so fast they almost looked like the same thing. "I'm not performing. I'm trying not to be the guy who misses a date and then somehow ends up with your money."

The sentence landed because it was exactly the right sentence. It protected her. It also left the door open with almost painful courtesy.

Neither of them said anything for a second. She could hear street noise through his mic and her own fan chopping the room behind her into uneven pieces. Her earrings sat on the table like two tiny dropped screws.

"Okay," she said finally. "Fine. I'm not shoving anything at you right this second."

He rubbed the back of his neck. "Thank you."

"That wasn't me agreeing with you."

That got a real smile out of him, tired but real. "I know. I can hear the difference."

"Tell me what you ended up wearing tonight," he said after a moment, softer. "So I can hate myself accurately."

She looked down at the dark green top, the jeans, her bare feet on the linoleum. Desire and humiliation met so neatly in her chest it made her a little sick.

"Maybe later," she said.

"Fair." His mouth softened. "I'm sorry."

"I know."

When the call ended she stared at his thread until the words blurred. A minute later another message came through: his Cash App tag, then beneath it, don't, seriously. The warning only turned the screw. Worse, somehow, than if he'd asked outright.

Then she grabbed her bag and went to June's.

***

The air outside was barely cooler than the apartment and felt greasy on her upper lip where the wax strip had taken too much skin. Half the block was on stoops. A little girl in pink clogs dragged a jump rope through the gutter. Somebody had a game on loud enough that the announcer sounded like he lived inside the laundromat wall. Lena walked past all of it in the outfit she had chosen to be looked at in and hated every reflective surface that caught her.

June's place was three blocks and a turn away, basement stairs dropping beside a rowhouse with a dead planter on the stoop and a bike frame chained to the rail. Cooler hit her first when she opened the door, then nicotine, then the sharp plastic smell of overheated chargers. June's apartment always felt like a bunker somebody intelligent had been forced to improvise in. Cords. Old laptops. A folding desk crammed with two monitors and a chipped mug full of pens. A box fan shoved in the corner like an afterthought.

June sat in her rolling chair with one knee up, tearing a Wawa receipt into perfect little squares while something loaded on-screen. She always did that when she was thinking, reducing paper to neat confetti with nicotine-stained fingertips until the desk looked like it had lightly snowed. She had changed into basketball shorts and a faded union T-shirt and was eating dry Honey Nut Cheerios from a ceramic ramekin because, she claimed, milk after dark was demonic. Her dark hair was twisted up with a pencil.

She took one look at Lena and stopped chewing. "Jesus. He missed all that?"

Lena shut the door harder than she meant to. "Don't be funny."

"I'm not being funny. I'm being outraged on your behalf." June set the ramekin down. "What happened?"

Lena gave her the chronology too exactly. Six-fourteen call. She'd been about to leave. No, technically she had one shoe off, one on. Van window smashed after load-out. Pelican case gone. Auto-glass place on Columbus. Three-fifty short till Friday if the glass guy came through and the rental guy didn't act like a prick. She heard herself talking and knew she sounded like she was trying to make a police report convincing.

June let her get through it. Only then did she ask, "He told you three-fifty exactly?"

"Three-fifty. Exact. It wasn't a pitch."

"I didn't say it was." June picked up another piece of receipt. "What got taken?"

Lena told her.

"And the venue's not covering anything?"

"There'll be insurance or whatever eventually. He needs to float the ugly part now."

"Eventually," June said, flat. "Great word. Love eventually."

Lena took the only other chair, which had a sweatshirt on it and one dead AA battery in the seat crease. She moved both to the floor. "Can you not do that?"

"Do what?"

"That voice."

June looked at her, unreadable already. Fatigue made her face go blank faster than anger did. "I'm asking regular questions."

"You're asking them like I'm dumb."

"No." June leaned back and the chair complained under her. "If I thought you were dumb, I wouldn't bother with the questions."

That was exactly the sort of line June could get away with because she said it in the same tone other people used for pass the salt. Lena rubbed her thumb hard across the inside of her wrist where lotion still made the skin feel coated. "He didn't ask me for money."

"Okay."

"He actively said no."

"Okay."

"And I said I could send it and he said no."

June took a Cheerio from the ramekin, pointed it at her like a tiny coin. "So what's the problem?"

Lena hated how cleanly that landed. "The problem is I still want to."

June crunched the Cheerio, thinking. "How much do you want him to have and how much do you want not to be the woman who sat at home in a full face because a man had a disaster?"

The sentence was so close to the center of it that Lena actually laughed, once and sharp. "Fuck you."

"Probably a mix, then."

"You are such an asshole."

"And still useful." June swiveled toward her monitor long enough to smack a key before the screen went dark. "Look. If you want me to say don't do it, I can say don't do it. Don't do it. There. Clean."

Lena stared at her.

June shrugged. "What, you wanted a TED Talk?"

"I wanted..." Lena stopped. She had wanted something embarrassingly specific. She had wanted June to say that helping was generous and adult and not the kind of thing women got conned into by accident. She had wanted absolution dressed as advice. Saying that out loud would have made it too naked to keep.

"You wanted me to tell you what kind of person it makes you," June said.

Lena looked up so fast it felt like getting caught.

June saw that and exhaled through her nose. "Sorry. That came out meaner than I meant it."

"No, it came out exactly how you meant it."

June pushed the chair back a little and sat straighter. The joke left her voice. When that happened she did not sound colder. She sounded cleaner. "Three hundred and fifty dollars is not a symbolic amount of money for you, Len."

"I know what three hundred and fifty dollars is."

"Do you, tonight?" June asked.

The room tightened. Somewhere overhead the people in the upstairs apartment dragged something heavy across the floor. The box fan clicked on one side with every rotation. Lena could smell the stale sweet cereal on June's breath, the cigarette smoke in the curtains, the electrical hotness of an overworked power strip. Everything in the basement was material. Nothing floated.

"I know exactly what it is," she said. "It's not a fucking GoFundMe. It's a bridge till Friday."

"For him."

"Yes, for him."

"The man you haven't stood next to yet."

There it was. Not cruel. Not even wrong. Just placed where it would do the most work.

Lena folded her arms, then unfolded them because that felt adolescent. "You're acting like I offered him my Social Security number and both kidneys."

"No. I'm acting like once you float him three-fifty, this stops being a date that got canceled." June's eyes stayed on hers. "Next time you talk, the money's in the room too."

The phrasing annoyed Lena because it was good. She could feel June slotting her into place with it.

"You think he's lying."

"I think men tell the truth and still make a mess with your money every day of the week." June reached for the receipt pile again, then stopped and flattened her palm on the desk instead. "I don't know him. I know timing. I know panic costs extra. I know if a rental guy smells desperation he'll charge for the air in the room."

"He didn't ask."

"I heard you."

"So then why are you doing all this?"

June's mouth thinned, not quite a smile. "Because once you hit send, you won't remember me saying hey, maybe don't. You'll remember whether I treated you like a mark or a grown woman. I'm trying for the second one."

Lena looked away. The monitor had gone to a screensaver of dark shapes sliding over darker shapes. Her own face in the reflection looked harder than she felt.

"What would you do?" she asked.

June snorted softly. "I would steal copper out of an abandoned building before I asked a person I wanted to fuck for bridge money."

Despite herself, Lena laughed. "You're disgusting."

"Correct." June leaned over, snagged the ramekin, and held it out. "Cheerio?"

"No."

"Suit yourself." She ate one, considered, then said, "But you're not me. Thank God for the city. If you want to help him, help him. Just don't wake up tomorrow acting like you only missed dinner. You'll be out three-fifty too."

The line landed hard. Lena looked down at the phone in her lap and could feel the fabric of her top sticking between her shoulder blades.

"He said Friday," she said.

"Then Friday matters." June pointed toward Lena's bag with her chin. "If you send it and Friday comes with some fresh bullshit attached, clock that. Don't help him and then help him lie to you about it."

Lena was quiet.

June tilted her head. "How much were you thinking?"

"Three-fifty."

One of June's brows went up. "He said three-fifty."

"I know what he said."

"Okay." June let that sit for a beat. "So it's not just a bridge."

Shame came up fast enough to feel like heat. "Fuck off."

"I'm being serious."

"So am I."

June nodded once. "Then be serious all the way. If you send it, don't come back next week talking like your thumb slipped."

That pissed Lena off because it was accurate and because accuracy from June had always felt a little proprietary, as if she had rights to Lena's motives by seniority. "You don't get to sound like you're above this. You've handed men cab fare and rent and cigarettes and whatever else because you felt bad for them."

"Yeah," June said. "And every single time the money changed the room."

Silence again. Not hostile, not yet. Just loaded. The fan clicked. Upstairs somebody laughed at a joke from a TV audience. June reached up and removed the pencil from her hair. Her dark hair fell down over one shoulder in a tired wave, and for a second she looked younger, more like the girl Lena had known at twelve, ferocious and underslept and ready to tell a nun exactly where to shove her opinion.

"I don't think you're stupid," June said, quieter. "I think you're lonely and he's had a bad night and those are dangerous ingredients together."

Lena swallowed. The sentence should have sent her back from the edge. Instead the edge came into view, clear and manageable. Dangerous ingredients. Fine. Adults used dangerous ingredients all the time. Adults burned the garlic a little and kept cooking.

"He's not using me," she said, hearing even as she said it that the sentence had not been asked for.

"I didn't say he was."

"You meant it."

June's face went blank again. "Maybe I meant I don't know yet."

That was probably the most honest answer available, which made it useless.

Lena pulled out her phone.

June did not move. She did not lean in to see the screen or tell her not to. She took another Cheerio, bit it in half, and looked toward the dark monitor as if she had already said what she needed to say.

The gesture irritated Lena almost as much as if June had grabbed her wrist.

Eli's name sat at the top of the thread above the last message he'd sent ten minutes earlier: you okay? even now, even in the middle of his own mess, checking on her. Her throat tightened, but not with some generic movie ache. It was more specific than that. It felt like the stubborn rubber edge of a jar lid that had started to give. She could almost hear the seal breaking.

She opened her banking app. The checking balance looked smaller for being spoken aloud by the screen: $1,142.38. Rent gone. Phone bill still pending. A client payment overdue. Three hundred and fifty dollars would leave enough, technically. Enough was such a filthy word.

"You don't have to do it from checking," June said without looking at her. "You've got the trailer money."

Lena looked up.

June shrugged one shoulder. "I know. I'm not breaking news."

The mention of it made Lena's stomach turn. The trailer money lived in her head in stacked forms: Rosa's old vinyl cushions, the smell of mildew and sunscreen, the little dent in the screen door where her mother used to kick it shut with both hands full. She did not want Eli anywhere near that money, not even conceptually. Not yet.

"I'm not touching savings for this."

"Good," June said. "Then you'll feel it."

"You are incredible."

"I know."

Lena went back to the phone. Her hands were steady. That offended her. She opened Cash App and pasted the tag he'd sent after their call. Eli Ward resolved under the little profile circle. don't, seriously sat right below it, which only made the idea settle more deeply.

She typed `350.00`.

The screen asked if she wanted to pull it from her linked checking account. Once it went through, it went through.

She thought of the date that had been supposed to start twelve minutes ago. By now the wine bar would be filling, somebody else's elbows on the chipped counter, fries going limp in a metal basket. She thought of him in a parking lot full of safety glass and black tape. She thought of Friday.

She hit Pay.

Cash App flashed green. A check mark bloomed, then confetti. `$350.00 sent to Eli Ward.`

For a second nothing happened. Then his thread jumped.

Len.

Another bubble.

Jesus Christ.

Then the voice note icon.

She pressed play.

His breathing came first, rough with movement, then his voice, lower than before. "I told you not to do that." A pause that sounded like him looking up at the sky or the roof of the van or whatever hard thing was nearest. "Thank you. Fuck. Thank you. I'm paying you back Friday even if I have to sell blood and one decent kidney. I hate that you did this and I also..." He exhaled. "I don't have a good sentence yet. Let me have a good sentence later. Please get yourself a drink anyway, okay? Even if it's just on your couch being furious at me."

The note ended there. Not polished. Not one of his prettier ones. The rough edge bought more of her trust than a smoother thank-you would have.

Then another text bubble came through.

tell me when it lands

June held out her hand and Lena, without thinking, passed over the phone. June read the short text bubbles, listened to none of the note, and handed it back.

"Well," she said.

"Well what?"

"Now it's real."

Lena slid the phone into her bag. She wanted to defend the transfer again, but the button press had changed the argument's temperature. Before, it had been hypothetical and loud. Now it sat between her and June with the dead weight of a case of wine dropped in the middle of the floor. Nobody was pretending it wasn't there.

"He'll send it back Friday," Lena said.

"Maybe." June stood, stretching until her shirt lifted and a pale strip of stomach showed above the shorts. "Maybe he will. Maybe he'll get weird. Maybe he'll love you forever and you'll tell this story at your disgusting wedding. I don't know. I just need you to remember this exact green check mark if Friday gets cute."

Lena almost smiled. "You're unbearable."

"And yet here you are." June went to the fridge, opened it, stared in, and came back with two cans of lime seltzer because there was apparently nothing else in there worth the trip. She handed one over. "You're not telling Gabe."

It wasn't a question. Lena bristled, then stopped, because she already knew June was right.

"No."

"Farah either."

"Obviously not."

"Good." June cracked her can. "Gabe'll make it a whole man-trust seminar and Farah will ask for his social, blood type, and dental records."

"She would not ask for his dental records."

"She would if she thought he had decent teeth."

A real laugh broke out of Lena, and the relief behind it came so hard she almost cried, which was disgusting, so she drank seltzer instead. It tasted aggressively of fake lime and aluminum. She looked around the basement apartment at the cords, the screens, the little paper squares June had torn from the receipt until they formed a drift near the keyboard. Real life sat everywhere in the room. Bills. Dust. Sugar cereal. Work tabs waiting to be reopened. Her own night had become part of that mess now.

After a while she stood to leave. The outfit felt different on her body, no longer anticipatory, not exactly wasted either. Just reassigned.

"You sure you're okay?" June asked, and for once the question was plain enough that Lena couldn't hear the angle in it, if there was one.

"Yeah." She adjusted the strap of her bag. "I mean. No. But yeah."

June nodded like that answer was respectable. "Text me if he says anything weird."

"He won't."

"Sure."

At the door Lena turned. "Thanks."

June lifted her can in a tiny salute. "Go wash your face. You're gonna sleep in all that and wake up furious."

Outside, the block had thinned a little. Night had come down properly, though the brick still breathed heat. Lena walked home with the can sweating in her hand and her phone heavy in her bag.

In the apartment the clean sheets waited under the fan's crooked wind. She set down the can, took off her sandals, and pulled the transfer receipt back up without turning on the overhead light. She sent the screenshot he'd asked for, then left the phone glowing on the sheet beside her while the fan worried one corner of the pillowcase loose again.


# Chapter 6

The transfer receipt stayed bright on Lena's phone long after the room had gone dark enough that the screen hurt. Cash App: $350.00 sent to Eli Ward. Green check, confetti animation, the whole infantile little celebration for having made herself poorer at 11:42 p.m. She had screenshot it because he had been standing in a parking lot with broken glass in his voice, because he had said tell me when it lands, because there had been no way to do the thing halfway once she'd decided to do it.

In the morning the screenshot was still there, and so was the number under checking after PECO took its bite overnight: $264.18. Enough to act like nothing was wrong if she got careful. Enough to make the next two weeks uglier than she'd planned. She stood at the sink in her underwear, drinking coffee gone bitter on the second reheat, and tried out sentences the way she tried out caption lines before sending them to a client.

He didn't ask.

It wasn't rent money.

Adults helped each other.

That one held longest. Better to call it help than stand at the sink building a case against herself before breakfast.

She opened her savings and looked, just to feel less cornered. What Rosa had left in cash still sat there in its larger, quieter block, obscene in a different way. Lena had already subdivided it in her head into taxes, Visa minimums, rent, winter, whatever bad surprise came next. She hadn't touched that. Last night had come out of checking, out of the breathable part of her life, the part meant for groceries and SEPTA and whatever cheap dinner let her pretend she lived like a person with options. That distinction mattered for maybe three seconds before her brain supplied the obvious answer: money moved, and then it was moved.

Gabe texted while she was buttoning her shorts.

`Girls want to know if phone drummer fixed his apocalypse`

A second bubble came right behind it.

`Also Maite's making pernil Sunday if you can stop by`

Lena smiled despite herself. Gabe could never leave one subject alone long enough to look casual. Somewhere in his louder, brighter house the twins were probably climbing him while he tried to type with one thumb, and Maite was yelling from the kitchen that if she wanted any skin she needed to come early. She could hear the room without hearing it.

She typed, deleted, typed again.

`He's not a drummer.`

She deleted it. It was too corrective. It sounded too much like she cared.

`He's okay. Whole thing was a mess. We had to rain check.`

Before Gabe could answer she sent the second one.

`I picked up a shift Sunday though. Tell Maite I love her and I'm sorry.`

The dots appeared. Disappeared. Came back.

`Farah got you in chains`

`Only retail chains. The sexy kind.`

`That sentence just made me lose my appetite`

She could do this. She could keep the transfer where it belonged: between her, June, the receipt, and the man whose voice had gone low and stunned when it hit.

Outside, South Philly was already hot enough to smell yesterday's beer sweating out of the sidewalk. A scooter coughed past. Somebody upstairs from the tailor shop was fighting in Dominican Spanish with the windows open, not because they wanted an audience but because the building trapped heat like a grudge and nobody got privacy in September. Lena locked up, checked that the screenshot was no longer sitting naked on the home screen, and walked to Farah's with her bag knocking damply against her hip.

Farah was on a step stool when Lena came in, reaching for a row of skin-contact bottles no one on the block bought unless they wanted to perform a personality at dinner. She had one foot braced against the shelf, silver flashing at her temples under a messy knot, and the whole shop smelled like cork dust and orange peel and the cold wet mineral note of the white-wine case that had leaked near the back fridge.

"You look like a court date," Farah said without turning around.

Lena dropped her bag behind the counter. "Morning to you too."

"I'm being warm. If I wanted to be rude, I'd ask if you slept in your eyeliner on purpose."

"I wasn't wearing any."

Farah got down from the stool and looked at her properly. She had a strip of price-tag backing stuck to one palm and a pencil tucked through the neck of her T-shirt. "Even worse. What happened?"

Lena went for the version that had the fewest moving parts. "The date didn't happen. His van got hit after load-out. Somebody smashed a window, grabbed part of his kit. It turned into one of those stupid nights where everybody is standing around in a parking lot pretending they're calmer than they are."

"Jesus." Farah peeled the paper off her palm and flicked it at the trash. "He had insurance?"

"I didn't ask for the whole fucking claim sequence."

"You didn't ask because you were trying not to look like a payroll auditor." Farah's mouth tipped, not kind exactly but not cruel either. "Did he at least sound embarrassed?"

Lena heard the question under the question. "Yeah."

"Good. Men should sound embarrassed sometimes. It keeps the blood moving."

Lena laughed. It came out thin. She turned on the register, listened to its little waking beep, and hated the way everything with money announced itself. The drawer popped. Receipt tape curled like a tongue from the side.

Farah watched her a second longer. "You want tea?"

"I'm fine."

"That wasn't a wellness check. I don't want you passing out into the Beaujolais."

"I had coffee."

"Then God help the customers."

The first hour moved the way shop hours always did on hot weekends: slow until it was suddenly not. A guy from two blocks over came in for two warm six-packs and spent five minutes arguing with the price of ice as if Farah personally had frozen it with malice. A woman in scrubs bought a bottle of sauvignon blanc and the cheapest dark chocolate bar from the side rack, then stood at the card reader squinting at the total long enough that Lena pretended to rearrange bags so the pause would have some place to go. The bell over the door kept making its brittle sound. The AC kept losing to the weather.

Between customers Lena checked her banking app once, then again five minutes later as if numbers might feel less accusatory on the second look. $264.18. Rent wasn't due yet. The Visa minimum would sit on the twenty-second like a little knife with a calendar invite. One caption invoice was supposed to clear Tuesday, maybe Wednesday if the client felt slow and holy. She locked the phone and slid it under the counter, like hiding it from herself counted as discipline.

Near noon Farah cut open a box of shelf talkers and said, "I can get Sami to cover Thursday close if the distributor actually sends the rose this time."

Lena was breaking down cardboard in the back, the corrugation rough against the heel of her hand. "I'll do it."

Farah kept writing on the case stack with her marker. "You already have Saturday."

"I can do Thursday too."

That made Farah look up. "You can. That's not what I said."

Lena shrugged, too fast. "I've got a gap."

"If you're broke, say you're broke. Don't say gap like you're briefing a hostage negotiator."

"Broke-adjacent, then."

Farah set the marker between her teeth, capped it, and came farther into the back room where the floor always smelled faintly of wet cardboard and old cork. "I don't need a confession, Lena. I need to know if you want hours or if you want distraction, because those are different payroll categories."

The question landed harder because it was better than the one Lena had been bracing for. She leaned the flattened box against the wall. "Hours."

Farah studied her another second, then nodded once. "Thursday close. If the truck's late, you stay till inventory's done. That gets you closer to one-eighty than one-twenty."

"Thanks."

"Eat before you come. I mean it. You get mean when your blood sugar drops and then you start telling customers the truth."

"I tell them plenty of truth."

"You told that man last month that his favorite cabernet tasted like mulch and divorce."

"It did."

Farah gave her the smallest smile. "See."

They worked the rest of the shift in pockets: silence, then bursts of talk, then silence again. Farah told her the landlord next door was trying to raise commercial rent on a nail salon nobody had painted in six years. Lena took a call from a subtitling vendor who wanted a turnaround so stupid it almost qualified as an insult. A college kid came in asking for orange wine "that didn't taste like feet," and Farah sent him to the fridge with such regal contempt that Lena had to bite the inside of her cheek not to laugh.

When the lull came, Farah pushed half a bruised peach across the counter on a paper towel. "Eat."

Lena looked at it. "That's a depressing amount of fruit."

"It's a realistic amount of fruit. Eat it."

The bruise had gone dark as tea at one side, but the rest was cold from the shop cooler and sweet enough to make her feel briefly restored. Juice ran down the heel of her thumb. She licked it off before it could drip on the register keys.

Her phone buzzed with a message from Eli. She saw only the start in preview.

`I know last night got ugly and I don't want you...`

She turned the screen facedown so fast Farah noticed.

"Oh, so the musician lives."

"Apparently."

Farah was slicing a case strap with her box cutter. "You don't have to narrate this to me like a courtroom sketch artist. I'm just saying if he starts acting like his emergency moved into your apartment, that's a bad sign."

"He didn't."

"All right."

It was only two words, but Farah had a way of putting entire ledgers into them. Lena went back to stacking shelf tags. She could feel the message under the phone like heat under a door.

After clock-out she took the long way home so she would not have to walk past Gabe's place and smell dinner in the windows. At the corner store she stood in front of the fridge and did the arithmetic that had become second nature since her divorce: eggs or decent coffee, laundry card reload or the good detergent, sliced turkey or just peanut butter again. She left with a dozen eggs, a bag of rice, two cans of black beans, and the store-brand coffee that tasted faintly like wet pennies unless she drowned it in sugar. The guy at the register gave her twelve cents in change and a receipt so long it looked accusatory.

By the time she climbed the stairs above the tailor shop her T-shirt was stuck to the middle of her back. The apartment held the day's heat with vindictive loyalty. She fed the window unit two sharp slaps before it rattled into obedience, changed into an old tank top, and opened the subtitling file Farah's call had reminded her to accept because she could no longer afford the dignity of saying no to nonsense.

It was a Hungarian film, or maybe just Hungarian enough to her ear that it made no difference. The vendor had sent translated dialogue in a sidecar document and audio that crackled in one channel. Lena did not understand a single word in the original. What she had were timecodes, an English approximation, and two actors fighting in a kitchen so small their voices kept bouncing off tile.

She spent forty minutes deciding whether a line needed `you always do this` or `you always do this to me`, because one version meant habit and the other meant indictment, and the translation note from the vendor only said EMOTIONAL ACCUSATION. The man's voice went up at the end as if pleading. The woman's cut through him in flat strokes. A pan hit something hard. A chair scraped. Lena timed the captions to breath and collision more than to language, feeling for the moment one mouth took over from the other.

At 9:17 Eli sent a voice note. She knew from the waveform shape before she opened it that it wasn't short. She left it unopened and kept working.

In the film the woman crossed the room in hard shoes and said something that made the man laugh once, sharp and humorless. The translation offered `Then keep your gift.` A beat later came `Nobody asked you to be noble.` Lena rolled back the waveform, listened to the original again, and changed `gift` to `favor` because it sounded less ceremonial and more cruel.

By ten she had billed three hours she would maybe get paid for in fourteen days. Thursday close would add somewhere between one-twenty and one-eighty, depending on the truck and whether Farah remembered to count the inventory time right. Three hundred and fifty dollars could be reduced, if she worked at it hard enough, into separate harmless pieces: two extra shop shifts, one ugly foreign-language file, coffee she did not buy, dinner she cooked herself. It was only money. Money went and came. Money was not a verdict unless you let it be.

She finally set the headphones down because the Hungarian couple were still screaming and she could feel their argument in the bridge of her nose. In the silence after, the building gave her the neighborhood back: TV laugh track through the wall, somebody dragging a cooler down the sidewalk, a SEPTA rumble far enough away to feel like furniture moving in the earth.

Eli's note was still waiting.

She looked at it, then at the rush file still half done, then at Thursday close written in Farah's blue marker in the photo she had snapped of the new schedule. By midnight her eyes felt sanded. Her hands smelled like peach skin and cardboard and the faint metallic ghost of coins.

She put the phone facedown again and went back to the arguing lovers.

***

June's place was cooler by eight degrees and smelled like cigarettes nobody was currently smoking, old plastic, and the cumin from whatever had been microwaved an hour ago. Lena had texted first to see if June wanted the bruised half of Farah's free Manchego, and June had answered `come down if you're alive / school portal wants a body count.`

The basement apartment looked as if every cord in South Philadelphia had molted there. Old laptops sat shut and stacked near the wall. A box fan chopped the air in one corner. Mateo was at the folding table in a maroon school hoodie with one drawstring missing, his beat-up headphones hanging around his neck, one ear pad held together with black electrical tape. He was clicking a mechanical pencil open and shut with the concentration of a person trying not to say what he really thought.

June barely glanced up from the district website on her screen. "Tell me you brought cheese and not sympathy."

"I brought very judgmental cheese."

"Good. Sit down. This thing wants three proofs of address, which is adorable because children famously control housing."

Mateo did not look up. "It also wants my emergency contact to be somebody who picks up the phone before noon, which feels biased."

June stabbed the keyboard once. "Use me first and your mom second."

He stopped clicking. "She'll get weird about it."

"She can get weird after you stay enrolled."

The sentence could have come out hard. In June's mouth it came out practical, almost bored, like she had already spent the emotional version somewhere else and only the useful part was left. She wore work boots unlaced and an old black tank top, and there was a nicotine stain on the fingers she used to flatten Mateo's crumpled attendance form. Her face looked younger when she was tired, not prettier exactly, just stripped down to the bones Lena had known since middle school.

Lena put the cheese on the table between a geometry workbook and a stack of photocopies. "What did he do?"

Mateo's eyes stayed on the paper. "I was late."

June snorted. "He was late nine times in eleven days and skipped one advisory because the vice principal decided headphones were a gateway drug."

"I took them off."

"You took them off after she said your name three times."

"That still counts as taking them off."

Lena sat on the arm of the couch because every chair in June's place had paperwork on it. "That's a very lawyerly defense."

Mateo glanced at her then, brief and sideways. His acne was worst across the forehead this month, angry little marks above the line where the headphones lived. "It's a factual one."

"There he is," June said. "The boy speaks in full paragraphs only when he's technically right."

A small smile tried Mateo's mouth and gave up. He went back to the form.

Lena reached for one of the photocopies. It was a temporary guardian authorization, half the boxes already filled in by June's dense all-caps. Another sheet was an attendance contract with phrases like RESPONSIBILITY and IMPROVED OUTCOMES that made Lena want to bite somebody. There was a printed email from a counselor noting that continued lateness could affect access to after-school programs.

"This is for the lab?" she asked.

Mateo's head came up fully at that. "Yeah. If they pull it, I can't stay for comp-sci. Which is stupid, because I'm late because I'm walking my cousin to middle school three mornings a week, and then they're gonna make me miss the only useful thing in that building."

"There it is again," June said. "See how he suddenly has a TED Talk."

He ignored her. When he cared, the words came cleaner, almost adult. "Mr. Nguyen said if I keep the seat I can help with the district app thing for the robotics team. If I lose it because of homeroom, that's insane."

Lena liked that about him, the way whole opinions were apparently stored under the blank surface and only came out when the subject deserved it. "Did you tell them that?"

"I told them the cousin part. I'm not telling them about comp-sci because then it sounds like I'm negotiating."

June shoved the temporary guardian form toward Lena. "Can you read line seven? My eyes are fried."

Lena read it aloud. "`If parent or legal guardian is unavailable due to treatment, displacement, or other documented hardship...`"

Mateo said, "That is such a gross sentence."

"Most official sentences are gross," Lena said.

June leaned back, rubbed both hands over her face, then sat forward again before the exhaustion could harden into stopping. "All right. We'll upload the rehab letter, your mother's ID copy, and the lease. Then tomorrow I go to that feral check-cashing place on Snyder and get this notarized."

"You work tomorrow," Lena said.

"I work all the time."

"You also need sleep sometimes."

June gave her a look that said sleep was a hobby for richer people. "If the portal rejects one more PDF, I'm setting the school on fire. Teo, sign there. No, not like you're forging somebody else's death certificate. Write your whole name."

Mateo bent over the paper. His handwriting was unexpectedly neat, each letter separated from the next with stubborn care.

Lena had come because she wanted, if she was honest, a verdict. Not a big one. Something June could say while lighting a cigarette on the back steps: you did a kind thing, you didn't do a dumb thing, he sounded real about it, stop chewing the inside of your cheek. But June's attention was split into ten necessary strips. A school portal was failing. Mateo needed proof of residence and a signature and probably dinner. June's phone kept vibrating with work Slack notifications she ignored with practiced hatred. There was no room in the apartment for Lena's private moral accounting without taking up space somebody else needed for actual trouble.

June clicked to another page and swore under her breath. "Who designed this? A narc with a grudge?"

"What if," Mateo said, still not looking up, "you uploaded the file they wanted."

"What if you shut up and hand me the rehab letter."

He passed it over. His fingers had printer ink on them. A fine line of graphite marked the side of his hand from the pencil.

Lena said, "I can print things upstairs if you need the Wi-Fi not to die."

June answered without looking away from the laptop. "Bless you. They're rejecting the lease scan because apparently JPEGs are immoral now."

"I'll do it."

"You don't have to."

"I know."

That got June to look at her, finally, directly. There was a second where Lena thought June might ask the real question. Instead she took in Lena's face the way Farah had at the shop, precise and fast.

"You look tired," June said.

"Everybody keeps saying that like it's news."

"It's not news. It's me checking if you're about to become work for me."

The line was half joke, half neighborhood affection, and under other circumstances Lena would have thrown it back. Instead she heard in it the whole old arrangement between them: June as the one who could fix a mess if Lena named it. June as the person who knew where the copier still worked at nine p.m., which landlord took partial cash, which form actually mattered. June as the person who had heard enough of her life to sort panic from inconvenience inside thirty seconds.

"I'm fine," Lena said, and hated how quickly it came.

June held her gaze one beat longer, then went back to the laptop. "Good. Then print the lease and steal that kid a plate before he tells me he's not hungry."

"I'm right here," Mateo said.

"And yet you never feed yourself."

"I had cereal."

"That was yesterday."

"It was this morning."

June looked at Lena. "See? This is my glamorous life."

There was affection in the complaint, old and automatic. Mateo rolled his eyes but the color had come back into the room a little, the pressure distributed. Lena went upstairs, printed the lease from her phone because June's printer had died a year ago and still lay under the couch like a pet nobody admitted was dead, then came back down with the pages warm in her hand.

By the time she returned, June had coaxed the portal into accepting three of the four uploads and was on hold with somebody from attendance. She stood with the phone pinned between shoulder and ear, rummaging in the fridge one-handed. "Yes, I understand that's the policy," she said, voice flat with manufactured patience. "I'm asking what you want from me that I haven't already sent you twice."

She pulled out leftover rice, a container of shredded chicken, half an onion in plastic wrap. Mateo had opened the Manchego and was shaving slices off with a butter knife that was absolutely not meant for precision work.

"Use a real knife before you lose a thumb," June said into the air, not sure whether she was addressing him or the woman on the phone.

Mateo muttered, "You know thumbs are useful," and kept cutting.

Lena took the butter knife from him and did it herself. The cheese had sweated a little in the walk over. She could feel the softened edge give under the blade. June, still on hold, pointed with two fingers at the microwave, then at Mateo, then at her own mouth: feed him. Lena did.

None of it was noble. That was the part that made it persuasive. The rice clumped because the container had dried out in the fridge. Mateo wanted hot sauce and found the wrong one. June got transferred twice and only raised her voice once, which for her counted as sainthood. While the rice turned in the microwave, Lena saw the stack of work printouts under the attendance forms: KeyNest spreadsheets, maintenance tickets, somebody's lease-renewal dashboard. June's whole life looked like interfaces that assumed a cleaner reality than the people using them could provide.

When the school finally picked up in human form, June's voice changed. It did not get softer. It got straighter.

"Hi. Yes, thank you. Salazar. S-A-L-A-Z-A-R. I'm his aunt and current guardian, and I'm looking at the portal right now. It says the parent-treatment document is unreadable, which is interesting, because I can read it just fine."

Mateo stopped pretending not to listen.

June listened, eyes on the table, then said, "No, his mother isn't available for an in-person signature. That's what treatment means. Right. So if I bring the notarized form and the lease tomorrow, he keeps his lab access in the meantime? I need you to say yes or no to that, not give me a poem."

Lena looked away, smiling into the cheese because if June caught her enjoying this she would start performing. Mateo's pencil finally went still.

After another minute June got the answer she wanted, or something close enough to weaponize later. She hung up and exhaled through her nose.

"Tomorrow by three," she said. "They'll keep you in comp-sci if I show up with the notarized form and if you stop strolling into first period like a visiting dignitary."

Mateo's whole body loosened a fraction. He hid it by reaching for the rice. "I wasn't strolling."

"You were spiritually strolling."

He ate two bites standing up, still half folded into himself. "Thanks."

June waved a hand. "Be worth the paperwork."

It was as close to tenderness as she let herself get when he could hear it. Mateo heard it anyway. He took his plate to the couch and sat with his knees high, headphones back around his neck, eating with the concentration of a kid who had been pretending not to be hungry for too long.

Lena watched June stack the papers back into order. Her fingers were quick even tired. She smoothed the corners, clipped the forms together, slid the rehab letter into a plastic sleeve so it would not get ruined in her bag tomorrow. There was nothing dramatic in it. That was why it landed. A woman could do all this and still be funny at block parties, still fix fans at Sunday dinner, still know exactly how much lighter fluid to bring to the grill. Ordinary care had never looked pure in their neighborhood. It looked annoyed and underfunded and relentless.

"You want a drink?" June asked. "Water, not feelings."

"That's rude," Lena said.

"It's efficient."

Lena took the water. The bottle had been in June's freezer too long and thin ice floated at the lip. She drank, feeling the cold on her teeth, and almost said it. I sent him three hundred and fifty dollars and now I keep acting like that makes me a person with character instead of a person with bad impulse control. I picked up extra hours before the money even cleared like I was paying court costs to myself. Tell me if this is insane.

Instead she said, "He sent a voice note."

June twisted the cap back onto her own bottle. "And?"

"I didn't play it yet."

That made June laugh once, tired and real. "Jesus, Len. Why not?"

"I don't know. I wanted to finish work first."

"You wanted to decide what it meant before he told you."

Lena made a face. "Maybe."

June took that in while looking past her at Mateo, who had paused mid-bite because his phone was lighting up with a group chat. "If he says thank you like a grown man, fine. If he makes you feel like an ATM with bangs, kill him."

Mateo said without looking up, "That sentence was disgusting."

"You're sixteen. Everything is disgusting to you."

"It should be."

June stood and reached for her cigarettes, then remembered she was not going upstairs yet. She tucked the pack under the guardian forms instead. "Play it or don't. Just don't sit there inventing nine versions and picking the meanest one. That's your hobby, not your job."

It was not the verdict Lena had wanted. It was too partial, too folded into the rest of June's life. But maybe that was the closest thing to an honest answer she was going to get from anybody who loved her enough not to dramatize. June had real paperwork on the table, real obligations that needed a notary stamp and a working printer. Lena's question, next to that, looked embarrassingly optional.

She stayed another twenty minutes. Mateo found a typo in the attendance form and became briefly eloquent about bureaucratic illiteracy. June heated herself a plate only after he had eaten half of his. A Slack message from KeyNest finally drew a hard curse out of her, not because of anything grand, just because somebody wanted an updated tenant spreadsheet by morning and had typed `gentle reminder` in the subject line. Lena left when the apartment started to feel too full of other people's tomorrow.

Outside, the night had that warm September drag where sound traveled farther than it should. Somebody was playing old bachata from a parked car. A couple argued at the corner in voices flattened by exhaustion. Lena walked home with the warm lease-copy smell still on her fingers and June's half answer knocking around in her head.

At her kitchen table she ate rice from the container she'd brought back because June had shoved leftovers at her on the way out. Her subtitling file waited on the laptop. The Hungarian lovers were paused mid-accusation, mouths open in a silence that looked almost comic without sound.

She opened Eli's note.

His voice came in a little rougher than usual, like he had not fully slept. The parking-lot chaos from last night had dropped away. What remained was careful and close to the ear.

"Hey," he said. "I know you hate being made into a saint, so I'm not doing that. But I need to say this clean. You saved my ass."

He took a breath. She could hear traffic behind him, or maybe TV, something moving in the distance that made the intimacy feel earned rather than studio-smooth.

"Not just with the money. The money mattered, obviously. But last night was one of those garbage nights where everything gets small and stupid and you start feeling embarrassed to even be standing there in public with your own mess. And then you didn't make me beg, you didn't make me explain it six different ways, you just... you were with me. That part got me."

Lena sat very still.

"I'll pay you back," he said. "That's not even a question. But I'm probably gonna remember the other part longer. The way you showed up. I don't know. It made me feel less alone in my own life for a minute. That's a big thing to give somebody."

The note ended before it could get slick. There was no speech about fate and no big promise about next time. Just his breath at the end, and the tiny awkward noise of somebody tapping the phone because he had almost let the silence go on too long.

Lena played it again.

The second time she caught the places where he had almost corrected himself and had not. She caught the specific shame of public mess, the thing she understood immediately because humiliation was always louder when other people could see it. She caught, too, that he had said `with me` instead of `for me`. It was not charity or rescue. It was company.

The transfer receipt was still buried in her notifications somewhere, green and stupid. Farah's Thursday close waited on the fridge under a magnet shaped like a tomato. The eggs she had bought instead of takeout were lined up in their carton like minor witnesses. None of that vanished. The money had left her account. Thursday would still smell like cardboard and spilled sauvignon blanc. She would still be eating beans and rice by the sink, doing subtitling math in her head, telling herself the store-brand coffee was a preference and not a budget line. But Eli had given her something she could brace against the expense: he had said with me, not for me. He had made the loss feel less like a stupidity tax and more like the cost of staying beside somebody through public humiliation.

She hated how badly she wanted that version. She hated, too, how relieved she felt when it settled in her chest and stopped rattling around loose.

On the laptop the Hungarian woman was still frozen in her kitchen, mouth sharp around a word Lena could not hear until she hit play. Lena slid the headphones back on, returned to the line she had been leaving half wrong all night, and changed `Keep your favor` to `Then keep it.` The cut was cleaner. The cruelty sat closer to the bone without having to explain itself.

Her phone buzzed once with a follow-up text from Eli.

`Get some sleep, okay? I know you pretend you're made of wire.`

That made her laugh softly, against her will. She had told him that once, weeks ago, talking about how caption deadlines stacked in her back and wrists until she felt soldered together. He had saved it. Used it right.

Lena starred the voice note, turned back to the file, and let the room settle around the choice she had already made not to treat the rain check like an ending. By the time the lovers on her screen started shouting again, the transfer no longer felt like evidence against her. It felt like part of the kind of woman she was trying, with some strain, to believe herself to be.


# Chapter 7

The plastic fastener holding the dress tags to the side seam kept scraping Lena's ribs through the lining. She had tucked the paper tag flat under her bra band so it would not show through the fabric, but every time she bent over the bed to check her overnight bag it sprang loose again, a little dry scratch against her skin.

Last night's transfer had been only the first line item. She had bought the dress at the Marshalls on Oregon because the receipt gave her thirty days and because the blue was dark enough to survive one careful evening. The tag said $39.99. The round-trip train had cost $78.40 if she did not miss the ride home. She had spent another eleven dollars on a tiny roller of amber oil at Rite Aid because the bottle of perfume she already owned still smelled, to her, like courthouse carpet and the winter her divorce papers came through. By eight-thirty that morning she had already shaved her legs, under her arms, and the backs of her thighs in the thin bathroom light, balancing one foot on the closed toilet lid like a woman much more graceful than she was. She nicked her ankle and pressed a wad of toilet paper to it until the bleeding stopped. In the overnight tote she packed black underwear that still had some elastic left in it, a toothbrush in a sandwich bag, travel deodorant, ibuprofen, charger, and one condom from the three-pack she had bought so quickly the night before she had not looked the teenage cashier in the eye.

The foil square sat in the inside pocket against her wallet like a dare.

She checked the time again. She checked Eli's last message again, though nothing in it changed because she looked harder.

`Tell me what color so I can picture you correctly.`
