When she had written back `blue`, he had answered almost at once.

`Blue on you is never gonna be wrong. Meet me under the big clock at 4:15? I might smell like dust and gaff tape.`

That had come in at 7:06, while she was still in her old sleep shirt and standing at the sink with toothpaste foam on her tongue. She had been carrying it around since then, the sentence with its easy certainty, the fact of a man in another city already imagining her body moving through the day toward him.

She had not told Gabe. If she told Gabe, the whole thing would become family material immediately. He would ask what train. He would ask what time she expected to be back. He would tell Lena not to be stupid in the affectionate older-brother-reversed way he had developed since the divorce, as though he had been born thirty-three and married. She had not told Farah either. Farah's face would have gone too careful. Farah would have handed over a hug or a joke or some bottle-shop wisdom about men being, in the aggregate, not worth dry cleaning, and Lena could not have borne even practical tenderness before she had something solid to show for this.

She had told June, because June already knew everything and because the part of Lena that still wanted witness had wanted one person to know she was getting on the train.

June's answer had come at 7:21. `Text when he's 3D.`

That was June at her gentlest, a joke hiding a blessing inside it. Lena had smiled at the screen and hated herself a little for needing that much from eight words.

At 30th Street she bought coffee that tasted like somebody had filtered it through copper pennies. Families sprawled across the seats under the high ceiling with their bags around them like barricades. A woman in lavender scrubs slept open-mouthed against a backpack. Two college boys argued over whether they had time to buy egg sandwiches before boarding and kept saying bro with the worn reflex of men tapping a loose tooth. The departure board clicked and shifted above everybody's heads. Lena loved that board in a workman's way. It changed without drama. It told the truth in red blocks.

On the platform she kept one hand over the opening of her tote as if she were carrying cash. The dress moved differently from anything else she owned. It whispered when she walked. In the train window, with the station dimly reflected over her own face, the blue made her look less tired than she felt. She took that as a good sign, which was stupid, but the whole trip rested on a structure of little stupidnesses already. She sat by the window and watched the industrial backs of Philadelphia pull away, brick and steel and a seam of graffiti bright enough to look freshly wound. The seat fabric smelled like old rain and detergent.

Eli kept her company all the way past Wilmington. Not constantly. He was too good at this to overplay it. A picture of a cable snake on a scuffed stage floor with the toe of his boot in frame. A voice note low enough that she had to press the phone closer to her ear.

"You on the train?"

His laugh had that warm grain in it, the one that made her think of felt and amplifier hum.

"Tell me when you see marsh. I always miss it when I'm on no sleep."

She did tell him. She told him about the flat pewter stretch outside the window and the white birds standing in it like dropped napkins. He sent back, `There you are doing location scouting for me.` He asked whether she had eaten. He reminded her to drink water because train coffee did not count. He said it lightly, not as a correction, and that kind of care had become one of the things she wanted from him most: not flattery, not even desire exactly, but the irritating, impossible miracle of being remembered in real time.

By Baltimore the whole car smelled faintly of fries and somebody's body spray. Lena went to the bathroom and changed shoes because the heeled sandals looked better with the dress and because she had packed flip-flops for the return like a woman planning sensibly for morning-after feet. In the mirror the station light had found the fine broken capillaries at the side of her nose. She dabbed on more concealer. Her hands shook only a little. When she came back to her seat, Eli had sent a message with no punctuation at all, which always made him feel more bodily to her, less composed.

`you close`

`twenty minutes maybe`

`I'm looking at that clock already in my head`

The last message sat with her all the way into Washington.

Union Station hit her first as brightness. White vault overhead, polished floor, the noise of wheels and rolling bags multiplying under the ceiling until it all became one hard public sound. Everybody looked temporary and intent. Lena stepped off the escalator into the main hall with her tote cutting into her shoulder and saw the clock immediately, bigger than she had pictured from his photos. She went to the bathroom first because arriving too early and then standing there in plain sight felt worse than making him wait one minute. In the stall she fixed the dress where it had ridden up under the bag strap and checked that the paper tag still sat flat inside the seam. In the mirror she rubbed away the shine above her lip and pinched color into her cheeks like she was seventeen.

At 4:08 she walked out and took her place under the clock.

He was not there, which was fine, because she was early.

At 4:12 he texted.

`Running ten late. Load-out bullshit. Don't let me walk in and catch you giving me that disappointed face.`

She smiled in spite of herself and typed back, `You have never seen my disappointed face.`

`I know exactly what it looks like.`

At 4:24 she was still under the clock, with a family from Ohio unpacking themselves onto the benches nearby and a man in a Georgetown hoodie pacing in little aggravated diagonals while he argued into his earbuds about receipts. She checked the entrance every time somebody tall with dark hair came through. She checked it less gracefully every five minutes after that. At 4:31 she wrote, `You close?`

He answered at 4:33.

`I hate this. Another ten. I owe you first drink and then some.`

That was still within the range of ordinary inconvenience. Train stations were made to hold inconvenience. That was practically their religion. Lena walked the perimeter of the hall once because standing in one spot made her feel visible in a way walking did not. She paused at the window and looked out at the traffic with no interest in traffic. She took out her phone and put it away again.

At 4:47 she called him.

Straight to voicemail.

The voicemail itself did not panic her. Reception in big public buildings was always weird. Batteries died. Men carrying cables ignored their phones. Whole adult days went crooked. She told herself all of that in a series of brisk internal sentences that sounded, annoyingly, like caption notes. At 4:51 she sent, `I'm gonna sit somewhere. Tell me where you are when you can.`

There was a bar off the main hall with brass railings and televisions no one seemed actually to be watching. Lena chose a stool with sightline to the doors and set her tote on the one beside it so nobody could take the space where he might still land. The bartender was a middle-aged woman with shellacked green nails and a paper-crane tattoo on the inside of her wrist; every few minutes she straightened the stack of coasters by the register with two swift taps, as if untidiness offended her at the molecular level.

"What can I get you?" she asked.

Lena almost said water because water was what a reasonable woman waiting for a slightly late date ordered. Instead she heard herself say, "Gin and tonic. Light on the tonic."

"Smart," the bartender said, and slid a glass of ice water over too.

Lena thanked her too quickly.

The drink came cold enough to hurt her teeth. She made it last until 5:10. At 5:03 Eli sent nothing. At 5:07 he sent nothing. At 5:10 Lena finished the gin anyway because the ice had gone thin and flat and she could feel herself becoming the sort of woman the whole room could read correctly.

The bartender came back. "You eating or just drinking your dinner?"

"I think I'm meeting someone."

The words sounded worse out loud than they had in Lena's head for the last six weeks.

The bartender gave no visible sign of pity, which made Lena grateful enough to want to cry. "Kitchen's decent if he keeps being an asshole."

"Thanks," Lena said, and looked down at her phone so she would not have to meet the woman's eyes.

At 5:14 another message finally arrived.

`Phone on fumes. Not ghosting you. Swear to God. Give me ten.`

This one did calm her, stupidly, for the length of one swallow. The back of her neck loosened. Then it thinned out again. Ten minutes. Another ten. Phone on fumes. Swear to God. She worried the paper napkin under her drink into wet strings and kept the tote on the stool beside her.

She stayed.

***

By 5:38 she had ordered fries she did not want because an empty bar stool with one untouched drink in front of it would have been unbearable. The fries arrived in a metal basket lined with fake newsprint. Grease darkened the paper in expanding circles. The television over the bar played a baseball game with the sound off while a closed-caption crawl underneath lagged two pitches behind, the catcher crouched and rising before the words caught up. Lena watched the delay more than the game. She could not help it. It felt like professional deformity and prophecy at once.

At 5:41 Eli called.

She answered so fast she almost dropped the phone.

"Hey."

What came back first was noise. Traffic. A car horn close enough to clip the mic. Somebody dragging something heavy over pavement.

"Lena."

His voice was breathless and lower than usual, as if he were trying not to shout.

"I'm so sorry. I'm so fucking sorry."

The apology landed first as relief. A body. A voice. Whatever else was wrong, she was not talking to nothing. She closed her eyes and let that hit her before she did anything smarter with it.

"Where are you?"

"Not where I said I'd be. I know. I know." He exhaled hard, and for a second the line crackled. "My sister Noelle's place got blown apart last night. Her ex showed up drunk and went through it. I'm over here trying to get her out before he circles back. I should've called. I know I should've called."

Lena opened her eyes. The bar came back around her in detail so sharp it felt punitive: the lemon wedge softening in her glass, the paper napkin half dissolved under the sweating base, the couple at the high-top sharing crab dip with the calm concentration of people who had successfully arrived somewhere together.

"What do you mean, blown apart?"

"I mean he kicked the bathroom door in and ripped the shower rod down and dumped her mail in the sink. She keeps all her bills in one of those blue Danish-cookie tins like she's ninety. He turned the water on over the whole thing. He smashed this stupid rabbit lamp her aunt gave her when she first moved out. I'm standing in broken glass right now trying not to cut through my shoe."

It was too many details and therefore, perversely, easier to believe.

"Jesus."

"Yeah." She heard him say something away from the phone, his voice blurred by distance. When he came back he sounded closer and rougher. "She keeps apologizing to me like she did this. I swear I'm not making up an artful fucking tragedy because I'm late. I know how this sounds."

That line, the ugliness of it, the fact that he heard how it sounded before she had to say it, slid under her anger in a way she hated.

"Where is she now?"

"Downstairs. Crying in a Corolla with three trash bags and a basil plant. One of the bags ripped because of course it did." He let out a short exhausted laugh that died immediately. "Lena, I'm sorry. I know you came all the way down here."

Her hand went to the paper tag under the seam and pressed it hard enough that the little plastic barb dug her finger. She pictured a blue cookie tin tipped on its side in a flooded sink. A rabbit lamp broken at the base. A woman she had never met holding a basil plant because there were always things people saved that made no sense to outsiders. The story had the stupid frayed edges real messes had. It did not arrive packaged for belief. It spilled.

"Did she call the cops?"

"They came. They took notes. They said because he was gone they couldn't do much till she filed on Monday unless he came back while they were standing there, which was apparently too much to ask of everybody." He made a noise between a laugh and a swallow. "My phone's at four percent. I'm trying to find a charger in this apartment and everything is wet or smashed or under a man named Craig's foot from downstairs. He keeps saying, 'You gotta move, sweetheart, you gotta move,' like volume is logistics."

Lena could see Craig immediately, which again made the whole thing worse and more plausible. A big man in slides, bossing grief around because bossing was the only useful thing he knew how to do.

"Okay," she said. "Okay. Just breathe for one second."

He did, obediently. She heard the inhale scrape in, heard traffic under it, and watched the bartender cut another lemon while the blue cookie tin filled with sink water in her head anyway.

"You should go," he said. "I mean back to Philly. Not like go away, Jesus. I just mean don't sit there waiting on me while I do this. I hate that you're there by yourself."

The shame of being observed alone in a bar moved through her body again, clean and hot. She looked automatically at the tote occupying the stool beside her, the one she had kept empty for him. The bartender was wiping glasses. Nobody else cared. That almost made it worse.

"I've already been sitting here."

"I know." His voice gentled. That careful tone, curated or not, still found her every time. "I know exactly how shitty that is."

She pressed the sweating glass harder into the napkin until cold water slicked over her fingertips.

"Tell me what you need," she said.

On his end, a car door slammed and somebody shouted for Craig. When he came back to her, his breath had gone flatter.

"I don't need you doing that."

"I'm not asking if you want me to."

"Lena."

"What do you need?"

The line rasped against fabric. Voices moved around him.

"Right now? Charger. Coffee. Probably a room for her if we can't get the lock changed tonight. But I'm not asking you for money from a sidewalk while my life is a cartoon. I'm not that guy."

He said it with just enough disgust that she felt the old, stupid protective impulse rise in her chest immediately, fierce as a cough.

"I didn't say you were."

"No, but the universe did. Loudly."

In the mirror behind the bar she saw herself smiling despite the burn behind her eyes, smiling because he had made room for her embarrassment too, because he had turned his own into something shared. The knot inside her loosened.

"Take care of your sister," she said. "Text me when you're plugged in. If she needs a room, say that. Don't do the proud thing."

He let out a breath that sounded half wrecked, half relieved. "You're unreal."

"No," Lena said, and it came out rougher than she intended. "I'm in a bar by Union Station eating fries that taste like fryer oil from 1998. That's extremely real."

He laughed, genuinely this time or well enough that she could not tell the difference.

"God. I wish I was there."

"You were supposed to be."

"I know."

The grief in those two words was not hers, but it fit neatly enough over hers to warm it.

"Go," she said. "I'll wait to hear from you."

"Get yourself home safe first."

"Text me when you can."

"I will."

The line cut. Whether because his battery died or because he ended it, she had no way to know.

For a full minute Lena sat with the dead phone in her hand. The bartender cleared the soggy napkin from the stool beside her and set down a fresh one there, as if somebody might still claim the seat. Lena left her tote where it was.

She ordered another gin.

The bartender raised one eyebrow before pouring it. "That kind of night?"

"Apparently."

"You need a menu?"

"I need him to stop being in crisis."

"That's not on the menu," the bartender said. "Chicken tenders are."

Lena laughed, which hurt more than crying would have.

She did eat the tenders. She ate them because food made everything look less dramatic and because the bartender had been kind without making Lena explain herself. Halfway through, a cluster of conventioneers came in wearing matching lanyards and took over the high-top near the door. One of them, a woman with a flowered notebook and a smoker's rasp, spent ten minutes complaining about panel attendance before switching mid-sentence to the fact that her mother had started hiding money in soup cans again. The room filled and emptied around Lena while she waited for the next message and tried not to feel how precisely public her body was. Every time the door opened she looked up. Every time it was not him.

At 6:27 a message came through, then a second, then a blurry photo.

`Found a charger.`

`Noelle's okay. Shaky, furious, keeps saying she's stupid for giving him the spare key.`

The photo showed two black contractor bags, a cracked mirror leaning against a wall, and the white ceramic ears of what might once have been a lamp sticking out of a grocery crate. At the edge of the frame she could see dark wet dirt or plaster or cat litter, impossible to tell. The picture was bad enough to be convincing.

Lena zoomed until the pixels broke apart.

`You don't have to stay mad and nice at the same time btw.`

She typed three versions of her answer before sending one.

`I'm not staying nice out of charity. I'm pissed. I'm also not twelve. Keep helping her.`

He reacted with a heart before sending, `That was hot, unfortunately for my current circumstances.`

She laughed again, quieter this time. The room stayed bright and exposing around her, but now the story in her phone had a cracked mirror, rabbit ears, and a basil plant shoved into a Corolla cup holder.

By the time she paid her tab it was after seven and the paper tag in the dress had worked loose entirely, a small flag scratching her side every other step. The receipt was ugly: drinks, fries, tenders, tax, tip. She folded it once and shoved it into her wallet on top of the Marshalls receipt, one bad decision laid neatly over another. In the station bathroom she looked at herself under the fluorescent lights and saw a woman who had done too much to arrive here for the evening to count as minor inconvenience. Her lipstick had worn off at the center of her mouth. The concealer under her eyes had gone grainy. The dress still fit, but now it smelled faintly of fryer grease and tonic.

She touched the overnight bag. Toothbrush. Underwear. One condom. The small, awful museum of intentions.

Her train home was not for another forty minutes. She sat near the gate and watched families gather themselves into rows. A toddler in dinosaur pajamas ran at full speed into his father's legs and bounced. A young man in a Howard hoodie slept sitting up with both arms crossed over a cello case. Above them, the announcements kept arriving in that compressed station voice, all sibilance and metal. Delayed. Boarding. Final call. Words that moved bodies without ever having to care about them.

Eli sent a voice note while she waited.

"Okay. We got her to a place on New York Avenue that's ugly but has a lock and sheets. She's in the shower. I finally plugged in. I know today was a fucking disgrace on my end. I know it. Thank you for not making me defend every terrible minute of it while I'm still in it. Text me when you're on the train?"

He sounded exhausted. He sounded grateful. He sounded, most fatally, relieved by her in a way that let her imagine she still had power inside the wreck.

`Getting on now,` she wrote back. Then, before she could think too hard, `If the room cleaned you out, let me know. I'm serious.`

The typing bubble appeared so quickly it felt like his pulse.

`Don't.`

Then:

`Okay maybe let me see what damage is when I can think. I hate even writing that.`

Then:

`You should be here for good reasons, Lena. I wanted today to be good reasons.`

She boarded the train with that line inside her, protecting it as if it had weight.

***

By the time the train pulled into Philadelphia the sky had gone a hard dirty violet and then broken open. Rain sheeted across the platforms at 30th Street so thick the lights smeared in it. Lena stood under the awning with everybody else for two minutes telling herself she would wait it out, then gave up and ran with the rest of them toward the doors because there was no waiting out weather that committed this hard.

The hem of the dress drank everything the street threw at it. By the time she got down into the subway and then back up near home, her calves were freckled with grit and rain, the thin straps of her sandals had raised red lines across her feet, and the paper tag tucked inside the seam had gone soft as bread. The plastic fastener still held. The cardboard had started to bleed dark blue into the lining.

She let herself into the apartment and stood just inside the door listening to the fan. The room smelled like warm dust and old coffee grounds and the wet city she had carried in on her clothes. Her bed was unmade in the exact hopeful hurry she had left it that morning. The bathroom light was still on. She had a brief, crazed urge to strip the dress off and wad it into the trash so she would not have to look at evidence of how carefully she had arranged herself. Instead she set the tote down, sat on the bed without unzipping it, and called June.

June answered on the second ring. "You home?"

Lena had not even said hello yet. "Yeah."

"You sound like hell."

"I think I got stood up and then maybe didn't."

June breathed into the receiver once, thoughtful and sharp, as if she had set the sentence on a counter and was sorting the pieces.

"Do you want me to come over?"

The question loosened something in Lena immediately, almost humiliatingly. "If you're around."

"I'm around."

"You don't have to bring anything."

"I'm not coming empty-handed to a disaster. Open the downstairs door when I buzz."

June arrived twenty-three minutes later carrying a Wawa bag gone translucent at the bottom with grease and rain. She always knocked with the side of her fist, two quick raps like she expected doors to be stubborn. Her hair was damp at the temples, darkening the little silver streak she pretended not to have. One bootlace trailed untied, collecting water from the hallway. She smelled like wet concrete and cigarettes she had not yet gotten to smoke.

"Move," she said softly when Lena opened the door.

Lena moved.

June set the bag on the table and pulled out fries, two bottles of Diet Coke, a packet of aspirin, and a slice of chocolate cake in a clear clamshell crushed at one corner. "The cake got in a fight with my bag. It lost."

"You didn't have to get cake."

"I didn't get it for emotional reasons. It was there."

That was also, in its way, gentleness.

Lena sat back on the bed because standing made the room tilt. June took one look at her dress hem and clicked her tongue.

"You trying to return that?"

"I was."

June crouched and touched the soaked edge between two nicotine-stained fingers. "Not if the dye from the tag ran. Maybe if you steam it and lie with conviction."

Lena laughed once and then, because her body was cheaper than her mind, nearly cried instead.

"Okay," June said, straightening. Her voice went quieter, tidier. The joke left it. "Walk me through it."

Lena did. She started with the train because starting with the station felt too naked. She told June the time of the first text and the second and the call that went to voicemail and the bar and the bartender and the voice note and the photo and the room on New York Avenue. Under stress she could feel herself getting exact enough to be annoying, but she could not stop. "At 4:12 he said ten late. At 4:33 he said another ten. At 5:14 he said phone on fumes. At 5:41 he called. I know that sounds insane. I know I sound insane."

"You sound like somebody who got put in a bad position and keeps replaying the tape." June handed her a Diet Coke, already open. "Drink that."

Lena drank because being told helped.

June held out her hand for the phone. Lena gave it over. June read the messages without commentary, peeling the Diet Coke label into damp gray curls with her thumbnail. She zoomed in on the photo. She listened to the voice note once. Rain ticked at the window. The fan chopped the room into blunt little pulses of air.

"Well?" Lena said.

June set the phone down on the bed between them. "Well, he should've called before you were sitting there dressed and visible. That's one thing."

Lena flinched at visible.

"I'm not saying that to be a dick," June said. "I'm saying that's the part he owns, no matter what the sister situation is."

"So you think there is a sister situation."

June made a face. "I think the details are too stupid not to belong to somebody. Nobody invents the cookie tin unless they've seen a cookie tin."

That was exactly what Lena had thought, and hearing it from someone else felt like oxygen.

"Right?"

"Probably." June pushed a wet strand of hair behind her ear and looked back at the phone. "Also that kind of mess burns cash fast. Lock change, cab, motel, whatever. Bad nights invoice you."

"He said he wasn't asking."

"Maybe he won't. Maybe he just wants somebody to tell him he's not crazy. Those are different things until they aren't."

Lena pulled the Diet Coke away from her mouth. "You think he's gonna ask."

"I think you should know your answer before he does anything else."

Practical language. Systems, money, consequence. It was one of the reasons June was so often right about things before other people were right. Lena both relied on it and resented it. Tonight she wanted it.

"If he asks," Lena said slowly, "is that automatically bad?"

June looked at her over the bottle she had just opened for herself. "That's not the question."

"Then what's the question?"

"Can you afford to help without rewriting the story afterward so you don't have to feel stupid?"

That landed cleaner than comfort would have. Lena stared at the damp dark line drying across the floor where June's boot had dripped.

"I don't know."

"Okay. That's honest." June reached into the Wawa bag and shoved fries toward her. "Eat one thing with salt before you make any moral commitments."

Lena took a fry. It had gone limp in transit and still tasted good.

"I hate that I came back with this as the version of the day." She heard the rawness in her own voice and hated that too. "I packed a toothbrush. I packed underwear. I bought condoms. I bought a dress I literally left the tags on because I thought maybe if everything went great I could still be responsible about it after. Who does that?"

"You do," June said. Not cruel. Not kind. Just factual. "You make backup plans even for your fantasies. That's very on-brand."

Despite herself, Lena barked a laugh. "Fuck off."

"There she is."

Lena scrubbed at under one eye with the heel of her hand and came away with mascara grit. "If this was nothing, June, I don't actually know what to do with that."

June caught the crushed corner of the cake clamshell between thumb and forefinger and pressed until icing kissed the lid. When she answered, the joke had gone out of her voice.

"Then don't decide it's nothing tonight."

Lena took a sip of flat cola and let the fizz sting the back of her throat.

"You really think that?"

"I think tonight was humiliating. That's different from nothing." June looked back at the phone. "And I think if you cut it off this second because you got embarrassed in public, you'll never know which pain you're actually choosing."

Rain hammered the sill harder. Somewhere downstairs a television rose and fell through the pipes. Lena could smell the wet hem of the dress now, the train and the bar and city water drying into fabric. June's phone buzzed against the chair seat. She glanced down, swore softly, and stood.

"Mateo. If I don't call him back, he'll decide broken glass is a solvable problem."

"Go ahead."

June scooped up her cigarettes and phone. "I'm taking this on the landing. Better signal. Don't let that man text you into a mortgage while I'm gone."

"Fuck off."

"Working on it."

She stepped out and pulled the door almost shut behind her.

Alone with the rain and the fan, Lena stared at the damp dark line from June's boot drying across the floor.

The phone lit in her hand.

Eli.

Lena snatched it up too quickly.

`Noelle's asleep finally. I'm sitting on the floor because the chair looks like it has bedbugs. Good news is she kept the basil alive. Bad news is the room cost more than my dignity.`

Then:

`Ignore that. Gallows humor. Long day.`

Then:

`You offered on the train. I'm trying not to take you up on it.`

The typing bubble pulsed, disappeared, then came back.

`Can I ask you something ugly or should I wait till tomorrow`

Lena stared at the words until they blurred.

The phone buzzed again before she answered.

`I'm short $280 on Noelle's room for tonight. That's the whole ugly question. If the answer's no, tell me no. I mean that.`

June came back in with the cigarette still unlit between her fingers. One look at Lena's face and she set it on the table.

"He asked?"

Lena held out the screen.

June read it once. "Okay."

Two hundred eighty dollars. One night's room on New York Avenue, ugly sheets and tax included. Not an invented giant sum. Just a number that could belong to a real emergency or a practiced lie.

Lena opened her bank app. Her checking account sat at $912.67. Rent would hit next week. Her Visa minimum was due on the twenty-second. Taxes waited farther off with their patient open mouth. Two hundred eighty dollars would hurt. It would not destroy her. That was almost worse. Destruction would have made the choice clearer.

"Say no if you want to say no," June said.

"Do you think I should?"

June leaned back and exhaled through her nose. "I think if you send it, call it what it is in your own head. Tonight's room. Not proof of love. Not proof you're not a sucker."

"You make everything sound like a spreadsheet."

"Because money is one."

Lena looked at the dress pooled around her knees, at the darkened hem and the warped paper tag hidden in the seam. She thought of the empty stool beside her in Washington, the couple sharing crab dip, the bartender polishing pity out of her face, the overnight bag with its obscene little inventory of hope. The bank balance glowed. So did the blank amount field.

It hurt less to pay for the room than to let the whole day stop under that clock.

She typed, deleted, typed again.

`I'll send it. One night only. Don't turn this into a pattern.`

The answer came back before she had even switched apps.

`Understood. I don't deserve you being this decent to me tonight.`

The line made her exhale for the first time in minutes.

She sent the money through Cash App with the note `for noelle room tonight`. The green confirmation pulse appeared. $280 gone. The amount settled in her body with a blunt, familiar drop, like missing one stair in the dark and catching herself before the fall could become real damage.

June did not ask to see the confirmation screen. She only reached over and took the empty Coke bottle from Lena's hand before it tipped.

"Okay," June said. "Now take that dress off before it mildews on you."

"Romance."

"I'm a full-service operation."

Lena stood, peeled the damp fabric from her thighs, and winced when the softened tag brushed her side one last time. In the bathroom she found that the cardboard had bled a navy bruise into the lining exactly where her ribs had sat against it all day. Returnable no more. She hung the dress over the shower rod anyway. Water ticked from the hem into the tub. The overnight tote sat where she had dropped it. When she unzipped it, the condom foil had bent in half around her charger. She stared at it for a second, then zipped the bag again.

From the other room June was opening the cake container with the focused irritation she brought to cheap packaging, muttering at the plastic hinge like it had insulted her mother. It was such a June sound, practical annoyance turned intimate through repetition, that Lena nearly started crying for real.

Her phone chimed with a voice note.

She listened in the bathroom doorway.

"Hey," Eli said softly. He sounded tired enough to be stripped down to something true or something expertly adjacent to true. "I got it. She's asleep. The room's covered till checkout. I'm gonna sit here a little longer and make sure she stays that way. I know today was awful. I know I made it awful. Thank you for not turning me into the worst thing that's happened to you this month."

He paused. In the pause Lena could hear motel air-conditioning and, farther off, the high television murmur of some game show audience clapping on command.

"I'll make this up to you. Not with words. I'll make it up in person. I swear."

The note ended.

June looked up from the table. "What'd he say?"

Lena set the phone facedown on the sink. "That he'll make it up."

June nodded once, not smiling. "Then let him."

Lena went back to the bed in her underwear and one of her own old T-shirts, the one Eli had once convinced her was sexy because it looked stolen even though it had only ever been bought from a merch table online. June passed her a fork with a wedge of damaged cake balanced on it. Lena ate it. The icing tasted cheap and perfect.

Outside, rain kept working the block over. Inside, the fan chopped the air, June's boot dripped by the door, and the blue dress hung in the bathroom bleeding its return value into the tub. Lena scraped cake icing off the plastic fork with her teeth and listened for the phone even after it went quiet. Union Station had followed her home anyway, all that brightness and waiting still lodged in her body.

She plugged it in beside the bed and turned the ringer all the way up.


# Chapter 8

Lena stood on a chair in the middle of Moore Street, taping down the paper runner while her phone knocked against her thigh through the pocket of her shorts.

Below her, Moore Street had been turned into extension-cord country. Somebody from the parish had dragged out the folding tables with the old bingo-night legs that never locked straight. Somebody else had zip-tied orange sawhorses across both ends of the block and declared that official enough to stop traffic. Aluminum trays sweated onto card tables. Coolers bled through their seams. A little plaster Saint Rocco, protected by a cloudy plastic hood that made him look like he had been shipped through weather, waited on a plywood platform in front of the tailor shop downstairs from Lena's apartment. His dog looked cheerful in a way that bordered on insulting.

The block smelled like lighter fluid, charred peppers, and the first spoiled inch of ice water at the bottom of a beer tub. Kids were already running under the tables, treating the whole thing like a license to be under adults' feet without getting yelled at immediately.

Every time the phone moved, she told herself she would not look. The whole point of tonight, she had decided around noon and then re-decided around three, was to be on her own block like an actual person instead of a woman waiting for a vibration, instead of a woman trying to erase Union Station with fresh evidence.

The chair wobbled. June caught the back of her calf with one nicotine-rough hand and steadied it without looking up from the grill.

"If you crack your head open before the saint gets outside," June said, "I'm not writing the incident report."

She had a black Sharpie tucked behind one ear, a grease pencil in the apron pocket she had stolen from somewhere, and a tank top already darkened at the ribs with heat and smoke. People kept calling her name from three directions at once, and each answer seemed to pull the block tighter around her.

"Junie, where's the extra propane?"

"June, can you look at this outlet?"

"June, tell him he can't put the raw skewers next to the brownies, what the fuck."

June pointed the tongs at a teenager Lena did not know, then at the extension cord wound near the church donation jar, then at a dented milk crate that apparently contained salvation in the form of matches, zip ties, and two unclaimed phone chargers.

"Propane's behind the ice, outlet's dead because you're using it wrong, and if the brownies taste like cumin that's between you and God," she said. "Mateo. Milk crate."

Mateo came slouching over from the curb in a navy school hoodie he refused to retire even in September. The foam on one side of his headphones had split and was shedding black flecks onto the neck of the sweatshirt. He hooked the milk crate with one sneaker and dragged it over without taking the hood off.

"Love being conscripted into church labor," he said.

"You're not church labor," June said. "You're free labor."

"Cool distinction."

He said it flat, but he was already crouching to untangle the cords, long fingers moving faster than Lena would have expected from the posture. Sixteen lived in him the way static lived in an old radio. He looked half switched off until a thing interested him, then all the current went to the same place at once.

Across the street Gabe came through the barricade carrying a forty-pound bag of ice against his shoulder like it had personally offended him. One twin trotted ahead in sparkly jelly sandals with a paper crown slipping over one eye. The other had a crooked butterfly painted across her face and held three glow bracelets on one arm like inventory. Gabe's SEPTA T-shirt was dark under the arms; the strip of paler skin where his wedding band usually sat flashed every time he shifted the bag.

"Tell me this saint has permits," he called.

"He's grandfathered in," Lena said.

One twin pointed at the plaster dog. "Why does the dog get to come?"

"Because he's essential personnel," Gabe said.

The second twin looked at Lena with the cold seriousness children reserved for the truly important administrative failures of life. "Can we have soda before dinner if it's for church?"

"That's not how church works," Lena said.

"That's exactly how this block works," Gabe said.

Farah arrived ten minutes later with a canvas tote cutting into one hand and a plastic tub of ice balanced on the other hip. She always seemed to have just stepped out of work, even when she had clearly gone home and changed, because the smell of citrus peel and cork dust followed her like a personal weather system. Tonight she had her silver hair twisted up with a pencil and wore a men's Phillies windbreaker over black linen pants, as if she had no intention of trusting the forecast or the event.

She set the tub down by the beer cooler and looked inside.

"You people did all this and bought Lite," she said. "That's a moral failure."

"It was on sale," Lena said.

"So are cough drops."

Farah took a lemon out of her tote, tossed it once in her palm, and went hunting for a knife as if she had been put on earth to rescue every public gathering from itself at the level of garnish.

Lena's phone buzzed again. This time she looked.

Load-in ran long. I can call around seven if that still works. Send me the saint when he comes out.

The message was plain, all load-in and timing. Her body still took it in with the same quick stupid relief that had been wrecking her concentration for weeks. She thumbed the screen dark before Gabe or Farah or one of the children could clock her face doing that face.

June did clock it. June clocked almost everything.

"Seven's better," she said, flipping peppers with the speed of someone shuffling paperwork. "Father whoever doesn't start the walk till then anyway. Put him on when Rocco comes out. He'll get the whole disaster."

Lena looked at her. "You think?"

June gave her a quick sideways smile, already reaching for the long lighter. "Len. Half the point of a neighborhood ritual is making absent people look guilty. We can fold him in."

It was so practical the way she said it, like she was talking about where to set the potato salad so it would stay out of the sun. Lena felt some private knot ease under her ribs. She had spent the week since D.C. answering small questions about the trip in a voice she hoped passed for casual. Fine, he got caught up with his sister. It sucked. Tonight had been turning, in her head, into another test Eli could fail by sounding too polished or too eager or too much like a man who existed only when she held the phone. June made it sound simpler than that. He could just call. The block could happen around him. People could meet him in the shape he fit.

"Hear that?" Gabe said, passing behind her with the ice. "Your mystery man is finally on the schedule. Girls, be normal."

"We're normal," said the butterfly-faced twin, which was such a false statement that even Mateo laughed.

By six-thirty the street had thickened into itself. Aunts who did not live on the block anymore arrived carrying foil trays and opinions. Old men in folding chairs started policing the grill from fifteen feet away. Mrs. Alvarado, Lena's landlord, came downstairs in a rhinestone visor and informed everyone that nobody was to leave grease on the sidewalk in front of her building because rats had rights too, apparently. Kids melted water ice down their wrists. Somebody's Bluetooth speaker played old dance tracks until the church volunteer with the procession sash made them cut it when the saint was due out.

June moved through all of it as if she had eight hands and no resentment about using any of them. She got the dead outlet working by making Mateo run the cord through Mrs. Alvarado's front window instead. She reassigned the skewers when one tray started going dry. She sent Gabe to pick up another bag of charcoal and talked an old woman out of putting actual votive candles under a plastic folding table.

"I love her," Mrs. Alvarado said to Lena in a conspiratorial stage whisper, nodding at June. "That one can run FEMA."

Lena laughed because the alternative was to stand there too moved by something as stupid as that.

When the little procession party finally came down from the parish hall with Saint Rocco on his plywood platform, the whole block pinched narrower to make room. Four men took the corners, grunting more from performance than strain. Dollar bills fluttered from ribbons pinned to the plastic hood. The saint's dog came first around the hood's curve, then the saint himself, squeezing between a parked Honda and Mr. Ricci's pickup with about an inch to spare.

"Call him now," June said, and shoved the power bank into Lena's hand.

***

Lena hit video before she could give herself a reason not to. The screen jumped, then found Eli in a smear of backstage dark resolving into cinderblock, coiled cable, the line of his jaw shadowed with stubble. A black work shirt, sleeves pushed up. He was somewhere noisy but not too noisy. He always seemed to find the pocket of sound that made the illusion intimate.

"Hey," he said, smiling when her face appeared. "Tell me I didn't miss the dog."

"You missed the dog by three seconds," Lena said, and turned the phone outward.

"Absolutely unacceptable," he said. "I want a replay."

The nearer twin, the one with the crown falling off, grabbed Lena's wrist with sticky fingers and shouted directly at the screen.

"He has sores on his leg because he had plague."

Gabe barked a laugh. "Jesus Christ."

Eli smiled straight through the volume. "That's useful intel, thank you. And honestly more than Lena usually gives me."

"I'm right here."

"You're withholding saint facts."

The butterfly-faced twin shoved into frame to correct the record. "He has a dog because a dog brought him bread."

"That's a solid union story," Eli said.

The girls screamed like he had passed something official and crowded the screen harder, both of them talking at once.

"Show him the money," one of them demanded.

Lena walked with the crowd as the saint came past the tailor shop window, dollar bills flickering against the plastic. Farah leaned into frame over Lena's shoulder, lemon knife still in hand.

"So you're the traveling man," she said. "Your first useful contribution can be explaining this beer selection."

Eli took one look at the tub behind them. "My guess? Nobody wanted to admit how much ice costs."

Farah gave one short, surprised laugh through her nose. "All right. He knows arithmetic."

Gabe elbowed Lena hard enough to move her half a step. "Ask him if he's bringing better next year."

"I'm hearing next year already?" Eli said.

"Careful," Gabe said, crowding into frame with the toddler-sized solemnity of a man about to perform skepticism for his sister's benefit while also enjoying himself. "This block annexes people fast. My daughters already think you're in the television."

One twin corrected him. "Phone, Daddy."

"Same disease," Gabe said.

Eli looked at the girls, then back to Gabe. "That's fair. If I don't show up in person soon, I assume they get to repossess something."

"Your kneecaps," said the butterfly twin promptly.

"Reasonable. Keep the paperwork ready."

The girls dissolved. Even Mrs. Alvarado, who had drifted close enough to hear, laughed and fanned herself harder with the parish program.

The procession reached the middle of the block, where the sawhorse spacing got stupid. One of the men carrying the platform swore because the plastic hood scraped lightly against a side mirror. The whole crowd made the same sound at once, a neighborhood gasp that was mostly delighted to have a near-disaster to react to.

"That saint's brushing the Honda," Eli said. "This is either faith or terrible zoning."

This time Farah laughed out loud. Gabe did too. Even one of the old men in the folding chairs slapped his knee hard enough to spill beer on his sock. Lena had to press her lips together. The pleasure of it hit sharp and a little humiliating.

Mateo appeared at Lena's elbow with the portable speaker under one arm.

"Can your boyfriend tell these idiots to point this away from the brick?" he asked, not looking at the phone.

Eli tilted his head. "Let me hear it."

Mateo held the speaker up. The marching-band recording coming through it sounded like it had been packed in wet towels.

"Yeah," Eli said. "It's bouncing right back at the wall. Pull it out six inches and tilt it toward the street. Also that thing's too low."

Mateo gave him a suspicious glance, then did exactly that. The sound improved enough that even the old men noticed.

"Huh," Mateo said.

"I cherish your respect," Eli said.

"Don't push it."

But he said it with the faintest corner of a grin, and Lena saw June see it too from the grill, a quick unreadable flick of attention before she turned back to the skewers.

For the next twenty minutes the phone passed through the block like a borrowed relative. One twin tried to feed Eli half a hot dog through the camera and got mustard on the screen. Eli thanked her for the offering. Gabe asked him what kind of work actually kept a man in black T-shirts in rooms with cables all his life, and Eli said, "Mostly preventing rich people from discovering acoustics the expensive way."

Farah held still with the lemon knife flashing in her hand, shop face flat as a cooler lid. Gabe tipped his head like he was listening for the salesman under the joke. Even Lena heard the line land with a plastic shine on it.

Eli heard the air go thin and winced. "Sorry. That sounded like I sell beard oil out of a van. Mostly I tape down wires, haul speakers, and get yelled at for echoes in rooms some asshole built entirely out of brick. If I come next year, point me at the bad extension cords and let me earn a plate."

That got Gabe first. Farah's mouth twitched after that, then gave in. From his folding chair one of the old men shouted that nobody was touching his extension cords without a permit. Eli put a hand over his heart. "Sir, I'd never disrespect another man's illegal electrical theology."

Now the phone really started moving. When the girls started chanting over each other, he answered one and then the other before either of them could feel ignored. When Mrs. Alvarado launched into the history of the saint as if she had personally reviewed the plague, he listened long enough to make her feel heard and then said, "I'm going to shut up and watch, or she'll smite me through Lena's data plan." When the music started up again, he asked Lena to turn the camera outward so the block could drown him out for a minute.

By the time the saint finished the short solemn loop and got set back down near the tables, Gabe was giving him street history, Farah was still pretending the review remained open, and one of the twins had promoted him to Halloween.

June threaded through with a tray of skewers balanced on one forearm. "Move," she said to nobody and everybody.

Then, to Lena, with a glance at the battery percentage, "Take him while you eat, before this phone dies and he becomes a ghost again."

"Jesus," Lena said.

"What? It's a church event."

Lena took her plate and the phone and moved toward the stoop outside the tailor shop. Eli's face steadied there, no longer jumping with everybody else's arms. Behind him somebody shouted for a monitor check.

"You good?" he asked.

She looked back at the block. June at the grill with smoke turning her outline soft. Gabe crouched to wipe mustard off one twin's chin with a napkin already ruined. Farah slicing lemons into plastic cups because if she had to drink bad beer she was going to improve it by force. Mateo pretending not to help Mrs. Alvarado stack extra chairs and helping her anyway. The saint under his plastic hood, looking slightly smug.

"Yeah," Lena said. "I'm good."

"Looks like home."

The word landed harder than it should have. She covered it by biting into a skewer too hot in the middle and burning the roof of her mouth.

"Elegant," Eli said.

"Shut up."

"No chance."

She smiled at the plate so nobody across the block would see it and read too much into it, though that fear was already starting to feel old. The block had just spent the better part of half an hour reading him in anyway. Let them.

Farah wandered over long enough to steal one of Lena's peppers.

"He survived inspection," she said to Lena, low enough to stay between them. Then, to the phone: "Don't get cocky."

"I wouldn't dare," Eli said.

"Good answer."

She moved on. Lena watched her go, absurdly grateful. Farah made people earn approval with competence or truth, preferably both. Tonight he had managed enough of each to get waved through.

The night thickened after that in the way good block parties did, losing all visible structure while still hanging together on coolers and aunties. People ate standing up because the chairs were always claimed. Somebody produced a tray of store-bought cannoli and got immediately insulted for it. The girls ran until their jelly sandals slapped dust up onto the paper tablecloths. June ended up with a grill brush in one hand and a beer in the other, fielding three separate conversations without losing the thread of any of them. More than once Lena caught someone reaching for June's elbow before they reached for the problem itself.

Useful and loved, Lena thought, then felt strange about how much tenderness was in the thought.

She drank two bad beers because they were there and because the first one laid a little velvet over the edges of the block. She let Eli drift in and out as the night demanded it. He disappeared for ten minutes to handle something on his end and texted back exactly when he said he would. When he came back, he let the girls yell good night at him without dragging the exchange out. He told Gabe he would come see the block for real once the run broke. Mrs. Alvarado borrowed the phone long enough to warn him that parking on Moore Street could end marriages, and he said, "Then I'll come humble and circle twice." She handed the phone back with the same expression she gave contractors on their first decent day.

Lena stopped bracing for the line she would have to sand down before it reached somebody else. The block kept testing him before she had to.

When the real dark set in and somebody lit the strings of old festival bulbs across the middle of the block, June finally killed the grill. The saint had been returned to the parish hall. The paper runners were translucent with grease. Streetlight and bulb-light worked together badly on everyone's faces, flattening some people and making others glow. Kids began crashing in stages.

Gabe got one twin onto his shoulder and took the other by the hand, the paper crown now crushed into a cuff around her wrist.

"Tell phone man he's invited to Halloween if he brings his own chair," he said.

"Why his own chair?" Lena asked.

"Because I'm not losing another one to this family."

The butterfly twin pointed at the phone. "And candy."

"And candy," Gabe said.

Farah collected her tub with the severe competence of a woman who planned to leave no useful object behind for freeloaders.

"You heading up?" she asked Lena.

"In a minute."

Farah's eyes flicked to the phone, then back to Lena's face, measuring something and deciding not to make trouble out of it. "Drink water before you get romantic," she said.

"That's disgusting."

"You know I'm right."

She walked off toward the corner, windbreaker tied around her waist now, calling over her shoulder that she was taking the decent opener because nobody on this block deserved it.

Mateo hauled a trash bag toward the sawhorses with the air of a prison trustee. "This is all from one night," he muttered. "You people live like raccoons."

"You ate four skewers," June said.

"I'm talking structurally."

***

Lena stayed on the stoop when most of the crowd thinned. Her sandals had raised two bright dents across the tops of her feet, so she slipped them off and let the warm concrete take her soles. Somewhere farther up the avenue a siren stretched and folded away. The block, after hours of being louder than any one person, began separating back into units: front doors, voices on steps, a television through a screen, the drag of chairs over pavement.

Eli called again when he was properly free. The background behind him had changed. Now there was the inside of a van or maybe a loading bay, a strip of sodium light and the metal lip of a road case. He looked tired in the familiar way work carved into him, eyes shadowed, mouth a little slack from the last hour of a long day. Lena trusted that look because she had spent too long learning the shapes his face made when the day got hold of it.

"Hey," he said, softer this time. "You made it through church lawless."

"Barely."

"You sound like smoke."

She rubbed her fingertips over the front of her shirt, gritty with ash and paprika and some sticky sugar one of the girls had transferred onto her without permission. "I probably am smoke."

June was two stoops down coiling extension cords, the Sharpie still behind her ear like the night had marked her for management and refused to let go. Mateo was helping Mrs. Alvarado fold chairs, which he would deny if accused. Every so often June looked over, counting distance or volume or risk the way she counted propane tanks and dead outlets. Each glance steadied Lena for a second, then left her feeling exposed.

"You okay?" Eli asked. "You went quiet."

She looked at the emptied middle of the street where Saint Rocco had stood an hour ago, dollar bills fluttering under plastic like trapped moths. The dress from D.C. rose up in her mind with such stupid clarity she could have touched the paper tags still hidden under the seam. Union Station had left a public bruise. Tonight had laid a warm hand over it, enough pressure that she could breathe without prodding the sore spot every second.

"Yeah," she said. Then, because the bad beer had lowered the gates just enough and because the block around her had spent the evening making him ordinary, she added, "No. More than okay, actually."

He waited. That was one of the things he did best. He could hold a silence open for her.

"I needed this tonight," she said. "I know that sounds ridiculous."

"It doesn't."

"It does a little."

"A little, maybe."

She laughed. Across the street Mrs. Alvarado was giving Mateo directions on how to stack chairs like she was teaching him a lost liturgical art. June bent to pick up a zip tie, straightened, and held still with the cord looped around both forearms. Lena knew exactly how well voices carried between the stoop and the tailor window. The words came up anyway, right there in the open air.

"After D.C.," Lena said, more quietly, "I had this gross feeling that if I didn't get you into my actual life, all of it was going to start feeling fake around the edges. Like you were fine and I was the idiot generating the rest of it."

The call hit a tiny snag. Eli's face held for half a beat, one eye squinting in frozen grain, then caught up.

Lena's stomach dropped hard enough that the stoop seemed to tip with it. She almost laughed and said never mind. She almost reached for some smaller sentence, one that would not leave a mark.

Then his voice came back.

"I get that," he said. "I liked hearing your block. I liked hearing the girls bully me and your brother talk shit and whoever kept yelling about charcoal. It felt like I finally got to stand there with you."

The line carried a little cheese on it, sure, but it fit the ache she had been carrying around all week so exactly it opened her up. The neighborhood noise had thinned enough now that her own voice felt exposed on the stoop. Voices carried too well on Moore Street in warm weather. Everybody knew that. Mrs. Alvarado knew it. June knew it. Lena knew it and said the next thing anyway.

"I love you," she said.

The block absorbed it in a scatter of small sounds: June, two stoops down, stopped winding the cord for one clean beat; a folding chair leg scraped over pavement; blood rushed hot into Lena's face because the sentence was now in the world, rattling under streetlight and screen glare instead of staying in the safe bad wiring of her head.

The call lagged again, less than a second. It lasted long enough to hurt.

Then Eli said her name first, which helped.

"Lena."

She gripped the phone harder. The power bank cord brushed her wrist like a live thread.

"I love you too," he said. "And you don't have to sound like you're confessing through a screen door."

She laughed once, helplessly, because of the church line in it, because of the relief, because she had in fact sounded like somebody trying to confess behind a lattice.

"You heard that, huh?"

"A little."

"Fuck."

"No," he said, warm and immediate. "No, I loved it."

Behind June, Mateo looked over from the chairs with the blunt incuriosity of a sixteen-year-old who knew adults were always saying humiliating things into phones and preferred not to learn specifics. Mrs. Alvarado definitely looked over. That was unavoidable. Her visor caught the streetlight and flashed.

June came back up the sidewalk carrying the last loop of extension cord over one shoulder. She kept moving, pausing near the stoop only long enough to unplug the power bank from the wall and hand it to Lena. The cable dragged warm across Lena's wrist.

"Seven percent," June said. "And sound carries out here."

The words were quiet enough to pass for logistics. They still hit Lena low and mean, like somebody had hooked two fingers under her sternum and pulled. She turned her shoulder toward the street before she even knew she was doing it.

"Thanks," Lena said.

June gave one small nod and went inside the tailor's side door to return something to somebody's back room, leaving the smell of smoke and metal with her.

For a second Lena felt absurdly bare. Then Eli said, "You still there?"

"Yeah."

"Good."

After that he asked how much cleanup remained. He made her show him the street one more time, the abandoned cooler, the saint gone, the paper tablecloth snapping at the corners. He told her he wished he had been there to carry chairs and get judged by the women. She told him Mrs. Alvarado would have failed him on principle. He said he respected her standards.

When the battery warning finally flashed red, Lena ended the call before it could die on her. She sat another second with the dark screen reflecting only the block lights and her own face, thinner and more tired than she ever liked in a phone camera, but lit from somewhere real all the same.

June came back out with the empty tray from the brownies and set it on the table to dry.

"Well," she said.

Lena looked up. "Well what?"

June glanced toward the dark windows across the block. "Now they know how serious you are about him."

Heat climbed up Lena's face again, meaner this time. She looked automatically at Mrs. Alvarado's stoop, the tailor window, the second-floor curtains with fan-light behind them. On Moore Street, privacy survived only when gossip couldn't get a laugh out of it.

"Jesus," Lena said.

"I'm not trying to be an asshole," June said. "I'm telling you the block heard you."

Lena slid her feet back into her sandals too fast and scraped one toe against the strap. "I noticed."

June nodded once. "Take your shoes before somebody steals them."

Across the street Mrs. Alvarado called from her stoop with the full authority of a woman who had heard plenty.

"Tell your boyfriend next year he brings better beer."

Gabe, halfway to the corner with one sleeping child on his shoulder, lifted his free hand without turning around. "And candy."

From farther down, Farah's voice cut back through the dark. "And proof of decent parking habits."

The whole block, apparently, had revisions.

Lena picked up her sandals and stood barefoot on the warm sidewalk with the phone cooling in her hand. "I will," she called back before she had time to think about what kind of promise that was.

Mrs. Alvarado had already given him beer duty. Gabe had given him candy. Farah had given him parking. Nobody on Moore Street bothered assigning chores to a man they thought was imaginary. By the time June killed the last light string and Mateo dragged the sawhorses toward the curb, Eli had been entered into the neighborhood the only way the block trusted: as somebody people could mock, test, and expect to show up.


# Chapter 9

Eleven days before October 15, Lena had four browser tabs open and all of them were the same lie in different fonts: available balance.

Rain dragged itself down the window by her desk in slow gray ropes. The tailor downstairs had his radio on low and was arguing with somebody through the floorboards, one machine voice and one human one, both irritated. Her caption file sat half-finished on the monitor, a celebrity divorce hearing with clean subtitles and filthy money, but the words would not stay in order. Every time she tried to catch the next line, her eyes slid back to the yellow sticky note she had slapped on the lamp two weeks ago.

OCT 15 - 4200.

IRS and Pennsylvania, as if writing the names down would make them more like people she could bargain with.

Her checking account held $684.17. Her regular savings had $1,918.43. The credit-union subaccount she had nicknamed Q3 TAX still sat at $4,200.00, round and accusing. In the online bank, under the nickname ROSA, there was $5,071.86 left from the trailer sale, the part she kept pretending was not normal money. Winter money. Dentist money. A radiator-dies-in-January-and-the-landlord-finds-new-ways-to-be-deaf money. The original $18,600 had looked thick and adult when it hit, big enough to feel like a plan. By early October it had already become what all money became around her: rent, invoices that came late, the Visa she kept saying she would kill next month, SEPTA taps, groceries, three hundred and fifty dollars for a stolen piece of sound gear she had sent because Eli had sounded too proud to ask, a train to D.C., drinks in a bar where she had sat in a dress with the tags folded into her bag like a little paper tooth.

There were numbers she could move and numbers she had promised herself she would not. The promise had not been to anybody noble. It had been to the version of her that woke up at three in the morning and checked accounts in the dark.

Signal buzzed again.

Eli: You awake enough for something ugly?

Then, before she could answer:

Eli: Voice note. Don't listen while you're trying to type. I know how your face gets.

That made her smile in spite of herself, which was annoying. He had never seen her at the desk in person, but he had built a whole private geography of her anyway: the headphone dent in her hair after three hours, the coffee gone cold by her mouse, the way she pinched the bridge of her nose when somebody said the same stupid thing twice and she had to subtitle it both times.

She put the hearing on pause and listened.

"Hey. I'm sorry. I'm trying not to sound like a fucking scammer and that's making me sound more like one, so I'm just gonna say it plain. The payroll from the Wilkes gig still hasn't cleared. It was supposed to land yesterday. It didn't. Noelle's weekly place wants the rest by tomorrow morning or they put her stuff out, and I already fronted the storage unit and the locksmith because the ex took the keys when he smashed up the apartment, which is a sentence I still can't believe I'm saying out loud. I'm short thirty-one hundred. If the answer is no, it's no. I mean that. I just... I hate asking you with your tax shit coming up. I know what month we're in. Eat something before you answer me, okay? You get mean to yourself when you haven't eaten."

The note clicked off. Rain on the window. The little wet rasp of tires turning the corner on Moyamensing. Her own breath, too loud now.

Thirty-one hundred.

It was not the first time money had come into them, as Eli put it, but the earlier transfer had fit inside the story she preferred about herself. Somebody's van window got smashed after load-out, a piece of kit disappeared, she covered the gap, and he had made her feel repaid before any actual repayment had happened. It had been three hundred and fifty dollars and a little thrill. This was a request with a back and teeth. Thirty-one hundred was the kind of number that made the rest of her life get up and move over.

She listened again because dread liked rehearsal. On the second pass she heard the part that had gotten her on the first: I know what month we're in. Nobody else in her life said that about taxes except people who also owed them. Everybody else said, did you do your taxes yet, like they were homework or a doctor's appointment, one ugly day to get through. Eli said it like a season. A weather front. Something that sat on your chest for weeks.

She typed, deleted, typed again.

Lena: I'm up.

Lena: Let me look.

The three dots appeared almost immediately, then disappeared. He was trying not to crowd her. He was good at that.

She got out the narrow legal pad she used when app math started feeling too fake. The paper was soft from kitchen steam and being shoved under plates. She wrote the numbers down in pencil.

Checking 684.17
Savings 1918.43
Q3 Tax 4200.00
Rosa 5071.86

Then under it:

Rent 995 on the 5th
PECO 118.34
Internet 86.19
Visa min 93
Groceries / SEPTA / existing life

Thirty-one hundred sat on the page looking almost elegant. It would have been easier if the number had been messier. $2,947.16 would have felt like a real emergency. $3,100 had the broad clean face of a decision.

She did the subtraction once and then again slower. If she pretended ROSA did not exist, the way she usually did, she could not do it. If she counted ROSA but kept the mental line she had drawn under five thousand, the one that said winter starts when furnaces fail, she still could not do it without opening Q3 TAX. If she opened Q3 TAX, then the yellow sticky note over her lamp stopped being a reminder and became a threat.

Her phone lit again.

Eli: Also you don't owe me an answer fast. I just need the honest one.

Honest one. He always said things like that, words that let her think he was making room when he was also standing in it.

She went to the kitchen sink and opened the cabinet over the stove, where envelopes went to die. A stale wedding invitation. A SEPTA receipt furred with lint. Forty-two dollars in cash from a bottle-shop shift, folded around a coupon for seltzer. A MetroCard from the D.C. trip she had kept for no reason except self-punishment. In the junk drawer she found six dollars in quarters, two transit tokens from years ago, and a little velvet box that had once held earrings but now held nothing.

She set the coins on the table anyway, because poverty had its rituals and one of them was making the pathetic pile visible.

Her mother's ring knocked softly against the mug when she reached for coffee. She looked at her hand.

Rosa's signet ring was not delicate and had never pretended to be. Oval gold face, the R nearly rubbed smooth, one side nicked from decades of dishes and trailer cabinets and cigarette lighters and whatever else her mother banged her hands against while living. Lena had been wearing it since the funeral, except for one week that summer when her fingers swelled in the heat and she had put it on a dish by the bed because the skin beneath had gone tender and pale. She had put it back on two days later and felt steadier, which was embarrassing, but there it was.

She held the ring with her thumb. The metal was warm from her hand. There were things she owned that had belonged to Rosa, a blender that leaked from the base, three embroidered pillowcases, a box of costume jewelry that turned ears green, but the ring was the only thing that had stayed in motion. It had knocked against coffee mugs, keyboard keys, wine bottles. It had weight. It left a little crescent mark in avocados when she forgot it was on.

She looked at the legal pad again.

Thirty-one hundred.

The apartment felt smaller when she thought about saying no. The desk shoved against the window, the bed two steps away, the storage bins under the table, the air full of hot dust from the laptop fan even though it was raining now; all of it pressed in at once. She did not think he would stop loving her if she said no. That would have been easier to fight. What she thought was worse: he would understand. He would take care of her embarrassment for her. He would say, baby, of course, don't even worry, and then she would spend the next week imagining Noelle's boxes in an alley because Lena had decided October was a bad month to be generous.

She picked up the ring hand again and hated how quickly her mind had moved there. It had skipped right past should I and maybe and gone straight to mechanics.

The pawnshop on Passyunk, the one with the fading violin in the window and the neon OPEN that only lit up the O and the P. She had been inside once with Farah years ago when Farah needed a bridge week before an insurance check came through. Farah had sold two silver bracelets she called ugly even while her hand stayed on them too long. The place had smelled like old metal and lemon cleaner and a little despair, though maybe that last part had come from the people waiting.

Lena sat back down, typed before she could stop herself.

Lena: Give me an hour.

He answered with a heart and then, a second later, because he knew not to leave it at a heart when she was frightened:

Eli: Thank you for even looking.

That thank you landed on her like he already knew the answer.

She changed into jeans that were dry, found her rain jacket, and almost texted June. Her thumb hovered over the thread. June would know the name of a place that loaned on jewelry without acting like they were doing you a favor. June would also ask three questions too many, not because she wanted the truth but because she liked to hear how people justified themselves while they were still in the act. Lena did not have the energy for June's voice getting careful.

She put the phone away and went out.

October had gone from sticky to soaked in less than a week. The block smelled of wet cardboard, diesel, and the sweet rot from the bags the restaurant around the corner always left too close to the curb. Somebody had chalked a hopscotch grid on the sidewalk and the rain was carrying the numbers sideways. Lena walked fast, head down, her hood shifting with every gust. She passed Farah's shop and did not look in. The window was fogged from the inside and the handwritten ORANGE WINE tag she had lettered on Saturday had bled at the edges where some customer with a damp sleeve had smudged it.

At the corner she stopped under an awning and took the ring off her finger a quarter inch, just to see.

It did not want to go.

She twisted. The skin bunched. She swore softly and shoved it back down, the panic immediate and stupid, as if the ring were offended she had practiced.

The pawnshop door was heavier than she remembered. A bell announced her in a tired metal clack. The place was narrow, deeper than it should have been, with guitars hanging behind wire and a row of laptops in a glass case like sedated animals. A television in the upper corner played a daytime court show with the sound off. Behind the counter sat a woman in a maroon cardigan with one cuff pinned up by a safety pin. Her reading glasses hung on a beaded chain against her chest. A crossword lay open beside the register, blue pen crossing out more than filling in.

She looked up once, took in Lena's wet jacket and face, and did not bother with false brightness.

"Pawn or sell?"

"Pawn," Lena said.

"Good. Selling is where people get dramatic."

The woman held out her hand without introducing herself. Her thumbnail was painted silver glitter, chipped down to the middle. Lena set the ring in her palm, then had to take it back because it was still on her finger. The woman waited. No pity, no hurry.

"Bathroom?" Lena asked.

The woman reached under the counter and slid over a bottle of hand lotion instead. Cucumber melon, the kind every nail salon on earth had used in 2004 and apparently one pawnshop on Passyunk still did.

"Try that. Soap makes it swell worse if you're already pissed off."

Lena put a dab around the knuckle. The ring moved, then stuck, then moved again with a little obscene wet pop. Her finger underneath looked naked and undercooked, a pale groove pressed around it so sharply it seemed drawn on. She hated how fast the hand stopped feeling like hers.

The woman turned the ring under the lamp. The TV flashed a bailiff's silent outrage above her head. She peered through a loupe, then set the loupe down and wrote something on a yellow pad.

"Fourteen karat. Nice weight, but the face is worn. You want a pawn, I can do six twenty-five. Ninety days. Interest is what it is. You miss the window, it becomes my ugly ring."

"Six twenty-five?"

"For a pawn. I can offer more if you sell it, but you said pawn."

Lena heard herself say, "It was my mother's."

The woman looked up. For the first time something like a personal expression crossed her face, not softness exactly, more annoyance at the universe for being repetitive.

"Honey, if they weren't somebody's mother's, ex's, grandmother's, I wouldn't have half this inventory."

Then, less sharp:

"Pawn keeps the story open."

Lena laughed once, ugly and surprised. She wiped rain off her upper lip with the back of her hand.

"Fine," she said. "Do the six twenty-five."

The woman pushed a form toward her and tapped the line for ID. Her name, as it turned out from the little plastic badge half-hidden by the cardigan, was Sonia. Sonia counted the bills twice, licked her thumb between stacks, and slid the money across with the ticket.

"Keep that dry," Sonia said. "And don't wait till day eighty-nine if you want it back. Everybody waits till day eighty-nine and then starts telling me stories about miracles."

Lena folded the ticket so small it disappeared inside her wallet. The cash stayed hot from Sonia's hand. On her way out, she almost turned back and said actually never mind, actually give it back, actually I'm still a daughter before I'm whatever the hell this is. But the bell had already clacked once. Sonia had already gone back to her crossword. The hand that had worn Rosa all morning was empty now except for lotion smell.

On the sidewalk Lena tucked the bills into the inside pocket of her jacket and looked at her bare finger again. Rain dotted the skin where the ring should have been, collecting in the pale groove and then slipping off. She sent Eli a single message.

Lena: I'm on my way to the bank.

For a second there was nothing. Then:

Eli: Lena.

Eli: You don't have to destroy yourself for me.

She stood under the failing O and P of the OPEN sign and stared at that line until anger gave her enough heat to move. If he had meant it, he would not have asked. If he had not asked, she would not be standing in the rain with six hundred and twenty-five dollars in pawnshop cash and cucumber-melon lotion drying on the finger her mother used to rap against tables when she wanted everybody to shut up.

She put the phone away and kept walking.

* * *

Freedom Credit Union had the same depressed carpet it had when she opened her first account there at nineteen, a blue meant to hide salt and shame. The lobby smelled like wet umbrellas and printer toner. A fake ficus leaned in the corner with dust stuck permanently to the lower leaves. Lena stood in line behind a man arguing softly with his phone and a woman depositing rent money in an envelope already gone translucent at the edges.

The pawnshop cash sat in her jacket pocket like a fever.

By the time she reached the teller window she had gone past sick and into a kind of bookkeeping trance. She had already made the decisions in the notes app while walking over.

Keep ROSA above five thousand.
Keep enough in checking for groceries and transit.
Break Q3 TAX.

That last line looked cleaner on the phone than it did in her head. Breaking the tax money was not dramatic in a movie way. No sirens. No federal man bursting through the credit-union doors. It meant that on October 15 she would either pay less than she owed or float part of it on the card and call that strategy. It meant she had taken the only round number in her life and bitten a chunk out of it.

The teller was a young guy with a saint medal outside his shirt and a strip of blue athletic tape wrapped around his right pinkie. He kept flexing that finger without seeming to notice. His nameplate said MARCUS.

"What can I do for you?"

"I need to deposit cash and send a wire."

Marcus gave the faint blink people gave when you said wire instead of transfer, as if you had arrived from an older, sadder economy.

"Domestic?"

"Yeah."

"You'll need this." He slid the form through the slot and turned it so the boxes faced her. "There's a twenty-five dollar fee."

The fee made her almost laugh. Of course there was a fee. Every ugly thing in her life came with a smaller ugly thing clipped to it.

She filled in the form with Eli's bank information from the screenshot he had sent after her last message. Account number. Routing number. Name: Elias Ward. She had never needed his full legal name until a financial institution asked for it. Seeing it printed in her own hand gave it a squat documentary weight that made her stomach shift.

Amount: $3,100.00

Purpose of transfer:
