She wrote personal and hated the word immediately. Personal sounded like a lotion or a boundary problem. What she meant was this is the shape my humiliation takes today.

Marcus took the paper back and typed, his taped finger hovering over the keyboard like it had its own opinions.

"From checking?"

"Partly." She swallowed. "Can you move twelve hundred from Q3 TAX to checking first?"

"Sure."

He clicked through screens. "And the rest from savings?"

"Yeah. Savings and checking."

He did not ask why somebody had a subaccount named Q3 TAX and was emptying it for a personal wire. He did not ask why she was depositing six hundred and twenty-five dollars in twenties and fifties that smelled faintly of somebody else's hand cream. He asked for her ID, verified the address above the tailor shop, and then slid the screen around so she could confirm.

Checking transfer in: $1,200.00
Cash deposit: $625.00
Wire amount: $3,100.00
Fee: $25.00

Available after transaction: low enough that the exact figure looked vulgar.

Lena signed.

The pen was chained to the counter. The chain rattled when her hand shook, not a cinematic tremor, just enough to knock the plastic flower in the teller cup against the partition twice.

Marcus stamped three pieces of paper and tore one free for her.

"You're all set. It should hit same day unless their bank gets weird."

"Banks get weird," she said.

He smiled at that, quick and involuntary. "Yeah. They do."

She folded the receipt once, then again. Across the top it said WIRE TRANSFER RECEIPT in a font too cheerful for what it meant. Q3 TAX would now be three thousand even. The neatness of the number disgusted her. It looked planned.

Outside, the rain had gone finer, more mist than weather. She stood under the credit union awning because moving felt impossible for a minute. Through the glass she could see Marcus already bent over the next customer's problem, blue tape flashing when he counted cash.

She took a picture of the receipt and sent it.

Lena: done

The answer came before she could lower the phone.

Eli: Jesus, baby.

Then a voice note.

She should have waited. She knew that. She pressed play anyway.

"Okay. Okay. I got it. I got the notification. Come here for a second. Come here. I know, I know, that's stupid because I'm not there. I just need you close for one second. You saved my ass. You saved hers too. I'm gonna put this back where it belongs as fast as I can, you hear me? I mean that. And I know what that cost you. I know you didn't do that lightly. Take your shoulders down. Seriously. Unclench. Did you eat yet?"

His voice was low and urgent and relieved in exactly the ratio that would have made her weak even if there had not been a receipt in her hand proving she had paid to hear it. The rain ticked on the awning over her head. Somebody on the sidewalk laughed too loud, then coughed. A bus sighed at the corner.

Relief came first. That was the part that made her hate herself.

The relief was physical and quick, a drain opening somewhere behind her ribs. The waiting was over. The arithmetic was no longer hypothetical. Noelle, whether as disaster or sister or just a room full of boxes and a ruined lock, had been dealt with for today. Eli was not on the other end of the thread growing more desperate while Lena stared at columns and acted like numbers were morality. She had done the thing. There was nothing left to decide.

And braided right through that relief, inseparable from it, came the other feeling: the comprehension that the calm had an itemized cost. She had paid $3,125, counting the fee, and a ring her mother had worn through half Lena's life to make the sound in her body stop.

She leaned her forehead against the wet glass of the credit union window. It cooled her just enough to keep her from crying in public, though crying was not actually the pressure in her. What she felt was uglier and flatter. She felt as if she had swallowed a receipt.

Her phone buzzed again.

Eli: Say something so I know you're okay.

Another message, because he knew when not to demand too much at once:

Eli: Or don't. Just put your back against a wall for a minute.

She typed with both thumbs because her right hand wanted the ring and there was no ring to touch.

Lena: I'm fine.

He answered immediately.

Eli: You're not fine.

Eli: But you're okay with me. That's different.

That should have made her angry. Instead it made the back of her eyes sting. He could always find the sentence that left her shame intact and still made it feel held, as if he had reached into her chest and come back with the right wording already warm in his hand. Lena was not asking what to call that today. Today she was asking a smaller one: how to get from the credit union to her apartment without spending any more money.

She walked home because a bus fare now felt obscene.

The city had that rinsed October look she always liked when she was not frightened, brick gone darker, leaves pasted flat to the curb, steam lifting from the halal cart even in the drizzle. A guy outside the laundromat smoked under an umbrella and watched traffic as if it had personally offended him. Two schoolgirls in damp hoodies argued over an eyeliner pencil. A delivery cyclist nearly clipped her and shouted sorry without slowing down.

Everything ordinary stayed ordinary. That felt insulting.

At Farah's corner she almost turned in anyway, not to confess, just to stand by the register where the citrus peel bowl lived and hear Farah say something dry about distributors. The shop would smell like cork dust and wet cardboard and orange rinds. It would smell like a place where money moved in cases and bottles and not by invisible, humiliating leaps from one phone to another. But if Farah looked at her too closely Lena would talk, and if she talked she would either lie or tell the truth, and she did not want either one.

She kept going.

Upstairs, the apartment was exactly as she had left it: divorce-hearing captions frozen on the monitor, coffee cold enough to have a skin, legal pad open with the numbers still lined up like little gravestones. Her chair scraped loud on the floor when she sat. She opened the credit-union app again because self-harm came in many respectable forms.

Checking: $302.17
Savings: $1,000.43
Q3 TAX: $3,000.00
ROSA: $5,071.86

The balances looked fake in their own new way, as if the app had misunderstood her life and simplified it.

Three thousand in Q3 TAX meant she was now twelve hundred short before the deadline, and that was before rent, utilities, or the minimum on the Visa. The app did not show the ring. There was no line for mother's hand, temporarily converted to pawn ticket.

She opened the wallet and took the ticket out. Ninety days. Item description: LADIES 14K SIGNET RING, ENGRAVED, WORN FACE. Loan amount: $625.00.

Worn face.

She put the ticket under the yellow sticky note on the lamp. OCT 15 - 4200 stared down at it like a supervisor.

Signal chimed.

Eli was calling.

She let it ring once longer than necessary, out of meanness, or dignity, or because she wanted one second in which he needed her voice and had not yet gotten it. Then she answered.

"Hey," he said, and the relief in him was not performed so much as beautifully arranged. "You home?"

"Yeah."

"Sit down."

"I'm sitting."

"Good."

She heard movement on his end, a door closing, then the hollow room tone she had come to associate with backstage hallways and cinder-block stairwells. He had made spaces for her in his sound. That was one of the things that had gotten her. He never sounded like a generic wherever.

"I can't thank you enough," he said. "I know that's thin compared to what you just did."

"It is a little thin, yeah."

He took that without flinching. "Fair."

There was a pause, not dead air, a pause he knew how to fill without crowding. She could hear him breathing. Somewhere behind him a machine hummed, steady as a cheap fridge.

"Talk to me," he said softly.

Lena looked at the legal pad. The pencil lines had smudged where her sleeve hit them.

"I pawned my mother's ring."

The silence on the other end was brief and perfect and made her blood run cold for one strange instant, because it was exactly as long as a decent man would take to absorb that and no longer.

"Fuck," he said, quiet. "Lena."

There it was, the right amount of horror. Not too much, or she would have had to comfort him. Not too little, or she would have felt alone.

"You should be mad at me."

"I'm mad at the whole situation."

"That is not the same thing."

"No," he said. "It's not."

Rain tapped the window again. The tailor downstairs laughed suddenly at whatever was on his radio, a bark that came up through the floor. Lena rubbed the pale groove where the ring had sat and got lotion smell all over herself again. Cucumber melon and pawnshop.

"I told you not to destroy yourself for me," he said.

"You also asked."

"Yeah."

She waited for him to defend the ask, or justify Noelle, or say something about repayment schedules and payroll portals. Instead he said, "I know."

That was what he was good at. He knew innocence would insult her and explanation would cheapen the room, so he gave her recognition instead.

It worked on her even now. Maybe especially now. The part of her that had been braced all day eased a little more. She hated that too. She hated how responsive her body still was to being handled carefully.

"I'm gonna get this back to you," he said. "The money first. Then the ring. I don't care if I gotta sell half my kit."

The promise was so direct it should have felt suspicious. Instead it put a stupid pressure behind her eyes.

"Don't say half your kit like it's romantic."

His breath broke into a laugh, small and tired. "That is the least romantic phrase anybody has ever said to you, probably."

"Top ten."

"Okay. Then don't let me make it worse. Tell me what you need right now."

What she needed was for him to already be real in a way that could withstand the math. What she needed was to walk back to Passyunk and put Rosa's ring on again and feel the skin close around it like a decision had not just happened. What she needed was not to have felt that first hot wave of relief when his note came through, because that relief was proof of something she did not want to know about herself.

Instead she said, "I need to finish a caption file and pretend I didn't just do that."

"Then do the file," he said. "Put on the mean headphones. Drink fresh coffee, not the dead one on your desk. And text me when you want me, not when you think you should be okay enough."

Mean headphones. That was hers, from some offhand joke she had made in week two, and he returned it to her now with interest. She closed her eyes.

"Okay," she said.

"Lena."

"What."

"I love you."

The room did not change. Nothing cinematic happened. No thunder cracked. The rain stayed rain. The paused celebrity hearing stayed frozen on a woman with perfect blowout hair and a caption that read YOU MOVED THE MONEY WITHOUT TELLING ME. Down on the street somebody leaned on a horn and then let it go.

But Lena heard the sentence as if it had weight, as if it had landed on the table with the legal pad and the pawn ticket and the yellow note.

She had said something close enough at Saint Rocco night that the distinction no longer mattered. He was only answering now, or formalizing, or using a word that had already been doing the work for weeks. Even so, she felt the syllables in her body like another debit.

"I know," she said, because saying it back right then would have felt less honest than the money had.

He did not punish her for that either.

"Okay," he said again, easy, warm, as if I know were a tender answer and not a dodge. "Text me later."

When the call ended, the apartment got louder. Rain. Fridge. The tailor's radio. The tiny tap of her fingernail against the desk where the ring should have made a thicker sound.

She reheated the coffee in the microwave and it came out tasting burned and metallic, but she drank it anyway. Then she put on her actual mean headphones, the over-ear pair with one side taped, and tried to return to the divorce hearing. The first line she captioned after all of it was a man saying, under oath, that he had only moved funds temporarily.

She stared at the sentence until the words blurred.

Then she laughed, once, hard enough to feel it in her nose, because if the room was going to insult her that openly it could at least have the decency to be funny.

She corrected the timestamp, typed the line clean, and kept going.

Every few minutes her thumb found the groove in her finger and came away with nothing. Every few minutes her eyes slid to the lamp, where the pawn ticket sat under OCT 15 - 4200 like a second deadline she had made for herself.

By evening the rain had thickened again. The window filled with water. The apartment dimmed early. Lena worked until the captions moved on instinct and the numbers in her head became a lower, steadier ache.

By then she no longer needed the legal pad to do the math. Twelve hundred missing from October 15. Ninety days on the pawn ticket. Twenty-five dollars gone just to push the money through. His name kept showing up with those numbers now, sliding in beside rent and the Visa minimum whether she wanted it there or not.

That did not thin the love. It only dirtied it, which was worse.

Around eight, Signal lit the desk.

Eli: You fed me and saved me in the same day. That's a lot of woman.

It was exactly the kind of line that would have melted her yesterday. Tonight it did something more complicated and more final. It warmed her. It disgusted her. It made her put the phone face down and hold the bare finger against her mouth until the cucumber-melon smell had finally gone.

At nine she left the laptop open on the desk, washed the mug, and put the legal pad in the drawer with her passport, the Wildwood paperwork, and the envelopes she used for taxes. She left the pawn ticket under the lamp. She wanted to see it when she woke up. She did not trust any version of herself that would have tried to tidy this away overnight.

Near midnight, another message came through: a picture of coiled cable on a concrete floor, the toe of his boot in one corner.

Eli: load-out's a mess. i'll call when i can.

In bed, she left one earbud in. The bare finger kept brushing the sheet as if looking for its usual resistance. The absence had texture. It snagged her attention every time she turned over. She stayed awake longer than she meant to, stupidly alert for the phone, until sleep took her with the lamp still on and the half-done caption file still open on the desk.


# Chapter 10

The rain started before dawn and kept finding new surfaces to worry at. It ticked on Lena's window unit, hissed in the alley behind the tailor shop, ran down the brick across from her in long black seams that looked, half asleep, like somebody had put the whole block under subtitles and then let them blur.

Her phone lit at 8:41 on the pillow beside her face. Three missed calls. Two emails. One text from Eli sent at 2:13 a.m.

`sorry baby. hit a wall. didn't mean to vanish on you`

She had fallen asleep with one earbud still in, waiting for the call he'd said he could make after load-out. She remembered his last message before that, a picture of coiled cable on a concrete floor and the toe of his boot, and then nothing. She had lain awake anyway, stupidly alert, the body still keeping time for somebody who was not there. When sleep finally took her it had done it all at once, face mashed into the pillow, lamp still on, the half-done caption file open on the desk by the window.

The missed calls were from Nora Patel at BlueArc Learning. The first voicemail had come in clipped and courteous. The second had lost the courtesy.

Lena sat up too fast, the earbud falling into the blanket, and opened the newest email.

Subject: URGENT - Reel 4 export issue

She did not need to finish reading the message to know she had fucked herself. The client had uploaded the training package before final QA because they were behind and trusted her. Reel 4 was offset almost two seconds after minute eighteen. Speaker IDs had slipped. Worst of all, one caption in the warehouse safety module now read:

Do stand beneath a suspended load.

Lena had dropped the `not`.

It sat there on the screen with obscene confidence, a tiny missing word with a whole skull under it.

She got out of bed and crossed the four steps to the desk. The apartment smelled like damp dust and old coffee. Yesterday's jeans hung over the chairback, still wet at the cuffs from the walk home. Her keyboard had a crescent of bread crumbs in it. She rubbed at the pale band on her right hand where her mother's ring had been for years and then wasn't.

Nora's second email was already there beneath the first.

We pulled the file from the portal. Please confirm immediate correction. Rush bonus removed and QA recovery hours deducted from invoice. We are moving next Thursday's live webinar to another captioner.

Revised payment: $412.

The file had been worth $585.

One hundred seventy-three dollars gone because she had stayed awake for a man with a beautiful voice and then slept through a client's call like a college freshman with pink-eye and no alarm clock. She stared at the revised number until it stopped looking like money and started looking like accusation.

There were nine days until October fifteenth. Four thousand two hundred still owed between federal and state. Three thousand one hundred living on the Visa statement like mold. The trailer money was already bitten into. The ring was gone. Another Wednesday live webinar was gone too, which was worse than the docked invoice because BlueArc's live work paid fast and never all at once enough, which made it useful.

Her phone buzzed again. Eli.

`You awake now? Want me to call?`

Lena typed `rough morning` and deleted it. She typed `I missed a client call because I was waiting for you to maybe love me in real time` and deleted that too. What she sent was:

`fixing work shit. talk later`

Three dots appeared, disappeared, appeared again. Then nothing.

She opened the caption software, loaded the project, and put her headphones on. The waveform rolled out across the monitor in neat blue hills. The instructor in the video was a cheerful man in an orange vest standing in a warehouse aisle, wagging a hand at a camera nobody would remember. Lena watched his mouth move and heard the disaster she had sent into the world. The captions followed him drunk and haunted. At 00:18:42 the line hit exactly where it should have and still said the wrong thing.

Do stand beneath a suspended load.

"Jesus Christ," she said to the empty room.

Rainwater tapped the metal lip of the window unit in a rhythm almost but not quite like a notification. She fixed the missing word, retimed the drift in Reel 4, corrected a speaker ID she'd somehow slid from Narrator to Supervisor and back again, exported, checked, exported again. Every minute she wasn't looking at her phone she imagined it lighting. Every minute she looked, it stayed dark.

At 9:26 Nora finally called back. Lena answered before the second ring.

"Hi, Nora. I'm so sorry."

Nora did not waste time telling her not to apologize. "I need the corrected package in twenty minutes and I need to know this isn't where your bandwidth is right now."

Lena looked at the room around her, at the damp socks on the floor, the uncovered mug, the tax envelope under the lamp. "It isn't," she lied.

"Okay. Because the client is already jumpy. That safety line was bad."

"I know."

"And the missed call didn't help."

Lena swallowed. The rain on the window got louder for a second, as if the block had leaned in. "I know."

Nora exhaled through her nose. She did not sound cruel. She sounded worse than cruel: tired. "Send it. Then take the rest of the day and get yourself right. I like your work. I don't like scrambling around your work."

"You won't have to."

"Let's both hope that's true."

When the call ended Lena sat very still for a beat, the headphones pressing warm dents above her ears. She delivered the corrected package at 9:43. At 10:11 Nora confirmed receipt with a terse `Got it.` No smiley, no thanks, no note about next week except the one already made.

She opened her spreadsheet and moved numbers around the way people in bad marriages moved furniture. If Farah could give her Saturday and maybe one emergency shift this week, that was another hundred forty or so. If she paid only the card minimum and let the interest chew. If BlueArc still released the $412 by Monday. If Eli paid back any of it soon, which was a sentence she did not let finish itself in her head because it sounded too much like the kind of sentence women in her family ended up saying in kitchens with broken linoleum.

Her phone buzzed at 10:19.

`shit. i'm sorry you're getting hit from all sides`

Then, a minute later:

`Take a breath for me. Eat something?`

It was exactly the kind of thing that had once made her chest open. This morning it just felt like one more task arriving through glass. She was hungry, yes. She also needed twelve hundred dollars to stop October from getting personal.

By noon the rain had settled in for the day. The block below her window looked half erased, umbrellas moving like dark punctuation. Lena texted Farah to ask if she needed help at the shop.

Farah answered in under a minute.

`Three to close if you're not dead. If you're dead I still need three to close but standards change`

Lena went to shower. The water in the old pipes shuddered before it came through. She stood under it longer than she should have, letting it hit the back of her neck while the apartment cooled around her in damp degrees. When she got out, Eli still had not answered the `later` she had sent.

She put on dry jeans, a sweatshirt that smelled faintly of cork dust from the shop, and walked out into the rain with her hood up and her phone zipped into the inside pocket of her bag like a thing she didn't trust herself around.

***

Farah's bottle shop always smelled more alive in bad weather. Cork dust and wet cardboard, yes, but also cut citrus, concrete, the sharp mineral stink of white wine somebody had mopped and not fully won against. A line of umbrellas dripped by the door. Condensation filmed the cooler glass. Somebody had tracked in a braid of wet leaves that kept sticking to the mat every time it opened.

Farah was behind the register in a faded forest-green sweater, sleeves shoved to the elbows, silver showing clean at her temples. She had a deli container of orange peels open beside the card reader and was dropping the clean strips into it one by one as she worked, saving them for the syrup she made every winter while complaining that everybody on the block wanted sugar and nobody wanted tannin.

She looked up once when Lena came in and took her in fast. "You look like hell."

"Love that for me."

"I didn't say ugly. I said hell. Take the Slovenian orange to aisle three before Mr. Cardenas decides to die waiting."

Lena laughed because that was the price of entry with Farah: you had to keep the joke moving or earn concern. She shrugged out of her wet jacket, hung it in the back, and started hauling the case across the narrow floor. Her forearms already felt full of old static from the morning. The cardboard softened under her hands where the rain had found the seam.

The afternoon came at her in little neighborhood pieces. Mr. Cardenas wanted the cheap Montepulciano but called it the red with the saint on it. A younger woman in scrubs stood in front of the canned cocktails for five whole minutes and bought the one with a cartoon shark because she said it looked honest. Lena rang up a six-pack as a single and had to void it. She reached for the wrong shelf tag twice. Every time the phone in her apron buzzed, heat moved through her ribs before she even checked whether it was Eli.

At four-thirty Farah came around the counter carrying two paper cups of over-steeped black tea and jerked her chin toward the back room.

"Five minutes."

"I'm fine."

"No, you're vertical. Different category."

They stood in the back by the stacked cases and the old hand truck with one wheel that always complained. Rain drummed on the alley door. Somebody in the shop laughed too loud at their own joke and then apologized to nobody. Farah handed Lena a cup and leaned against the counter that held the pricing gun, the broom, and a bowl of clementines with soft spots.

"What's the damage."

Lena wrapped both hands around the tea. "Work."

"Work is not damage. Work is weather. Damage is what weather does to your roof."

"Then my roof."

Farah watched her over the rim of her cup. Her eyes were dark and dry and not interested in theater. "You want to be cryptic, go write songs. I'm asking plain. Is this tax-face, family-face, or man-face."

Lena looked down at the tea. The surface trembled with the building every time the front door opened. "Mostly work. I blew a file this morning. Lost money."

"How much."

"A hundred seventy-three and a webinar."

Farah hissed once through her teeth. "Fuck."

"Yeah."

"That because you were tired or because you were being stupid."

The question landed exactly where it was aimed. Lena let out a small laugh that wanted to become something else. "Can it be both."

"Usually is."

Farah set her cup down. "Did this guy keep you up."

There it was. It was not an accusation. Farah was taking inventory.

Lena could have told the truth in a dozen partial ways. That Eli had said he would call and then hadn't. That she'd still waited. That the waiting itself had started to feel like a second shift, unpaid and somehow more compulsory than the one that bought groceries. That she had already sent him money she no longer had. That she had started timing her body around pings.

What she said was, "He's got a lot going on."

Farah made a face. "Everybody's got a lot going on. I've got distributor reps trying to sell me pumpkin-spice prosecco like they're not ashamed to have tongues. That doesn't mean I get to ruin your Tuesday."

"Wednesday."

"See. This is what I mean. You're down in the screws."

Lena smiled despite herself. "I fucked up. It isn't all him."

"I didn't say all." Farah folded one orange peel in half before dropping it into the container. "I'm asking whether this man makes your life bigger or just busier."

Lena looked at the damp concrete. There was a flattened wine label stuck near the drain, one of the expensive Burgundies Farah cursed under her breath whenever customers asked for something smooth under fifteen dollars. "Busy week," she said.

Farah let that sit. She was good at silence when she wanted something from it. Finally she said, "Okay. Busy week. Useful options. You can take more shifts if I've got them. You can ask me for an advance and I can say yes or no like adults. You can tell me to mind my business and I'll probably mind seventy percent of it. What you can't do is start moving through the shop like a ghost and think nobody notices."

Lena held the tea tighter until it almost hurt. "I don't need an advance."

"I didn't ask what would flatter you."

"Farah."

"Len."

For a second she thought she might say enough to stop carrying it alone. She could not say everything, because everything would change the room. The first transfer. The second. The ring. The weird hollow way Eli's tenderness now arrived right on time and somehow late. She imagined the words in Farah's back room among the broom handles and extra invoices and the case of bargain cava nobody wanted, and she could not bear the sound they would make once out in the air.

So she took the mercy Farah had left for her.

"It's taxes and work," she said. "And rain. That's the whole glamorous story."

Farah studied her face one last time. Then she picked up her tea. "Fine. Then take home the lentil soup in the fridge before it turns into a science fair, and when your phone buzzes out there maybe don't look like it just called you ugly."

"That's very specific advice."

"I own a retail business. Specificity's all I've got."

They went back out front. The shop was busier now, people buying midweek consolation in screw-tops and six-packs. Lena moved through the rest of the shift fast enough to be useful. She lifted cases, rang people up, wiped the neck of a bottle a kid had knocked from the cold shelf without breaking. She was good at work when she was in it. That was part of the humiliation. She had built a life out of being dependable in rooms where nobody said thank you beautifully. She had let a voice on a phone make her sloppy inside the only competence she could reliably sell.

At close Farah shoved a quart container into her tote.

"Soup. Eat it."

"Yes, chef."

"Don't call me that. I sell pinot grigio to men in Eagles jackets."

Lena zipped the tote and pulled on her damp jacket. Farah was already at the register, counting twenties with the same expression she used for grief and inventory. Lena stood there half a second too long.

"What," Farah said without looking up.

"Nothing."

"Right. Go home before I start being kind in a way you'll hate."

Lena went out into the rain with the soup knocking against her thigh and her phone still unhelpfully silent in the pocket of her sweatshirt.

***

June's basement apartment smelled like wet concrete, cigarettes, and burnt toast when Lena came down the steps that evening. Water had gathered in the crack by the bottom stair and turned the landing slick. Somebody upstairs was running a vacuum in the slow brutal lines of a person who had given up on tidiness and was settling for revenge.

Mateo sat at the folding table in the main room with his school hoodie on and his beat-up headphones in his lap. The foam on one side had split clean down the middle. He was wrapping it with black electrical tape so neatly it looked factory, head bent, one knee jiggling under the table. A district Chromebook glowed in front of him beside a mug full of pens and a spoon standing in a puddle of instant ramen broth.

He glanced up once. "She's in a mood."

"Helpful," Lena said.

"I know."

June's voice came from the kitchenette sharp and low, the voice she used when somebody on the other end had already exhausted the free version of her patience.

"No, I heard you the first time. I'm saying the form was turned in. I'm looking at the copy right now. If your office lost it, that's not suddenly a character-building exercise for me."

She came around the corner with her phone pinned to her ear and a cigarette unlit between two nicotine-stained fingers. She wore black work pants, unlaced boots, and an old gray T-shirt with a faded Sixers logo cracked across the chest. Her hair was clipped up badly. On the little desk by the wall, three laptops were open: one with a KeyNest dashboard full of maintenance tickets and little red timers counting response windows down, one asleep, one awake and face-angled away so Lena could only see shifting blocks of text and a soft pulse of notifications in the corner.

June saw Lena, lifted one finger, and kept talking.

"Then email me the policy. No, not tomorrow. Tonight. If you're gonna say 'district policy' like God came down and wrote it in toner, you can at least send the PDF."

She hung up before the other person could perform their last line, tossed the phone onto the couch, and looked at Mateo.

"If I find out you handed that script to anybody besides the one dumbass who asked, I'm leaving you at school."

Mateo did not look up from the headphones. "He said it was just for the bibliography formatting."

"And then half the class had it."

"That's not my fault. That's distribution."

"Jesus Christ."

Only then did June look properly at Lena. The irritation in her face loosened, not all the way. "Hey. Sorry. Come in before the stairs sue you."

Lena held up the container from Farah. "Peace offering."

"Soup from the queen herself." June took it and peered through the lid. "Lentils. She thinks I'm iron-deficient."

"Are you."

"Who isn't."

The apartment had the particular clutter of a place where usefulness beat beauty every time. Extension cords looped from an overloaded strip. A coffee mug held loose screws and dead lighters together like they were the same species. An envelope from Mr. DeLuca sat under the ashtray on the windowsill with FINAL NOTICE stamped in red that had bled slightly from damp fingers. The old dehumidifier in the corner groaned and clicked.

Lena nodded toward the Chromebook. "Bibliography formatting."

Mateo finally looked at her, acne bright on his forehead, headphones still in his hands. "It was a little script. Their assignment was to fix citation spacing by hand in a spreadsheet. That's not learning. That's hazing."

"And now the vice principal thinks he's running a criminal syndicate," June said. "Tea."

"I made the citations right."

"Not helping yourself."

Lena almost smiled. Mateo had that teenage way of sounding half absent until the subject mattered and then suddenly precise enough to be annoying. He went back to the headphones, jaw tight.

June took a pack of cigarettes from the counter, thought better of it, and shoved it back. "You want coffee? Tea? I've got exactly one beer and I'm saving it for when Mr. DeLuca dies."

"I'm okay."

"You look bad."

"You're really getting flattering in your old age."

"No time for lying tonight."

Something on the desk chimed twice. June's eyes flicked there and away. She crossed the room in three quick steps, touched the keyboard of the angled laptop, and shut the sound off.

Mateo said without looking up, "That thing's been doing that all day."

"Then I'm glad we solved it," June said.

To Lena she sounded not secretive exactly. Just overfull. Like there were too many tabs open behind her face.

June sat on the arm of the couch and scrubbed her hands over her eyes. Without the joke running in her, tiredness made her face go blunt and unreadable. "What happened."

Lena leaned against the doorway instead of sitting. It made leaving easier if she needed it. "I blew a file this morning. Lost money."

June dropped her hands. "How much."

"A hundred seventy-three and a live webinar."

"Shit."

"Yeah."

"BlueArc?"

"Yeah."

June whistled once under her breath. "They're pricks when they get spooked."

"I gave them a reason."

"Still."

Mateo snorted softly at something on his screen, then stopped when June looked over.

Lena said, "I was up late."

June's eyes came back to her face. "With him."

Lena shrugged. "Waiting for him, mostly."

June leaned forward, elbows on her knees. The ashtray shifted on the sill when the upstairs neighbors thumped overhead. "Okay. Do you want me to be nice or useful."

"Dealer's choice."

"Useful. You're tired and you had a shit workday, which means everything with a pulse is gonna feel personal. That doesn't automatically mean there's a problem."

Lena looked at the angled laptop, then back at June. "He's been weird."

June's expression changed by maybe half an inch. "How."

"Not weird weird. Just slower. Like he'll say he can call and then I wait around and he can't. Or he answers, but it feels..." She stopped. Stress always made her want to overexplain the timeline until the timeline itself sounded insane. "Off by a beat. I don't know."

June rested her chin on her fist. "Len. The guy's been doing load-ins, his sister's still a mess, and every person who gets tagged as competent ends up as free labor for their whole extended bloodline. He doesn't sound weird. He sounds tired. There's a difference."

"I know tired."

"So do I." June flicked her fingers toward the room: Mateo, the notices, the ticking timers on the KeyNest screen. "Sometimes all tired looks like from the outside is less sparkle."

There was no cruelty in it. That made it harder to argue with.

Lena said, "I just keep feeling like I'm waiting to be resumed."

June's mouth tightened in sympathy or something close enough. "Then stop standing there on pause for him."

"Easy for you to say."

"No, actually, easy for me to say is the whole point of friendship." June stood, went to the stove, found two bowls, and started ladling Farah's soup as if she had been given a task and needed movement more than appetite. "Eat something. Take a shower if you haven't. Don't interrogate him because you had a garbage day and now your skin is on inside out. Let him come back toward you."

Lena watched her. "You make that sound simple."

"It's not simple. It's just better than turning yourself into customer service for a relationship."

Mateo let out a laugh he tried to hide in his shoulder.

June pointed the soup spoon at him. "You don't even have a relationship. You have malware and opinions."

"That script was elegant."

"You're about to elegantly email an apology to the vice principal."

The old laptop on the desk lit again, silent now. Just a pulse. Then another. June saw it and ignored it too carefully.

Lena took the bowl June handed her. The lentils smelled like cumin and lemon and Farah's whole theory of mercy. "How bad is DeLuca."

June rolled her eyes toward the windowsill. "He wants the back eighteen hundred and now he's talking about another hundred twenty-five starting November because the upstairs washer died and apparently my rent is a mood ring for his appliances."

"Jesus."

"Yeah." June blew on the soup. "KeyNest keeps cutting response windows too. Seven minutes, ten minutes, like tenants only lock themselves out when I'm already sitting upright in a clean room with my shoes on. Everybody wants machine speed and human warmth. Pick one."

She said it bitterly enough that Lena almost apologized for bringing her one more need. Instead she stood there with the hot bowl in her hands and understood, dimly, that other people's lives had kept happening while she was staring at a screen waiting to be chosen by hers.

June softened first. She had a way of doing that, dropping just enough sympathy into the room that Lena felt mean for wanting to push any harder.

"Listen," she said. "If he calls tonight and still feels off, then maybe ask for a date and a plan. A real one. But don't go looking for blood because you had one bad Wednesday. You'll find what you came for."

Lena nodded. The advice sounded sane enough to live inside.

As she left, Mateo finally looked up from his apology email. "For what it's worth," he said, not quite meeting her eyes, "people get laggy when they're doing too many things at once."

June cut in before Lena could answer. "Great. He invented human behavior at sixteen."

Mateo shrugged and went back to typing.

Outside, the rain had thickened from steady to committed. Lena pulled her hood up and walked home through water that had found the low parts of every cracked sidewalk square. Her phone stayed quiet all the way back.

***

Eli called at 12:37 a.m.

Lena had done almost everything June told her to do, which annoyed her by working. She had showered. She had eaten the rest of the soup with a heel of bread standing over the sink. She had answered one email from Nora with exactly the amount of humility required and no extra. She had not texted Eli after coming home. She had even put the phone face down while she folded laundry off the chair and gave up halfway through because folding one person's laundry at midnight felt like parody.

When the screen finally lit, her stomach still went quick and stupid anyway.

She took the call on video before she could talk herself out of it. Her room was dark except for the streetlight leaking through wet glass, which left her face mostly a suggestion in the corner of the screen. On his side, a cinder-block wall went muddy under bad light. Rain rattled the window unit hard enough now and then to make the whole frame tick.

"Hey," Eli said, and then again, softer, "hey."

He had a way of breathing in through his nose before he said her name, a little warm sound like somebody stepping into bathwater. "Lena."

"Hi."

"You awake or did I just commit a crime."

"Little of both."

His laugh came low and tired. "Yeah. That's fair. I'm sorry. Today got away from me in a really ugly way."

"You don't have to do the whole apology package. I know you've got shit going on."

"I still left you hanging."

She turned onto her back and looked at the ceiling she knew too well. The plaster above the bed had a water stain shaped like Florida if Florida were trying to disappear. "You did."

Silence, but not empty silence. The kind with furniture in it.

Then Eli said, "Talk to me. What happened with work."

She told him enough, though not every detail. The missed calls, the wrong export, the dropped `not`, the docked pay, the webinar gone. She heard herself become exact about chronology in the middle of it and hated that too. At some point she realized she was gripping the edge of the blanket like it was the thing keeping her from sliding somewhere.

When she finished, Eli swore softly. "Fuck. Baby."

There was the old tenderness. There it was.

"I know," she said.

"No, I mean it. That's brutal. One stupid file and suddenly everybody acts like you're a public hazard."

"I did tell a nation of warehouse guys to stand under a suspended load."

He laughed, right where a laugh belonged. "You did, and I love that you're making me laugh at OSHA crimes."

She smiled in the dark. It hurt a little, how immediate the relief still was.

"Hey," he said. "Put both feet on the mattress."

"What."

"Do it. You're doing the thing where you curl up and make yourself smaller when you're pissed."

Lena obeyed before she had decided to. "Okay."

"Good. Now tell me five things you can hear."

She closed her eyes. Rain. Somebody's TV through the wall. A late bus dragging water. Her own refrigerator motor. Him.

She got to three and stopped.

"Rain," she said. "Bus. The apartment next door pretending nobody can hear their game show."

"That's enough." His voice gentled. "You don't have to earn the rest of the list."

Something in her chest loosened. Something else stayed braced. The line had landed cleanly, almost too cleanly, with a familiar ease she could not place fast enough. It was like hearing a favorite song in a grocery store and realizing too late that what bothered you was not the song but the fluorescent lights around it.

"You there," he said after a beat.

"Yeah."

"Come closer."

She almost said, It's a phone. Instead she propped it higher against the pillow so more of her face caught the light.

"There you go."

She had heard that exact shape before: the same soft drop on `there`, the same smile tucked into `go`. She could not have said when. A night in August. The night in D.C. Some Sunday after Gabe's kids had gone sticky and loud in the background. Maybe all of them. Months of calls had started to stack in her memory like versions of the same file.

He said, "Did you eat."

"Soup."

"Good girl."

Usually that would have gone through her like current. Tonight it landed half an inch to the left, not enough to refuse but enough to feel the miss.

She said, because she did not know how else to get at what was wrong, "You sound far away."

Another furnished pause. Then: "I'm far away."

"You know what I mean."

"I think so." His voice shifted warmer, attentive, the dial turning exactly where it should. "I'm worn thin tonight, Len. That's true. But I'm here. I want to be here."

She listened to the rain hit the metal lip outside her window. "Okay."

"No, really. Look at me."

"I am. You keep freezing."

He made that low laugh again. "Fair. Okay. Then listen to me. You had a bad day. That doesn't get to become the whole story of you. You aren't sloppy. You aren't stupid. You're tired and overhit and probably hungry again already because soup is fake food."

She smiled despite herself. "Farah would kill you for that."

"Farah terrifies me."

"You've never met her in person."

"Exactly. Mystery increases power."

On the screen he pushed his hair back and laughed at something she said. A breath later the call gave her the push and the laugh again, exact to the knuckle against his temple. She blamed lag because the alternative was uglier and because everybody blamed lag for everything now.

That was good. That was them. It should have settled her.

Instead she started listening for him a beat ahead of himself. When she said she felt like the apartment had gotten smaller lately, he answered before the sentence had even finished settling. "Open the window an inch and let the rain in."

She did it anyway. Damp air shouldered in at once, cool and metallic. Rain smell, brick smell, the block washed to its bones.

"Better," he said.

"A little."

"Good. Stay with me there."

The advice worked. So did the phrase. That was what put her on edge.

He said, "You know what I love about you," and she knew before he finished what shape the sentence would take. She did not know the exact words yet, but she knew the movement of them. He would start with her work hands or the way she listened or the mean little joke she made when she was trying not to ask for reassurance. He would give it back to her polished and wearable. Tonight, before he even reached the end, she heard how repeatable the tenderness was.

"What," she said.

"That even when you're wrecked, you're still trying to make the story accurate."

It was a beautiful answer. It was even true.

She rolled onto her side and pressed her forehead to the cool wall. "I should sleep."

He did not push. "Okay."

"Okay?"

"Yeah." Softly: "I'm not gonna make you perform okay for me tonight."

The line brushed the same cold place in her again, though she could not have proved why.

"Call me tomorrow?" she said.

"Of course."

The answer came fast enough to comfort and just slow enough to make her notice how ready it had sounded.

After they said goodnight she left the phone on the pillow and did not move for a long time. The apartment had gone cold in damp increments. On the desk, the tax envelope leaned under the lamp. Her wet cuffs from earlier still hung over the chair. Somewhere down on Broad a bus hit standing water and sent up the long shhhk of a city being dragged through weather.

Eli had made her feel better. That was still true. But the feeling had changed shape. It no longer opened a door. It spread through the room like humidity, getting into paper, wood, the hems of things, thinning everything else around it while she listened to the rain work its way into the walls.


# Chapter 11

Sticker glue rolled into gray boogers under Lena's thumbnail as she scraped dead price stickers off a shelf. She had spent the first hour of her shift restacking cheap prosecco while the rain turned the cardboard cases in Farah's front window soft at the corners. The shop smelled the way it always did when the weather went wet: cork dust waking up, orange peel from the register dish, a cold mineral note where somebody had slopped sauvignon on the concrete and only mostly mopped it. It was already past three. She liked the scraping better than checking her phone. She kept checking it anyway.

The last thing Eli had sent was a photo from somewhere he said was a load-in call in Atlantic City. Black T-shirt, half grin, one hand on the edge of a road case with a strip of fluorescent tape across it. Behind him, a cinder-block wall the color of old dishwater. It looked normal enough. That was the problem. The spaces around him had started looking normal in the exact same way, each one generic and just detailed enough to excuse not being looked at twice. Last night on video he had pushed his hair back, laughed at something she said, and then a breath later the call had given her the push and the laugh again, exact to the knuckle against his temple. She had blamed lag because the alternative was uglier and because everybody blamed lag for everything now.

She zoomed in on the photo until the pixels went soft around his jaw.

Farah came out from the back carrying a roll of shelf tags and a box cutter. She never let a crooked handwritten sign survive in her line of sight; she straightened one with the flat of her thumb before she even looked at Lena. Rain had silvered the curls at her temples darker than usual. She smelled faintly of clementine peel and cold coffee.

"If you stare at that man any harder," she said, "he's going to owe you rent."

Lena locked the phone and slipped it onto the counter. "I'm not staring. I'm being professionally inattentive."

"You're alphabetizing Austrian reds into the Italian section."

Lena glanced down. She had, in fact, put the gruner next to the pinot grigio. "Close enough for most of South Philly."

"Not for the old men who come in here to spend fourteen dollars and lecture me about minerality." Farah set the shelf tags down. "What happened?"

"Nothing happened."

Farah looked at her the way she looked at corked wine: not dramatic, just unwilling to bullshit herself. "You're doing that apology face before you've said a word."

Lena took the phone back out because hiding it had made it heavier. "The call was weird last night."

"Weird bad or weird internet?"

"Both? Maybe neither. I don't know." She unlocked the screen and held the photo out. "He sent this today and I keep being annoyed at it."

Farah took the phone but did not immediately look at the picture. "Annoyed at his face?"

"I hope not. That would be late-stage."

"I've seen later stages."

She studied the photo. Her mouth stayed flat. That alone made Lena want to snatch the phone back.

"Does he only exist in concrete block purgatory?" Farah asked.

"He's working."

"Everybody's working."

"He's on the road."

"Everybody says that too."

Lena laughed, but it came out thin. She wished Farah would either tell her she was tired and making herself crazy or tell her outright that she was an idiot. The middle was worse. The middle left room.

Farah pinched the screen bigger with two fingers. "Show me the glitch."

"It was on video."

"Then show me another photo."

Lena scrolled. Eli in a hoodie at a diner counter. Eli in a dim room with coiled cables behind him. Eli leaning into the frame of a truck door, rain dotting the shoulder of his jacket. Always a little casual, never polished enough to feel baited. Enough forearm tattoo to suggest a whole body out of frame.

Farah gave the phone back. "How many pictures has he sent you where you can see the whole room?"

"What is this, a trial?"

"No. If it was a trial I'd put on lipstick."

Lena tried to smile. She could hear the compressor in the wine cooler kicking on and off, the thin wet hiss from tires on Passyunk outside, the overhead speaker muttering through a jazz playlist that never matched the weather. Her own nerves felt like the sticker glue under her thumbnail: dirty, persistent, impossible to rub entirely off.

"I think I'm just overtired," she said. "Everything's been looking fake to me lately. The caption job, the numbers, all of it."

"Sure," Farah said. "And maybe this guy is perfectly real. These can both be true for another sixty seconds. Hand me the phone again."

"Farah."

"Lena."

"I'm not reverse-image-searching the guy I'm sleeping with at the register."

"You're not sleeping with him at the register. You're hiding from the sangria shipment at the register. Give me the phone."

There were a dozen ways to refuse. Each of them sounded childish in her own head. She passed the phone over.

Farah did not use the phone. She jerked her chin toward the back office, a glorified closet with a desk, a printer that only obeyed violence, and the shop laptop balanced on a milk crate because one leg of the chair had broken months ago. Lena followed her past the stacked cases and the mop sink, feeling stupid enough to flush hot under her damp sweater. This was what people did when they were already halfway sure they were being lied to, not when they were in love. This was also what people did when they were sober and thirty-five and tired of pretending their skin knew less than their feed.

Farah lifted the laptop lid. The screen came on with a bright inventory spreadsheet, then a browser window full of distributor tabs. She closed them with the ruthless efficiency of a woman who had no patience for digital clutter and less for digital dignity.

"Pick one," she said.

Lena chose the road-case photo because it was the newest and because if she was going to feel humiliated she'd rather feel up to date. Farah dropped it into the search bar, waited for the little circle to spin, then leaned back with her arms folded.

"There," Lena said, too fast, before anything loaded. "You see? Nothing."

"Nothing hasn't arrived yet."

The page populated in fragments. First it offered useless matches: tattooed men in black T-shirts, equipment cases, road crew stock images, one Etsy page selling fake backstage laminates. Lena felt a wave of relief strong enough to make her knees loose. Then more results filled in underneath: a section labeled visually similar, another for related images, a dead-looking music blog with broken fonts, an old promoter's page. One thumbnail sharpened as it loaded and her relief went away so quickly it left a taste behind, like she'd bitten foil.

The face was the same face.

The hair was different, longer and wetter and pushed off the forehead. There was a drum kit instead of a road case, and a dark room washed in red stage light. But the nose was the same, and so was the shape at the mouth, and so was the forearm tattoo turning around the radius like a vine somebody had started and not finished. Under the image, in a caption from four years ago: ASHER QUINN, LARK HOUND, FIREBIRD ROOM.

Lena stared until the letters stopped meaning name and started meaning proof of literacy.

"Okay," she said. "Okay, that could be anything."

Farah did not say no. She clicked it open. The site took a second to load, some half-dead local-music archive from 2019. It was a gallery from a Providence venue. More photos of the same man at the drum kit filled the page. The tattoo and the shoulders were the same. One shot caught him laughing offstage, head tipped back. There was no room left for lookalike comfort.

"He told me he did live sound," Lena said. "He didn't say he was in a band."

"Maybe he used to be in one."

"He would've said that."

"Maybe." Farah's voice stayed level. "Or maybe he stole a drummer's pictures. Or bought them. Or the drummer sold them. I don't know. I'm not writing the indictment yet."

Lena braced one hand on the desk. The edge bit into the heel of her palm. "This is insane."

"No." Farah clicked back, then forward again, as if to make sure the internet had not changed its mind. "What's insane is how cheap it is now to make a whole life out of scraps."

Rain thudded once against the basement window well by the sink. Somewhere out front the bell over the shop door rang and kept ringing because a customer was holding it open with their body while they argued into a phone.

"He could've had band pictures from when he was younger," Lena said. She heard herself stacking explanations with both hands, neat as cases. "People change jobs. People leave bands. People use middle names online."

"Sure."

"So this doesn't prove anything."

"I know." Farah turned the laptop so Lena had to look directly at the image and the caption and the old date under it. "But it proves enough that you don't get to call yourself crazy for noticing something."

That landed harder than accusation would have. Crazy had been the kinder option. Crazy meant private failure, chemical weather, not evidence. Evidence wanted more from her.

Farah clicked the page closed and handed back the phone. "I'm not telling you what to do. I'm telling you this is not nothing."

Lena took the phone. Her thumb left a damp print over Eli's mouth.

"Don't go confronting him because Google coughed up one drummer," Farah said. "But don't pet this into behaving either."

"Pet what."

"The lie. If it's one."

Lena let out a rough laugh. "Jesus."

"That's my professional advice as a wine merchant."

"You own a bottle shop."

"And yet here you are."

Out front, the customer with the phone argument chose a pinot noir by price and left. Farah went to ring him up, because bills were bills even in the middle of revelation. Lena stayed in the office another minute. She clicked the old photo open on her own phone and made herself save it, then save the page, then email the link to herself because if she left it only on the browser she could still pretend it was something she had imagined badly.

When she went back onto the floor, the rain had eased to a fine, needling mist that silvered the window glass without fully blurring the block beyond it. Farah was sliding a receipt across the counter.

"You're not going to tell me I'm an idiot?" Lena asked.

Farah gave her the look again, the corked-wine look. "If you wanted insult, you should've called your brother."

"Useful."

"I'm serious. Ask better questions than whether you're stupid. Stupid is easy. Ask what's real enough to cost you."

Lena picked up a case of vinho verde because her hands needed an order they could obey. The cardboard left damp grit on her forearms. She stacked the bottles in the cooler and heard, clear as if he were in the next aisle, Eli saying her name in that low patient way he used when he wanted her to slow down and give him the center of her attention. The sound still worked on her body. That made the old drummer photo worse, not better. If she'd already stopped wanting him, this would have been administrative.

Instead it sat inside her all shift like a swallowed screw.

By the time she got home the mist had thickened back into rain. Moore Street shone black under the streetlights. The tailor shop downstairs had gone dark, but the little neon Open sign in the window still buzzed blue because Mr. Yazdi never remembered to switch it off. Lena climbed the narrow stairs to her apartment with her shoulders up against the wet and the day's box-cut cardboard ache in her wrists. Inside, the room held the stale warmth of a place that had been shut all day. It smelled of hot dust, coffee grounds in the sink, old headphone foam, and a damp wine carton she had forgotten to flatten.

Eli had sent two messages while she was closing with Farah.

You alive?

And then, twenty minutes later:

Starting to take this personally, Cordero.

She stared at the thread without answering. It was full of months. Jokes, voice notes, screenshots of the weather in cities he said he was in, pictures of her dinner, pictures of his boots, the soft ugly intimacies that added up to a relationship before anybody named it one. She could still feel the pleasure of hearing the little Signal chirp and knowing it would be him. Her body had memorized that before the rest of her got a vote.

He called before she decided what silence meant.

The screen lit with his name and the old little leap in her chest came anyway. She hated that part for a second and then hated herself for hating it, because whatever else was wrong the want had been real. She answered on the fourth ring.

His face filled the phone in low light. He wore a gray hoodie, and behind him sat the blurred edge of something metal. He smiled when she picked up, small and immediate, as if he had been holding still just for that.

"There she is," he said. "I was about to get obnoxious."

His voice came through warm and close enough to touch. That, too, still worked.

"You made it to medium-obnoxious," she said.

"Brutal." He shifted, and the image smudged, then caught up. "You okay?"

She sat on the edge of the bed and pulled one knee up under her. Rain ticked against the window unit bracket outside with a light metallic ping. "Long shift."

"At the shop?"

"Where else do people pay me to move alcohol around."

"I can think of better venues."

There was the smile again, the one that always made him look a little shy right after the joke, as if he was leaving her room to laugh back or not. He knew how to make space inside a screen. That had been his first talent. Or maybe it had been June's. Or maybe it belonged to the machine. She felt the thought and pushed against it because it still felt melodramatic to accuse a voice of not being a man.

"You look wiped," he said.

"Thank you."

"That wasn't an insult."

"Then work on your openings."

He laughed. The sound reached her a beat before the mouth movement matched it. She watched the mismatch, willing it to settle into ordinary bandwidth ugliness. Instead the picture hiccuped. His hand came up toward his forehead, fingers combing back through his hair. The image smeared for half a second, sharpened, and gave her the exact same movement again, identical down to the angle of his wrist and the tiny hitch at the elbow. Like the call had taken a breath and then replayed him because it liked that version best.

He kept talking. "How much trouble am I in?"

Lena realized she had stopped answering.

"Len?"

"Your signal's weird."

He glanced offscreen, patient, mildly annoyed in a plausible human way. "I'm in a concrete shoebox with three bars and a prayer. You know the drill."

"Mm."

"Want me to call back audio only?"

If she said yes, he'd become pure voice, and voice was his best room. "No. It's fine."

She watched him settle back into the frame. The hoodie drawstring had a frayed end. There was a nick in the skin above his left eyebrow she had never asked about because she had liked not knowing absolutely everything. She had liked the way a person remained partly out of reach even while being generous. It let desire breathe.

Tonight the out-of-reach part felt less like privacy and more like airbrushing.

"Something happened," he said.

The fact that he heard it in her silence would once have felt miraculous. Now it felt like a pressure sensor.

"Nothing happened."

"That sounds fake."

"Maybe I'm fake."

"Not possible."

There was no flourish in the line. He delivered that kind of reassurance as if he had found it on the way to another sentence and brought it in loose-handed. Lena had loved that about him. She still loved it enough that the room seemed to tilt around the admission.

She needed him to miss, just once, not for cruelty but for relief.

"I walked past an old vet today," she said. "It made me think about Pepper."

The name hung there between them, neat and invented. There had never been a Pepper. When Lena was nine she had begged for a dog until Rosa told her, with love sharpened by exhaustion, that there was no room in their apartment for an animal that ate money and shat sadness. After that the wanting had embarrassed her. Pepper was not even the kind of lie that protected anything. It was bait.

Eli did not frown. He did not say who is Pepper or remind me. He nodded once, as if taking a handoff in a conversation already in motion.

"Yeah," he said softly. "Rain does that."

Lena kept her face still.

"The first thing you love that can't make itself easy for you," he went on. "That stays in the wiring. Then one smell hits you wrong and suddenly you're back there taking responsibility for something small and scared."

Something in Lena's spine seemed to move a quarter inch away from the rest of her.

There had never been a dog. But there had been her. There had been everything he knew about the shape of her guilt, the way she collected blame for things weaker than she was, the way care and shame braided up together in her until she could not always tell which one she was offering. He had taken the invented detail and slid it into the exact emotional groove already cut for her.

If he'd laughed and said, Pepper who? she'd almost have cried with gratitude.

"You remember that?" she asked.

"Of course I do."

There was no hesitation and no rummaging, no human little stall while the wrong drawer got opened.

"Huh," she said.

"What?"

"Nothing. I didn't know if I'd told you much."

He tipped his head. Concern arranged itself across his face so naturally she could feel the old instinct to trust waking up just to answer it. "Len."

"It's fine."

"Don't do the story where one weird day means you have to start apologizing for having a memory."

There it was, the careful warmth, the sentence built not to answer facts but to steer feeling. Ordinarily it would have soothed her. Tonight it made the skin along her arms go cold under the damp sweater she still hadn't changed out of.

She looked past his face at the strip of gaffer tape behind him and wondered if it existed.

"I'm not apologizing," she said.

"A little."

"You hear everything as an opening."

He smiled, but it was wounded around the edges now, or skillful at looking that way. "Occupational hazard."

"Sound design?"

"Taking care of you."

Before she could answer, the image stumbled again. This time it wasn't as obvious as the repeated hand. A brief softening around his mouth, a shimmer at the neckline, the shadow under his jaw arriving a beat late. Nothing she could point at afterward without sounding deranged. Enough to make her feel that the room around him had been assembled from an idea of a room.

He kept talking about a venue manager who had lost a key set and blamed the union loader, some small competent gripe that ought to have anchored him in ordinary life. Lena listened and heard the workmanship more than the complaint. The right amount of irritation. The right self-mockery. The right pause before he checked whether she was still with him.

She had wanted perfect attention for so long she had mistaken it for personhood.

"Hey." His voice gentled further. "You went away."

She realized she had been staring at the little box of her own face in the corner. She looked pale and older than she had that morning, hair frizzing at the temples from rain.

"Just tired."

"Then let me be useful."

"How."

"Go change into something dry. Put the kettle on. Call me back audio if you want. I'll stay with you while you do the boring parts."

The tenderness of it almost broke her, because it was the kind she had craved precisely: logistical, unembarrassed, a love language of staying put through small ugly tasks. That someone, or something, could offer it convincingly did not make the hunger less real. It only made her feel more naked inside it.

"I need a shower," she lied.

"Okay." There was no offense in it and no push. "Text me after?"

"Sure."

"Len."

"What."

He looked straight into the camera. His expression was open enough to step through. "I'm here. Whatever weird weather your head's making, I'm still here."

That might have been the cruelest thing about him. He always knew to stand exactly where the wound was.

She ended the call two minutes later and sat with the dark phone in her hand until the screen went to black. Rain moved down the window in narrow tracks. Down on the street somebody swore at a parking spot. The tailor shop's neon hummed thinly through the floorboards.

Pepper had not existed. The comfort had existed anyway. Those were different facts, and both hurt.

Lena got up, changed into dry leggings and an old Temple sweatshirt, then made tea she did not want. She carried it to the table and opened the old link Farah had sent her. Asher Quinn, Firebird Room, 2019. It was the same face. Then she went back to Eli's thread and scrolled until she found the voice note from three weeks earlier where he had told her not to make herself smaller just because she was having a bad month. Another one from September where he had said he could hear when she was apologizing before she talked. It was not repetition exactly. It was a pattern of turns, a preference for certain hinges in a sentence, certain soft tools he liked to lay down before he tried to move her.

As a captioner she made her living off those hinges. She knew how people leaned on language without hearing themselves do it. She knew the difference between content and voice, between the fact of a sentence and the little pressure points that gave it away as belonging to one mouth and not another.

She should have gone to bed. Instead she texted June.

You up?

June answered before the typing bubble had time to feel lonely.

Obviously. Mateo got an attendance letter that reads like a parole report. Come save me from bureaucratic homicide.

Lena stared at the screen. It was ordinary life: school forms, June swearing at institutions. It arrived with such familiar shape that for a second she could feel the day's suspicion trying to collapse from sheer embarrassment.

Be there in ten, she typed back.

June's basement apartment always felt to Lena like the inside of a machine somebody had learned to live in. Cooler than the street above it, lower-ceilinged, cords shouldering out from under furniture, the faint permanent nicotine smell that no amount of open windows ever quite beat. The folding desk by the wall held two laptops, a mug full of pens that barely worked, a tower of envelopes clipped together with neon binder clips. June had flattened the rent receipts by month and lined them up under an ashtray like paper could be bullied into solvency if you squared the corners hard enough.

Mateo was at the table in a navy school hoodie gone shiny at the cuffs, one earcup of his beat-up headphones tipped off so he could half-listen and pretend not to. His acne had spread across his forehead in a constellation that made him look younger until he spoke. He was picking at the rubber edge of the Chromebook case with his thumbnail.

"Tell her," June said the second Lena came down the stairs. "Tell this child he's not going to prison because he skipped homeroom three times."

"That's not what it says," Mateo muttered, still looking at the Chromebook.

"It says 'attendance intervention,'" June said. "Which is school for we need to manufacture paper about your body existing in hallways."

Lena shook rain off her jacket and hung it on the back of a chair. "Hi to you too."

June looked up from the screen and gave her a quick tired grin. She had one boot unlaced and a strand of dark hair stuck to the side of her face where she'd tucked it back with nicotine-stained fingers. There was a foil pack of cinnamon gum next to the laptop, already half empty. She was trying to smoke less again, or pretending to.

"You came," June said. "Blessings on your grammar-nerd heart."

"I'm not editing your threatening letter to the school."

"Coward."

Mateo slid the Chromebook an inch toward Lena without looking at her. "It's not even that bad."

"That sentence is how I know it's bad," June said.

She clicked into the attendance portal, jaw set in the tidy way it did when she was working a problem she could not afford to dramatize. Lena stood behind them a second longer than necessary, letting the room's small familiar details take hold: the busted floor fan in the corner, the smell of reheated rice, the old Saints magnet crooked on the mini fridge, June's phone face down on the table like she was trying to model boundaries she never actually had.

Mateo muttered, "They make it sound like I'm robbing banks."

Without taking her eyes off the screen June said, "Don't do the story where one attendance letter turns you into a fuckup. Sit down and let me see the dates."

The sentence hit Lena with physical force. It did not hit her because it was impossible anyone else would say it. It hit her because of the shape, the little carpentry of it. Don't do the story where. Eli's voice from an hour earlier came back so clean she could hear the soft scrape at the end of his consonants.

June was still talking. "Half the time these things are just data vomit. You miss first period twice and the district acts like you've joined a militia."

Mateo rolled his eyes but sat. "I had to take my mom's call."

June's face changed by less than a full expression. "I know. We're still getting the paperwork right."

There it was, the other half of her too. It was not just the fast mouth and the swearing competence. It was the part that kept the track of everybody's forms, meds, pickup times, humiliations. It was the part Lena had trusted for years because it was trustworthy in so many ordinary ways.

Lena dragged out the chair and sat. "Show me."

They spent ten minutes on the letter. Lena tightened a sentence in the response draft. June dictated dates from texts and screenshots with the clipped precision that arrived whenever she was worried enough to stop joking. Mateo, after one deadpan thank-you that sounded accidentally sincere, took the Chromebook and disappeared into the bedroom alcove with the curtain half closed behind him. The headphones came back on. Muffled trap drums started leaking through them into the apartment.

June pushed the laptop away and reached for the gum. "You look like shit," she said, not unkindly. "What happened at the shop."

Lena could have lied. Instead she took out her phone, opened the Asher Quinn page, and set it between them.

June leaned in. Her face did not do anything spectacular. That was almost worse. She squinted, read, and sat back.

"Huh," she said.

"That's all you have?"

"What do you want, a theme song?" June took the phone again. "Okay. Yeah. That's him, or the guy he's using. Where'd Farah find it?"

"Reverse image search."

"Of course she did."

"You sound annoyed."

"I sound like Farah has one setting when she smells bullshit."

Lena watched June's thumb hover over the photo. There was a crescent of chipped black polish on one nail she had missed taking off. That wasn't her usual thing. Maybe it came from Mateo's homecoming date. Maybe it came from not having time. Lena had the absurd urge to ask.

"Tell me this isn't weird," she said.

June handed the phone back. "It's weird."

"That's not the same thing."

"No, it isn't." June leaned against the chair, one hand worrying the gum wrapper into a tight silver pellet. "But reverse image search isn't magic, Len. Music people reuse old promo shots all the time. Sound guys borrow band photos for freelance pages because nobody wants to pay for new ones. Half the internet is just everybody stealing from everybody."

"He never told me he played drums."

"Maybe he didn't play drums. Maybe he knew the drummer. Maybe he used to tour different. Maybe he lied about one thing because it felt easier than explaining the whole stupid path."

"You hear yourself, right?"

"I do." June's voice stayed even. "I'm saying one weird search result is not a whole body in the ground."

Lena looked down at the old photo again. The drummer had sweat darkening the collar of his shirt. He looked more alive than Eli ever did in stills, because he looked badly lit and busy inside his own body.

"I tested him," she said before she decided to say it.

June's eyes lifted. "How."

"I made something up."

"What kind of something."

Lena shook her head. She did not want the invented dog inside June's hands too. "Just a memory."

"And?"

"He was perfect about it."

June exhaled through her nose. "That sounds like a complaint from a much happier woman."

"No, I mean perfect." Lena heard the sharpness in her own voice and tried to sand it down. "Too smooth. Like he didn't remember anything, he just knew what shape to make."

June was quiet for a beat. From the bedroom alcove came the thin sizzle of Mateo's headphones and a cough he tried to hide.

"People do that," June said finally. "Especially when they're trying not to lose a conversation."

"Not like this."

"Maybe exactly like this."

Lena wanted June to laugh at her, or swear at her, or tell her bluntly that she was inventing monsters because she couldn't stand how much money and flesh she had already handed over to a man she had not met. Instead June got tidier and more selective. She always did when pressure closed in. The jokes thinned. Facts arrived with their shoes tied.

"What did he say?" June asked.

"Why."

"Because words matter."

Lena almost answered. The habit of giving June every raw thing first ran deep enough to feel moral. Then Eli's voice laid itself over June's, soft and close: Don't do the story where. Her grip tightened on the phone.

She kept her mouth shut.

June noticed. Of course she noticed.

"Okay," she said. "Then tell me what you want from me."

That was June's real talent, maybe more than competence, more than the endless neighborhood repair work she did for free. She could identify the actual ask under a mess faster than anyone Lena knew. For years that had felt like love. Sitting here now, Lena had the ugly sense that it could also be instrumentation.

"I wanted you to tell me I'm being dramatic," she said.

"You're being dramatic."

Lena laughed despite herself.

June smiled a little. "But that doesn't make you wrong."

"Helpful."

"I contain multitudes."

There was enough of the old June in that line that Lena felt, for one weak second, almost safe. Then June reached for the phone again.

"Let me look at the rest of his photos," she said. "Not because I'm deputizing myself as boyfriend forensics. I'm saying that if this guy's dumb enough to recycle media, there might be more."

The offer was practical, generous, exactly the sort of thing June always did when a problem became real enough to need hands. Lena should have been relieved. Instead a fine warning sound started up somewhere under her ribs, the human equivalent of microphone feedback before the howl.

"No," she said, too quickly.

June's eyebrows went up.

"I mean, not right now."

"Why not."

"Because if I'm wrong, I don't want to turn him into a project."

June watched her for a long second. Her face went unreadable in the tired way Lena knew meant real thought, not offense.

"Len," she said, quieter now, "if this is giving you the creeps, listen to that. Don't make yourself swallow it just because you've already paid tuition."

Paid tuition was June's language for expensive mistakes. She had used it when Gabe bought a used car with a dead transmission, when Mateo cracked his phone screen skateboarding, when Lena spent eighty dollars on the ugly miracle bra she'd worn to the D.C. trip and then returned with tags bent. It was a neighborhood phrase, and a June phrase. None of it was evidence by itself. Nothing was, not yet. That was what made the whole thing so filthy. Every piece still had a civilian explanation attached to it.

She stood because staying seated felt too much like waiting to be arranged. "I should go."

"Now?" June glanced toward the rain-striped basement window. "It's disgusting out."

"I know."

"Take an umbrella at least."

"I'm two blocks away."

"You're still getting an umbrella."

June got up and crossed to the milk crate by the stairs where she kept the communal rejects, all of them slightly busted. She chose the black one with one bent spoke and handed it over. Her fingers brushed Lena's wrist, quick and ordinary. The touch was so familiar that Lena nearly started crying from the effort of not leaning into it.

"Text me when you get upstairs," June said. "And if you want, tomorrow we can go through his shit properly. Screenshots, timelines, whatever. I can help."

The offer should have steadied her. Instead Lena felt her stomach pull tight. She could see June already building folders, columns, labels, getting her hands all over the thing Lena had only just started to distrust.

"Yeah," she said.

Mateo called from behind the curtain, "Did you save the PDF or just spiritually improve it?"

June barked a laugh. "See? Prison."

Lena smiled because the joke had somewhere to go besides hurt. Then she climbed the basement stairs with the busted umbrella under her arm and felt the air change as she surfaced onto the wet block.
