Somebody nearby was frying onions. A bus groaned at the corner. Water dripped from a sagging awning in steady taps that sounded, absurdly, like a metronome set too slow.

She did text June when she got upstairs. Habit was habit. inside. thx for the umbrella.

June sent back a thumbs-up and then, a second later:

Get dry. We'll figure it out.

That should have comforted her. It nearly did.

Instead Lena put the phone face down on the table, pulled a tax envelope out from under a stack of unopened grocery coupons, and sat with a pen. The envelope still had October 15 circled on the front in her own handwriting. She turned it over.

First she wrote ASHER QUINN.

Under that she wrote PEPPER.

Then she opened Eli's thread and started listing every place he'd said he was working in the last month. Atlantic City. Harborlight. The old annex off Ocean. She dragged his voice notes onto the laptop, opened a blank document, and typed two names at the top:

ELI

JUNE

She dated the page, dropped in screenshots, and started transcribing the lines she wanted to hear again in daylight. By the time the tea went cold and the microwave clock dragged toward eleven, HARBORLIGHT was boxed twice, June's last text sat in a folder on the desktop, and Lena had stopped waiting to feel less crazy before she started keeping records.


# Chapter 12

June had texted that they'd figure it out. By the next morning, with rain pasting free-pizza flyers flat to the South Philadelphia sidewalk, Lena took that to mean June's car keys. She went down June's basement steps with her phone in one hand and Eli's last message open on the screen long enough for the rain to dot it silver.

June's place always felt like the underside of the block: cooler, dimmer, full of cords and old machine heat. A fan buzzed in one corner for no reason Lena could identify except habit. Mateo was at the folding table in a gray Central hoodie with his beat-up headphones around his neck, picking a gummy bear off the W key of a school Chromebook with the edge of a library card. June stood at the counter in work boots and black socks, one lace trailing, tearing open junk mail with the nicotine-yellowed side of her thumbnail.

She looked up once. "You look bad."

"Thanks."

"You want coffee or the truth."

"I want your car keys."

Mateo stopped worrying the gummy bear and looked between them without moving anything else in his face.

June laughed, quick and automatic. "For what."

Lena held the phone out. Eli's message sat there in the same patient cadence that had kept working on her even after it started to chill her. "Long wet load-in. Harborlight's floor is murdering my knees. If I survive tech, I'm stealing you Sunday." Below it was the earlier one from Tuesday with the too-specific little detail that had done the damage: "The space is this old annex off Ocean. Smells like dust and salt and somebody's bad coffee." He had sent a picture after that, a black dance floor rolled halfway out over scuffed wood, cinderblock painted cream, one red EXIT sign.

"I'm going down there," Lena said. "Wildwood, Crest, wherever the hell this is supposed to be. I just need to see the building."

June set the junk mail down. "Now."

"Yes, now."

"In this weather."

"It's rain, not a biblical test."

Mateo glanced at the basement window where water was crawling down the glass. "Could be both."

June pointed the torn envelope at him without looking. "Not helping."

He lifted one shoulder and went back to the Chromebook.

Lena heard herself getting too formal and corrected it before the sentence finished. "I know this is unhinged. I do. I know if I say it out loud it sounds like I'm one step from making a wall of yarn in my apartment. But he told me Tuesday at three twelve that he was at a dance residency through Sunday, and if that's true then there is a place. There has to be a sign or a flyer or a bored woman at a desk who has seen his face once. I don't need to catch him. I just need one thing that isn't a phone."

June leaned back against the counter. She was still smiling a little, but it had been reduced to maintenance. "Lena. Surprise-inspecting your boyfriend at work is not proof. It's chaos. It's also, for the record, exactly the kind of thing that makes people lie harder."

"Then he can be mad at me from inside a real building."

"Or he can be in rehearsal and not answer because adults have jobs."

"Then I'll know there's a rehearsal."

"You can just call him."

"No." Lena rubbed rainwater off the phone with the heel of her hand and only smeared it. "If I call him, he'll talk. That's the whole problem. He'll say something warm in that voice and ask me why I sound tired and suddenly I'm apologizing again."

June's face did a small shut thing. Lena would have missed it if she did not know it so well from years of watching June hear bad news, bullshit a landlord, keep her temper with a copier repair line. When June was actually stressed, she got tidier. The jokes came off first.

"What did Farah say?" June asked.

"I didn't say Farah said anything."

"You didn't have to."

That was June too, the remembered fact offered like a receipt. Lena hated how much comfort that trick had given her over the years. She hated more that it still worked for half a second.

"Farah said if a place exists, it has a phone number," Lena said. "I know that's the reasonable move. I'm not in a reasonable mood."

"No kidding."

Mateo looked up again. "If you go, don't take Forty-Two all the way. It backs up where it narrows. Fifty-Five is ugly but faster."

June stared at him. "You have algebra."

"I also have ears."

"You have school."

"I'm leaving in twenty minutes." He slid the gummy bear free and flicked it into the trash with a tiny, satisfied snap. "Also if the thing is fake, Google won't help anyway. It'll just show you wedding venues and a haunted mini golf place."

Lena laughed before she could stop it. The sound came out wrong, too high and thin. Mateo looked embarrassed by having caused it and hunched over his computer again.

June picked up her phone, put it down, then picked up the keys from the chipped bowl by the sink. "This is stupid."

"I know."

"This is how people end up on neighborhood Facebook pages with a Ring screenshot and a caption like LADIES STAY SAFE."

"Probably."

"And if he's there, if you find out this is all normal, you're buying me lunch and gas and the extra toll because you decided to go full detective in a nor'easter."

"It's October. It's not a nor'easter."

"Look outside."

Lena did. The narrow strip of concrete at the bottom of the basement steps had gone black with water. The gutter was choking on leaves. Somewhere up on the street a truck hit a pothole deep enough to make a splash that sounded like a sheet dropped on tile.

June said, "Give me ten minutes."

"For what."

"For shoes, a charger, and because Mateo still needs lunch money unless your investigation budget covers that too."

It should not have mattered, but Lena felt the small flush of shame anyway. June still had a real life standing around her in damp socks: the folded utility bill by the fruit bowl, the cheap deli turkey in the fridge, Mateo pretending not to listen because he had learned that from her. It would have been easier if June looked cleaner inside the suspicion. Instead she looked exactly like herself.

While June went into the bedroom, Mateo shut the Chromebook and reached for his backpack. "If it's fake," he said without looking up, "don't let her keep making it sound complicated."

Lena blinked. "What."

He shoved a notebook into the bag. "Adults do that when they already know something is bad. They add words until it's not bad anymore."

"Teo," June called from the bedroom.

"What? I said if."

He zipped the bag. His forehead had broken out again, angry little red bumps across the hairline. There was a geometry quiz folded in the front pocket, the corner marked with a C in red pen and then a smaller note beneath it offering corrections for half credit. He saw Lena looking and flipped the pocket closed with a shrug that was more annoyed than ashamed.

"I'm working on it," he said.

"I didn't say anything."

"You did your face."

June came back in a black rain shell, car charger looped around two fingers, winding the cord tight around her knuckles as if she could get the morning under control by force. "All right. Move before I remember I've still got standards."

On the way out Mateo said, "If you stop for food, don't get anything from the first Wawa off the expressway. Their hot case tastes like wet keys."

June pushed the door open with her hip. "A poet."

The car was a silver Corolla with one missing hubcap and a phone mount suction-cupped to the windshield so long it had left a pale ring on the glass. The passenger seat held three reusable grocery bags, a rolled-up hoodie, and a folder stamped KEYNEST in blue. Lena shoved the bags to the floor and climbed in. The seat was cold through her jeans. The inside of the car smelled like peppermint gum, old french fries, and the damp wool of June's rain shell.

They drove without music through wet block after wet block, wipers thocking time. At Broad the light caught them long enough that Lena could see herself in the side mirror: hair knotted badly, mascara shadow under the eyes, mouth already set for an argument she could not yet name.

June merged south and said, "So what's the plan here. You want to roll up on this man with your phone out and say hello, can somebody verify my romance."

"I want to see Harborlight or whatever the place is called."

"That was not the question."

"Then no. I don't want to roll up on anybody."

"Good, because that's how you get a cop called on you by a nineteen-year-old intern with a lanyard."

Lena looked at the phone again. She had screenshotted everything before she came over: the message about the old annex, the one about tech, the picture of the floor, the little throwaway complaint about coffee that had been convincing precisely because it was so stupid. A man lying for money would invent emergencies, not bad institutional coffee. A real person complained about coffee.

Except a fake person could do that too. That was the point. That was the whole grotesque trick. It had made her feel known in the exact grain of her life and now every grain was suspect.

At the entrance to Fifty-Five, traffic slowed to a crawl under the rain haze. June drummed once on the steering wheel, stopped herself, and said, too casually, "Residency can mean a lot of things, you know. Half the arts world is smoke and nouns. It could just be a rented room with a grant name."

Lena turned and looked at her. "You know a lot about dance residencies all of a sudden."

June kept her eyes on the road. "I know a lot about people making small things sound expensive."

"That wasn't what I said."

"No, but it's what you meant."

The rain thickened. Tractor trailers threw up curtains of dirty spray that smacked the windshield and then ripped away. Beyond them the marshland flattened out gray-green and drowned-looking.

Lena put the phone facedown in her lap. She could feel it there like a second pulse.

"If this is nothing," June said after a while, "if the place exists and he's just not answering because he's busy, you need to decide whether you want to be with somebody or audit him."

Lena stared out at the highway signs blurring past. "If it exists, I'll apologize."

"And if it doesn't."

The answer stuck somewhere behind Lena's teeth. She kept her eyes on the highway signs until they smeared into green ribbons and still could not tell which version would be worse.

* * *

The first address took them to a low tan building two blocks off Ocean that looked like it had been designed by someone angry at windows. BEACH TAG OFFICE was stenciled on one side in flaking blue paint. On the other was a smaller metal sign that said BOROUGH STORAGE. A chain hung through the glass handles of the front doors. Inside, under buzzing fluorescent lights, Lena could see folding tables stacked on their sides and a bulletin board with one crooked flyer for a rabies clinic.

June killed the engine and leaned forward as if a better angle might produce a man, a rehearsal, a single fact.

"Maybe they use the service side," she said. "Maybe they load in around the back."

Lena did not move. "To what."

"These places get called weird shit. Annex, office, civic center, whatever. He said annex."

"He said Harborlight. He said a residency. He said dancers."

"And that's an annex."

"It's a beach-tag office."

Rain hammered the roof hard enough that the whole car sounded tinny and enclosed, like they were inside a kicked bucket. June reached for the umbrella wedged behind her seat. It was the kind you bought from a pharmacy and hated instantly.

"Come on," she said. "Let's at least see."

They crossed the lot with the umbrella listing sideways between them, mostly protecting neither. The puddles were deep enough to fold icy water over the tops of Lena's sneakers. Up close the building got worse. One window had a handwritten notice taped inside: CLOSED FOR SEASONAL OPERATIONS. Another had a flyer for flu shots from the previous year. The cement planter by the door held two cigarette butts, a dead seagull feather, and a drowned yellowing mums arrangement gone stringy with rot.

Lena put her hand flat to the glass anyway. Her reflection, warped by rain, leaned back at her like somebody drunk.

"No music," she said.

"Because it's locked."

"No scuff marks. No cases. Nothing."

June peered through the side panel. "One floor photo doesn't buy us the whole room."

"Why are you defending a building."

That got June quiet.

Lena stepped back. Across the street an enormous fiberglass crab above a seafood place grinned through the weather with one claw chipped off. Beyond it motels sat shut up for the season, plastic lawn chairs stacked in white towers and chained together. She had been down here with her mother enough times in October to know how the shore smelled when the money went home: wet plywood, fryer grease that had soaked into walls all summer, salt pulling at metal. It should not have felt humiliating. It was just rain and closed businesses. But shame made everything public. Even the gulls sounded like they were laughing at her from a roofline.

Back in the car June said, "Show me the other thing."

"What other thing."

"You said he mentioned coffee."

Lena pulled up the thread. June held out her hand for the phone without thinking, then seemed to realize the gesture and waited. Lena gave it to her anyway. June scrolled with her mouth set and read aloud, "Old annex off Ocean, bad coffee, floor's decent, the director wears scarves like she's in a foreign film."

She handed it back. "Okay. Then maybe he was down here once. Maybe he grabbed coffee, took the floor picture, and kept talking like he was still working out of it."

"That is a very furnished maybe."

"I'm trying to keep up with the weather and your mood."

The diner was half a mile away, exactly where Eli had once said he was grabbing lemon pie after rehearsal because the dancers were all hungry and impossible. Lena had remembered that because she had liked the phrase hungry and impossible. It made the room around him feel populated. It made his life extend past the edge of her screen.

Blue Gull Diner sat at the corner under a faded sign with one gull missing an eye. The blinds were down. Brown paper covered the lower half of the windows. Inside, chairs were stacked upside down on the tables. A handwritten sign on the locked door said SEE YOU MEMORIAL DAY and below that, in smaller pen, TIPS MAILED FRIDAY IF THE OWNER STOPS BEING CHEAP.

Lena stood on the slick concrete apron staring at it while rain ticked off the aluminum awning.

"Well," June said. "That's rude timing."

Next door a bait-and-tackle shop was open under a sign shaped like a marlin. Buckets of green crab sat by the door in black water. A woman in a red fleece vest was behind the counter with lilac reading glasses hanging on a beaded chain and one cuff darkened where she had clearly just reached into a tank. She looked up at them with the expression of someone who had not asked for tourists and knew exactly how much they were going to cost her.

June gave Lena a little chin lift. Go on, then.

Lena hated her for it.

Inside, the place smelled like bait brine and coffee that had been reheated past dignity. The woman kept one hand on a styrofoam cup and waited.

Lena said, "Sorry. Weird question. Do you know if there is some kind of dance residency around here? Harborlight, maybe. Or a place people call the annex."

The woman blinked once. "A dance what."

"Like a rehearsal space. Program. Arts thing."

The woman took a sip of coffee, still looking at Lena over the rim. "Honey, the only arts thing next door is when somebody from Philly throws up in the hydrangeas."

June made a noise that could have been a cough.

Lena felt heat start behind her ears despite the wet. "Okay. Sorry. My, uh, boyfriend said he was working down here."

"Doing what."

"Sound design."

"For dancing."

"Yeah."

The woman looked past Lena out at the rain, considering the whole stupid coast. "Nope."

"There isn't anything."

"There is a ballroom class for widows at Saint Ann's on Thursdays, and there was a Zumba lady in Rio Grande for about six minutes before she ran off with a plumber. That's what I've got."

June said, lighter than necessary, "It could still be one of those donor weekends. You get six people in a borrowed room and everybody calls it a residency because retreat sounds too much like a church basement."

The woman shifted her coffee to the other hand. Her wedding band had been cut off and soldered back together crooked. "Private where. In October. You think rich people come here to make dance."

"I don't know what rich people do," June said.

"Neither do I, but if they were here I'd hear about it because they'd complain the coffee sucks."

There it was, the coffee detail coming back to Lena stripped of magic, just a local woman making the same joke because it was an obvious joke to make about this town. She thanked her, or thought she did. The words felt wet and useless in her mouth.

Back in the car she shut the door too hard. June started the engine and let the defroster roar for a minute.

"Okay," June said. "So. Not there."

"Not anywhere yet."

"Right."

The phone sat in Lena's lap, screen gone black. She could have unlocked it and found Eli's face in two seconds. She could have played one of the voice notes where he said her name low, like he was already in the room. Instead she looked through the windshield at the empty diner, the sign about tips, the rain making the parking lot look oiled.

"This is humiliating," she said.

June watched the defroster push a clear circle through the fog on the windshield before she said, "It's only humiliating if you decide it is."

Lena turned to her. "I'm standing in a bait shop asking strangers if my boyfriend has a workplace."

"Okay. Fair."

The municipal building was farther inland, a squat brick rectangle between a pharmacy and a dentist with a giant inflatable pumpkin collapsing gently in the rain by the curb. Inside, Lena's sneakers squeaked on the waxed floor. The lobby smelled like bleach and the sweet rotten note of wet umbrellas. A woman at the desk wore a fleece vest over a Christmas T-shirt even though it was barely mid-October, and she kept straightening a paper clip while she talked on the phone about a soccer permit.

Lena waited until she hung up.

"Hi," Lena said. "I'm sorry. I'm trying to find a program and I keep getting nowhere. Do you know if there's a Harborlight dance residency in town? Maybe at an annex building near Ocean."

The woman looked at her, then at June, then at the rain flattening itself against the glass doors. "Harborlight."

"Yes."

"No."

"Not now or not here."

"Not now, not here, not in summer either." She clicked to a calendar on her computer, then dragged a paper binder toward herself anyway as if for ceremonial emphasis. "We have line dancing at the senior center on Tuesdays, rec basketball sign-ups, and a trunk-or-treat in the church lot next week if nobody steals the extension cords. That's the arts calendar."

June tried again. "What if somebody booked it under an event company or a donor's LLC. What if the load-in was small, there were no signs, and they paid cash to the building."

The woman gave her a flat look over the top of the monitor. She had silver glitter on two fingernails and a nicotine patch visible where her vest gaped. "If somebody rented private space for a week and called it a residency, that would still not make it a town program. And if they wanted sound permits or load-in or parking for any amount of equipment, they would come through me or code. Nobody has. Nobody named Harborlight, either."

Lena heard herself say, "So there isn't a venue by that name."

"No."

"Or an annex."

"Not unless you mean the old tax office and that place is locked up tighter than my sister's liquor cabinet."

June gave a short laugh that died in the room.

The woman softened half an inch. "You want the Chamber office in Cape May maybe. They know about the artsy people."

"He said Wildwood," Lena said.

"Then Wildwood should know about it."

"And they don't."

"Apparently not."

Lena thanked her and walked out before the woman could turn kind. Kindness would have finished the job.

Outside, the rain had thinned to a mean steady drizzle. The air tasted metallic, like the weather had scraped its teeth on the ocean and come back inland. June followed her down the steps and stood beside her in the lot while cars hissed past.

"Lena."

"Don't."

"I'm saying the lie could still be smaller than a whole invented man."

"Smaller how."

June tucked her hands into the pockets of the rain shell. "For him dressing the work thing up."

Lena let out a sound that was almost a laugh and almost something uglier. "You hear yourself, right."

"People lie about jobs when the real one sounds small. They take one decent room, one paid day, and spin a whole week out of it because nobody wants to say they were hauling cases for a church gala."

"So that's your good version."

June thumbed the key fob hard enough to make the Corolla chirp behind them. "My good version is maybe he didn't want you showing up after D.C. Maybe he wanted one part of his life that wasn't under a microscope."

"I didn't put him under a microscope. I wanted to meet him one time in three months. Then I wanted the story to line up with the world."

"You reverse-searched his face."

"Because his face turned into a drummer named Asher Quinn."

June looked away, out toward the pharmacy lot where a shopping cart rolled loose and bumped a curb.

"Okay," she said. "Okay. I'm not saying this looks good."

"It looks insane."

"I'm saying it doesn't have to mean nothing is real."

Nothing is real would have been easier to hear from almost anyone else. From June it landed wrong, too exact in the place she was trying not to look. Lena turned toward the street so she would not have to watch June say another clever thing with that careful mouth.

Cars sprayed past. Somewhere farther off, toward the boardwalk, a loose sign banged metal against metal in a steady, stupid rhythm. Lena pictured Eli's floor photo again: the rolled marley, the red EXIT sign, the cream cinderblock. It could have been anywhere. It could have been a high school gym, a rehearsal room in a church basement, a file somebody had bought for six dollars off the internet. She had loved the image because it placed him. Now all it proved was that an image could have corners.

June said, quieter, "Let's get out of the rain."

Lena gave her only the back of her wet coat and started toward the car because there was nowhere else to go.

* * *

June took them to the supermarket on the edge of town with no discussion, just a left turn off the boulevard and then another into a lot full of wet carts. The big red sign hummed in the mist. An inflatable pumpkin sagged by the entrance with one arm collapsed, permanently hailing nobody.

"I need groceries," June said when she parked. "Mateo texted. We're out of everything."

Lena stared through the windshield. "Now."

"Yes, now. People still eat when their love lives are a landfill."

"You can go in."

June killed the engine. "Fine."

She sat there a second longer, fingers still on the key. Then she said, not looking over, "Come inside or don't. I don't care. I'm not leaving you in my car to spiral hard enough to crack the glass."

Lena almost told her to go fuck herself. The phrase rose cleanly. It would have felt good in the mouth. Instead she got out and followed June across the lot under the pharmacy umbrella that kept trying to invert.

Inside, the store was overheated and smelled of floor cleaner, seafood, and the sugary rot of overripe peaches. Endcaps had already turned to Halloween: miniature chocolates, paper skeletons, an entire pallet of pumpkin-spice coffee pods. June moved fast with a basket over one arm, practical as always, grabbing bread, eggs, sliced turkey, cereal, the banana yogurt drinks Mateo liked when he was too anxious to eat anything that required chewing. She added dishwasher pods, a family-size bag of pretzels, and a box of generic tampons without pausing to conceal any of it.

Lena wandered two aisles over and then back again. The fluorescent light made everybody's skin look faintly refrigerated. A child in a raincoat licked condensation off a freezer door while his father compared prices on frozen shrimp. At the front of the store, by the checkouts, a metal bin held ten-pound bags of ice in blue plastic.

She took one before she thought about it.

It was pure shore habit. Rosa's trailer ice maker had given up years before Rosa died, and every time they left the supermarket Lena or her mother would grab a bag on the way out and sling it into the cooler between the beach chairs and the folding table. Don't come back empty-handed, Rosa used to say, even when what she meant was don't come back without the thing I forgot.

Lena stood there holding the cold, sweating weight against her hip and hated herself for still having hands that remembered.

At checkout June looked at the ice and did not comment. The cashier, a woman with a neck brace under her sweatshirt and two tiny gold studs in one nostril, rang it through with the rest.

"Four twenty-nine," she said, then glanced at the bag and added, "That thing leaks if you baby it."

Outside, the drizzle had thickened again. June popped the trunk, set the groceries inside, came around to the driver's seat, and turned the key.

The engine clicked once. Then again, smaller and meaner.

June tried a third time. Nothing but the choked rattle of a battery giving up with attitude.

For one long second neither of them moved.

Then June put her forehead against the steering wheel and said, with no style left in it at all, "Fuck me."

Lena was still standing beside the car with the ice hugged to her coat. Meltwater ran down her sleeve and into her cuff. One of the plastic grocery bags had cut a red line into the base of her fingers.

"Did you leave the lights on," she asked.

"No."

The dashboard glowed accusingly. June looked at it, looked away. "Maybe."

Lena laughed. She could not help it. It came out ugly enough that June turned.

"Don't start."

"Start what."

"This."

Lena lifted the bag of ice half an inch, as if presenting evidence. "I'm standing in supermarket drizzle holding ten pounds of sentimental ice because my fake boyfriend does not have a workplace and now your car is dead. I think this has actually started."

June got out hard enough to rock the car. "Pop the hood."

"I don't know where that is in your stupid car."

"It's under the dash."

"I know where it is in cars in general. I don't know where it is in your stupid car."

That made June stop, one hand already under the steering wheel. For a second Lena thought she might laugh. Instead June yanked the latch and went to the front.

The hood came up with a cough of old metal. Rain beaded on the engine cover. Lena stood uselessly beside the passenger door while carts clattered toward the entrance and back. Water dripped from the bottom seam of the ice bag onto her sneaker, then through the canvas to her sock. Across the lot a man loaded paper towels into a pickup bed with the grim concentration of somebody moving bricks.

June said, without looking up, "Do you still have that portable charger thing from your mom's trailer."

"In my apartment. Extremely helpful."

"I was asking."

"It sounded a lot like blame."

June straightened. Her wet hair had started to curl at the temples. "Everything sounds like blame to you right now."

"Maybe because we drove two hours to verify a sentence and the sentence lost."

"I said I know."

"Do you."

June shut the hood carefully, too carefully. "What exactly do you want me to say, Lena."

The cart kid came over before Lena could answer. He was maybe nineteen, in an orange poncho two sizes too big, with a healed skate scar cutting white through one eyebrow. He pushed a string of wet carts with one hand and took in the open hood, the dead car, Lena with the ice, June with her face set like she might punch weather.

"You need a jump?" he asked.

June exhaled through her nose. "If you've got one."

"Manager keeps a battery pack. Hold up."

He jogged back toward the store, poncho flapping around his knees. Lena watched him go because it was easier than looking at June.

After a minute June said, lower, "He could still be real."

Lena turned her head very slowly. The sentence used the same carpentry as *don't do the story where*: take her shame in both hands, narrow it, hand it back in a shape she could survive. That was the first sickening part. June's defense of him sounded like him.

June kept going, tidier now, every word set down like it was going to be audited. "He could've lied about the residency because he needed a story big enough to keep you from driving down here. He could be broke. He could've grabbed one floor photo from one job and leaned on it for a week. He could've cut a better life together out of scraps."

"Cut a better life together."

"You know what I mean."

Rain found the seam of Lena's collar and slid cold between her shoulder blades. June wasn't talking like somebody guessing anymore. She sounded like somebody explaining acceptable losses.

"No." She did, too well, and that made the word come out almost childish. "I actually don't anymore."

June's jaw worked once. "People make themselves look better online."

"People use old gym photos online. They don't invent an annex."

"He didn't invent your calls."

That should've been comfort. Instead Lena felt her stomach tip. Calls were not a defense unless June had already decided what counted as a person.

"No," Lena said. "Somebody made those."

That landed between them and stayed there, not yet accusation, no longer harmless. June's eyes flicked to her and then away.

The cart kid came back with a black jump pack and a middle-aged assistant manager in a store vest carrying an umbrella that was useless against the wind. The manager had one thumbnail painted glitter green and the rest bare. He clipped the leads on, told June to try it now, and the Corolla shuddered awake on the second turn.

"Don't shut it off till you're basically in Pennsylvania," he said.

June thanked him with a sincerity that would have charmed almost anybody. It did not charm Lena. The men headed back toward the store through the rain, already talking about a delivery truck.

June lowered the hood, got in, and left the engine running. Lena put the ice in the footwell because there was nowhere else to put it without touching June's groceries. She climbed in and shut the door. The car filled with heat that smelled faintly burnt.

For a while June drove with both hands at ten and two like a student driver taking a test. The groceries rustled every time they turned. One of the yogurt drinks rolled in the back and thudded softly against a loaf of bread.

At the first light out of the lot June said, "I'm not defending him because I'm stupid."

Lena looked out at the wet neon of a liquor store, the CLOSED flag plastered to its pole. "I didn't say you were stupid."

"No. You just make the air in the car say it for you."

"Then maybe crack a window."

June's laugh this time was one dry note and done. "Jesus."

They passed the turn that would have taken them toward the trailer park and kept going. Lena watched the sign disappear in the side mirror with a little stab of relief so sharp it embarrassed her. She was not ready to drag her mother's place into this too. She had already brought the ice.

June said, "You know what I think."

Lena kept her eyes on the side mirror until the trailer sign vanished completely.

"I think if he's lying, he's lying from underneath something. Money, probably. Shame. Maybe he thought if he told you the real job you'd hear the whole rest of his life in it and bounce."

The wipers slapped back and forth. A gull rose out of a puddled lot and then vanished in the rain.

"He got me to send money because of a smashed van window and a payroll freeze and his sister's ruined apartment," Lena said. Her voice sounded almost normal, which was worse. "At a certain point shame and business start wearing the same coat."

June's fingers tightened on the wheel. "I know."

That was the last full thing either of them said for a long time.

The silence did not arrive all at once. It came in pieces, taking the spots where their friendship usually lived. The comment about the billboard lawyer with the spray tan. The automatic ranking of shore grocery stores. The shared disgust when they passed a pickup with truck nuts shining wet as cherries. Any other drive, one of them would have said something. Any other drive, the other would have built on it. Now the words rose and died before they crossed the space between them.

By the time they hit the expressway, the car had become all mechanics: wipers, engine, the soft slosh from the half-melted ice at Lena's feet, the papery shift of grocery bags every time June changed lanes. Lena kept her phone facedown in her lap. It buzzed once somewhere near Deptford, a short bright insect sound, and neither of them looked at it.

Rain blurred the brake lights ahead into red smears. June reached to turn the heat down, then left her hand on the knob too long, as if she had forgotten what came after that motion. Lena watched the road signs count them north.

At the toll plaza June handed over cash without asking Lena to dig in the cup holder the way she usually did. The receipt fluttered onto the console and stayed there, dampening at one corner from the wet air. A song came on the radio low enough that only the bass made it through. June switched it off.

The city thickened around them by degrees: chain stores, gas stations, the first dirty walls of warehouses, a SEPTA bus hissing at a stop with its doors open to the rain. Home was coming. Lena did not feel relieved. She felt as if she had carried something heavy all the way to the shore only to discover it was hollow and then had been stupid enough to carry it back.

When they got off Broad and into the neighborhood grid, June missed the easy left she always took toward Moore.

Lena heard the signal clicking and said nothing.

June corrected at the next corner. "Sorry."

Lena looked at the wet windshield, at the pale ring where the old phone mount had marked the glass, at June's hands on the wheel, and kept her mouth shut.

That more than anything seemed to land.

The rest of the ride passed in the sound of rain and the patient engine, each block bringing them deeper into streets they both knew too well to need directions. Silence sat between them like a third person who had finally been invited in and had no intention of leaving.


# Chapter 13

The trailer always took a second shove after the key turned, as if Rosa were on the other side with her hip against it, making visitors prove they meant to come in. Lena leaned her shoulder into the aluminum door and the old mix hit her at once: salt dried into fabric, mildew in the bathroom grout, old sunscreen baked into vinyl, and beneath that the sweet powdery note that had been her mother's exact idea of clean. The place had cooled since summer, but the fiberglass walls still held heat the way a cheap pan held grease.

June came up behind her with two flattened banker boxes tucked under one arm and a grocery bag looped around the other wrist. She had on work boots with one split lace knotted back into usefulness and an Orioles cap so sweat-stained at the brim it looked found rather than owned, and a paring knife wrapped in a dish towel stuck out of the grocery bag like she was reporting for kitchen triage.

"There she is," June said, looking past Lena into the dim trailer. "Rosa's palace of mildew."

Lena stepped aside. "You say that like you aren't the one who insisted we come today."

"Because if I let you pick the date, we'd be here at Christmas finding religion in a Tupperware drawer."

They had spent most of the drive back down in a workable silence, the radio low, the traffic already thin in late October. After the wet stupidity of the Wildwood trip earlier in the week, silence had begun doing more than silence usually did. It had edges now. Still, June had shown up at seven with coffee and contractor bags and the kind of competence that always made a job look briefly survivable.

Inside, the trailer had the same stubborn arrangement Rosa had kept for years, as if she expected weather to negotiate with her if the cushions sat right. The dinette vinyl was printed with fake blue rope. The fridge stood open and unplugged, one back foot still propped on a folded ShopRite circular to stop the wobble. Twist ties hung looped on a nail near the sink. A casino cup on the counter held pens that had all dried out except one chewed-down marker. Rosa had gone to the hospital that spring and never come back, but the room kept her habits in position for her: breadbox empty and clean, dish towels folded in thirds, a coffee tin of spare change under the radio, the bathroom hand soap watered down to its last translucent inch.

June set the boxes down and opened windows in quick practiced snaps, tapping each latch twice when it stuck. "I brought real knives," she said. "Your mom believed in blades with the engineering integrity of butter."

"She said sawing builds character."

"That was because she liked watching other people work."

Lena smiled despite herself. It came easier in here than it had in the car. June belonged to this trailer nearly as much as she did. There had been whole Augusts when Rosa would take both girls down for three days at a time because it was cheaper than camps and safer than letting them roam the block with wet hair and opinions. June knew where Rosa hid the cards, which burner on the stove clicked twice before catching, how hard you had to slam the bathroom cabinet before the mirror stopped shivering.

They started in the back bedroom, which sounded more dignified than it was. It held two narrow beds and one dresser that never fully closed, with sand still trapped in the window track from one summer when Rosa had kept insisting she would come down one more time after chemo made food taste like nickels. Lena stripped fitted sheets that had gone cool and papery in the dark. June crouched by the dresser and came up with a stack of elastic-waist shorts, a church carnival visor, and a cosmetic bag gummy with exploded lip balm.

"She kept every free moisturizer anybody ever gave her," June said. "This woman would've survived the end times on hotel lotion and pepper packets."

"Don't throw out the little green tubes."

June looked up. "You want them?"

"No. I just don't want to watch you throw them out."

June gave her a quick sideways look, the kind that meant she'd heard the rule and wasn't going to test it. "Fine. Memory lotion can stay."

Lena turned to the closet because it was easier than explaining the difference between useless and unbearable. Rosa's beach chair still leaned in there, webbing frayed white at the edges. Above it sat the old plastic drawer unit where she kept towels, off-brand antacids, and the school things she had never thrown away. Lena pulled one drawer free and found what she had half expected: two plaid uniform skirts, a cardigan with the school crest half peeled off, and a manila envelope full of forms.

"Jesus," June said. "Look at us."

She shook out a class photo. Twelve-year-old Lena stood in the second row with her hair ironed straight for once, jaw set as if the camera had personally annoyed her. Next to her, June looked small and furious in the universal way of underfed Catholic-school girls, chin tipped up, shoelace untied.

"You looked cute," Lena said.

"I looked like I bit people."

"You did bite people."

"Only once, and she deserved it."

The laugh that came out of Lena was real enough that it surprised her. June heard the surprise and let it sit there without touching it. She sorted the forms with nicotine-yellowed fingertips, quick and neat. There were tuition notices, a field trip permission slip, and one of those annual registration packets with its stubborn little boxes for `Mother`, `Father`, `Parish`.

"Remember this shit," June said. "Like if they printed `Father` hard enough one was gonna appear."

Lena took the packet from her. Rosa had signed both lines in the same slanted hand, then crossed one out and written `guardian` in the margin. "My mom used to get mad at forms."

"Your mom got mad like it was a municipal service."

"And your mom?"

June shrugged one shoulder. "Left it blank till the office called. Then she'd write `N/A` so hard the pen cut the paper."

Lena could see June's mother at the Salazars' kitchen table, cigarette burning down in the ashtray, filling out school forms with the expression of someone being insulted by stationery. Outside, gulls kept making those ridiculous rusty-hinge sounds over a trailer park that was mostly shut down for the season. Somewhere farther off, a hammer hit aluminum siding in a dull patient rhythm.

June flipped to the next page. "Emergency contact. Also hilarious."

"Rosa used to put Mrs. DeSanto from upstairs."

"My mom put nobody. Which, to be fair, was accurate."

The tenderness in that could have turned sentimental if either of them looked at it too long. June saved them by tossing the papers into a keep pile and standing up with a grunt. "Kitchen before I lose the will to live."

The kitchen still held Rosa in smaller, meaner ways. There were rubber bands around the dish soap pump because she never trusted factory closures, a freezer shelf lined with paper towels to catch drips from a tray that had not held ice in months, bread stored in the refrigerator even in winter because she said shore air turned everything to sponge, and a margarine tub full of clothespins and two AA batteries. Lena touched the tub and felt, stupidly, as if the plastic might still be warm from her mother's hand.

June unpacked the grocery bag onto the counter: tomatoes wrapped in a deli sheet, white bread, a small jar of Hellmann's gone sweaty at the glass, pepper, salt, paper towels, the knife. "I wasn't eating one more stale pretzel on your emotional field trip."

"That was a research trip."

"That was hydroplaning into humiliation with you because you had a theory."

Lena flinched before she could stop it. June noticed. Of course she noticed. Her face changed for less than a second, the joke slipping off as she recalibrated.

"I brought lunch," she said, quieter. "Don't make me regret the tomatoes."

So Lena washed them at the sink while June found plates that had to be rinsed again for dust. The faucet coughed rust first, then cold clear water. Tomato skin gave under her thumbs with that faint waxy drag she had always loved. June cut thick slices, practical and unsentimental, and salted them from high up because she said it spread better that way. She spread mayo edge to edge on white bread and ground black pepper until the plate looked dirty.

"Your mom always overdid the pepper," Lena said.

"Your mom understood structure."

"My mom understood cheap lunches."

"Those are not different sciences."

They ate on the trailer steps because the light outside was too good to waste on fake blue rope vinyl. October had thinned the whole park down to exposed geometry: empty lots, shut shutters, a few stubborn geraniums going dark in planters, the narrow lane between trailers bright as a knife blade. The tomato sandwiches ran immediately. Seeds slid onto Lena's wrist. Mayo stuck to the heel of her hand. June ate in fast efficient bites, then licked pepper off her thumb and looked out toward the patch of scrub that hid the bay.

"Remember your mom making us hose our feet off before we came in?" she said.

"Because we tracked half the beach into the beds."

"No, because I tracked half the beach in and you followed me around like a witness."

"You always ran."

"I still do."

That landed lightly enough that Lena let it pass. June had already moved on, pointing with the crust of her sandwich at the trailer across the lane where an inflatable palm tree still leaned half dead against a deck. "Rosa loved making fun of those people."

"She said anything inflatable on purpose was an admission of defeat."

June laughed with her mouth full. For a minute they were exactly the girls they had been here: sunburned, sticky, talking shit before somebody called them inside. Lena could almost believe the silence from the drive had been only tiredness, the wet awful Jersey trip already flattening into anecdote.

Then June reached behind her for the next box to fill and caught hold of a shallow plastic file case Lena had set by the door. The lid popped open in her hands. Inside were envelopes labeled in Rosa's cramped block letters: `CAR`, `BILLS`, `LENA IMPORTANT`, `WARRANTY`, `CHURCH`, `DON'T SHRED`.

"God," June said, but this time she did not sound amused. She lifted the `LENA IMPORTANT` envelope between two fingers. "She archived your disasters."

Lena swallowed the last wet bite of tomato. "What?"

"I'm saying your mom saved every piece of paper connected to your life like she was building a case file for the defense." June thumbed through the contents. There was a dental bill, an old lease addendum, and a postcard from the apartment Lena had shared with her ex and hated almost immediately. "Look at this. She gave your mess its own category."

"Everybody has paperwork."

"Not like this."

The air had shifted so gradually Lena only noticed once it was different. The gulls had gone quiet. Somewhere a screen door banged twice in the wind. June set the papers down in a neat stack that looked angrier than a mess would have.

"What are you actually saying?" Lena asked.

June took a breath through her nose. When she spoke again the joke was gone cleanly, as if she had put it away for later. "I'm saying the buyer paperwork in your tote says eighteen-six after fees, and everybody gets to call that sad money because it came out of your mother. In September DeLuca taped a red notice to my basement door for eighteen hundred in back rent and fees, and by dinner I'd had three people explain cigarettes to me like nicotine was why Mateo needed a place to sleep."

Lena stared at her. "That's bullshit."

"Is it?"

"Yeah, June, it is."

Lena felt the vinyl edge of the stair tread pressing a hard line into the backs of her thighs. Tomato seeds were drying on her wrist. "That eighteen-six is already taxes, Visa, and whatever winter costs. It's not padding. And when DeLuca pulled that shit, I told you he was a parasite."

"I know."

June reached for the pepper and screwed the lid on with more care than the task required. "You told me in your kitchen. The school office said it to my face. Mateo misses enough mornings to get put on attendance probation, and that vice principal asks if there's a more stable adult in the house now. Same year your husband leaves and Rosa fronts the Moore deposit, people carry your boxes upstairs and call you brave for landing on your feet."

"Rosa loaned me that deposit," Lena said. "I paid her back in caption checks, and I didn't say one thing about you over Mateo. I said that office was treating him like a threat and you like bad wiring."

June's voice had gone cooler, more precise, which was always worse than when she raised it. Lena could hear the care with which she selected each word, the way she did when she was pinning down a contractor on the phone or explaining to Mateo why a thing could not happen the way he wanted it to.

"I'm not saying you had it easy," June said. "I'm saying even when you hit the sidewalk, the story lands on what happened to you. Mine lands on what I did wrong."

Lena let out a short unbelieving sound. "What the fuck does that even mean?"

"It means Farah drops soup when you stop functioning. I get asked for receipts."

The sentence sat between them with the impossible solidity of furniture. Lena could have argued each line item. She wanted dates and counterexamples and a fair hearing and maybe even a spreadsheet, which was how she knew she was hurt. But the trailer did not make room for anything clean enough to call abstract. The place smelled too strongly of dead sunscreen and damp paper. Her mother's labeled envelopes were right there on the step between them, proof of a form of care so specific it almost looked like surveillance.

"You're acting like I won something," she said finally. "My mother died. The trailer's getting sold. None of this feels padded."

June looked out at the empty lane instead of at her. "No," she said. "But even grief came with assets."

Lena stared.

June rubbed the side of her thumb over the serrated edge of the paper plate. "Forget it."

"Don't do that."

"Do what?"

"Say something ugly and then act like I'm dramatic for hearing it."

Now June looked at her. Her face had gone unreadable in the tired way Lena knew from years of late nights and bad jobs and family emergencies that were never allowed to become true emergencies because June kept getting there first. "I said what I said."

Lena wanted to stand up, or throw the plate, or force the thing open until it broke into usable pieces. Instead she wiped her hand on a paper towel that immediately tore. "Fine," she said, and heard how flat it sounded.

They cleaned up lunch in a silence not nearly blank enough to count as peace. June washed the knife and dried it on the dish towel she'd brought from home. Lena carried three trash bags to the dumpster and came back sweating inside her sweatshirt even though the day had turned. When she passed the bedroom doorway she saw June kneeling by a box of keepsakes, wrapping framed photos in newspaper with the same efficient hands that had once braided Lena's hair before the eighth-grade dance because Rosa had been at work late. The sight should have softened something. Instead it made Lena think, with a quick cold shame, of all the ways care could also be a way of learning where a person broke.

By the time the sun had shifted off the steps, the trailer was half emptied and Lena could not stop hearing the morning again with different captions under it. Jokes she had known for years came back labeled in another voice. Whole stretches of friendship started to look itemized.

***

By late afternoon the place had that flat October light Rosa always hated, the kind that made even clean rooms look vaguely condemned. Empty surfaces gave sound back too fast. A hanger clacked in the closet and it was suddenly the loudest thing in the trailer. Lena carried a box of kitchen things to the car, came back for another, and realized the rooms were already beginning to feel less like absence than inventory.

June was in the bathroom on her knees, unscrewing the cheap plastic caddy from the wall because she said the buyers would try to charge for anything left attached. "They're gonna act like this thing is custom millwork," she called.

"Leave it," Lena said.

"No. It'll take thirty seconds."

"June."

June sat back on her heels and looked over her shoulder. "Len. I'm saving you from bureaucratic theater. Let me have this."

The almost-joke in it was careful, offered rather than thrown. Lena let it stand. She went into the back bedroom to clear the closet's top shelf and found, shoved behind a beach bag, Rosa's blue quilted housecoat still on its plastic hanger. The cheap satin had gone dull at the seams. One cuff was darkened where sunscreen or gravy or both had once met fabric and then become permanent. Lena pulled it down and the pockets made a little crackling sound.

She knew before she checked that something would be inside, probably tissues or a receipt or a cough drop fused to the lining. Rosa never used a pocket once.

Lena slipped her hand into the right pocket and came out with two hard tissues, a casino players card, and three strawberry candies stuck together in a red glossy lump.

The sight of them hit in a place that had nothing to do with reason. It was too exactly her mother: Rosa keeping purse-candy in a housecoat she wore to fry flounder, Rosa pocketing things to deal with later and then leaving them there forever, Rosa in this trailer with the TV loud and the air conditioner rattling, shouting for Lena to close the damn screen before the moths got ideas.

Lena sat down on the edge of the stripped bed because standing had become a complicated instruction. The mattress bowed without sheets over the plastic cover Rosa believed in on principle. The housecoat lay across Lena's lap, slick and cool as fish skin. When she brought it closer the old powder smell was still there under everything else, mixed now with closet dark and the medicinal sweetness of the candies.

June appeared in the doorway and took the whole picture in at once. She did not ask if Lena was okay. One of the reasons Lena had loved her so long was that she usually knew better than that.

"Oh," June said, seeing the candies. "Jesus."

Lena laughed once and the laugh broke halfway through. "Of course."

June leaned her shoulder against the doorframe. "She kept those for me."

Lena looked up.

June nodded at the sticky red lump in Lena's palm. "Those awful strawberry things. She knew I'd steal two before I even sat down. She used to hide them in whatever pocket she had because Gabe would wipe out the whole bowl."

The memory entered the room so plainly that Lena could see it: June at fourteen, all elbows and appetite, checking Rosa's robe pockets before she said hello. Rosa smacking her hand away without conviction. The trailer then still full of wet towels and radio static and the smell of oil heating in a pan.

"You always wanted the mints," June said.

"I still want the mints."

"Which is insane. Those taste like church vestibules."

Lena looked at the candies again. Her eyes were running now, enough that the red cellophane blur doubled. She wiped her face with the heel of her hand and immediately got old powder on her skin from the sleeve. "I can't tell what's trash in here."

"Then don't decide right this second."

June came into the room, took the hanger from Lena with a gentleness so practical it barely announced itself, and laid the housecoat flat on the bed. She patted the pockets once, found another tissue, and threw it away. "Take the robe," she said. "If you leave it, you'll think about it every time it gets cold."

"It smells weird."

"Yeah. That's the point."

Lena laughed again, more cleanly this time. June folded the robe into a square that was not pretty but would fit in a box. Then she picked up the strawberry candies and held them out.

"You want these?"

"Absolutely not."

June slipped them into the front pocket of her hoodie. "Then I do."

That, more than anything, steadied Lena. Gross pocket candy was June's kind of mercy: no ceremony, no speech about memory or healing or taking what remains. Lena wiped her nose on a paper towel and stood up.

"You gonna cry again if I take the ugly useful stuff out of the kitchen?" June asked.

"Probably."

"Good. Stay hydrated."

Back in the main room, they finished the last hour in a version of teamwork so old it felt like muscle memory. June stacked plates by size and rejected sentimentality with a running low commentary. "Keep the opener. Nobody ever regrets a can opener. Leave the shell lamp, it has bad intentions. Take the old towels. Ugly useful stuff is all that survives families." Lena wrapped mugs in the advertising circulars Rosa had saved under the sink. The paper left ink on her fingertips. Outside, the light thinned further. A few year-round residents were already pulling down flags and folding chairs, whole lives reduced for weather.

In the silverware drawer Lena found a Polaroid curled at the corners: herself and June on the trailer steps in bathing suits, both trying not to grin and failing. Lena's hair was plastered flat from the bay. June had one knee skinned open and did not seem remotely bothered by it. On the white strip at the bottom Rosa had written `my girls` in blue ballpoint.

Lena held it a second longer than she meant to. Then June reached across, took it from her, and slid it carefully into the side pocket of Lena's tote without comment.

For a minute after that, something almost simple returned. Innocence was long gone, maybe since earlier that day, maybe months ago. What came back was an older habit of alliance. June carried the heavier box without being asked. Lena found the trailer keys on the hook by the door, looped through a faded dolphin float from some long-closed mini-golf place, and dropped them into her pocket. June remembered the buyer paperwork. Lena remembered the phone charger. Between them, the job kept moving.

When the last box was in the trunk and only the cleaning bucket remained by the door, June lit a cigarette outside by the gravel strip, one hand cupped against the wind. She almost never smoked in front of Mateo and had gotten sneaky about it in the city, but down here, with the trailer half stripped and the lane nearly empty, she did not bother pretending anything. The smoke came off sharp and ugly in the salt air.

"Five minutes," she said. "Then we lock up."

Lena nodded and went back inside for one final walk-through. The counters were empty, the beds were bare, and the fridge had been wiped out so hard it smelled faintly of bleach and old onion. Without the dish towels and the radio and the half-useful clutter, the place looked smaller than grief had allowed. She touched the dinette table once on the way past, the fake blue rope pattern slick under her palm.

Her phone buzzed in her back pocket.

The sound was so ordinary it took a second to feel invasive. It was Signal, from Eli.

She had barely answered him since the shore. He had responded to that new thinness the way he responded to everything at first, with patience so calibrated it nearly counted as style. He had sent a check-in text in the morning and a picture of bad coffee from somewhere he claimed to be loading out, all of it carrying the same no-pressure, baby, just-here ease. Today she had left him unopened since nine.

Now there were two messages and a voice note.

`You still down there?`

`Please tell me you ate something.`

The voice note was nineteen seconds. Lena did not want to play it. She played it anyway.

His voice came warm and low into the stripped trailer, too close to the ear as always.

"Hey. Open a window in there, okay? That trailer traps heat. Eat a real thing before you drive. And don't leave the ugly useful stuff behind just because it looks like junk. Bring home what still works. Call me when you're moving."

Lena stood very still with the phone at her ear long after the note ended.

Outside, gravel crunched under June's boots. The cigarette ember flared once, then dropped. Inside, the trailer gave back Eli's silence as if it had belonged in the room all along.

The phrase kept returning: ugly useful stuff.

June had said it less than an hour ago, standing at the sink with a stack of plates in her hands. Maybe Lena had given Eli that phrase months ago. She had given him enough to build a whole man out of. That was part of the problem. But now, in the emptied trailer with her mother's robe boxed up and the smell of old powder still on her wrist, the note did not sound contaminated by memory alone. It was the ranked little orders in it, the brisk practical mercy, the way care arrived as triage. The phrase was June's, but so was the posture under it. Eli's softer voice only made the theft easier to hear.

Proof would've been cleaner. Contamination got into everything.

"Len?" June called from outside. "You coming?"

Lena locked the screen, set the phone facedown on the dinette for one second, then picked it back up. "Yeah," she said.

She turned off the last light though there was still enough day to see. At the door she looked back once more at the trailer's narrowed rooms, all the old care marks still visible even with the objects gone: the circular under the fridge foot, the rubbed-clean patch by the light switch, the hairline crack in the cabinet mirror where Rosa had shut it too hard for years. Love stayed in a place like that as wear. Use stayed there too. Habit stayed there too.

Outside, June ground out the cigarette and held out her hand for the keys without thinking. Lena almost gave them over. Then she stopped, curled the dolphin float deeper into her own fist, and said, "I've got it."

June's hand dropped at once. "Okay."

Lena locked the door herself. The key bit her palm on the turn. By the time she got into the passenger seat for the ride back, every kind thing June had ever remembered about her seemed to have acquired a second use.


# Chapter 14

When the power died, the room got louder.

She'd been back from the trailer less than two hours, just long enough to open the laptop and start pretending work was a cleaner problem.

The fan in Lena's window clicked once and quit. The tailor's steam press downstairs gave a last insect whine and went dead. Rain, which had been sitting behind all the electrical noise for an hour, came forward and took the apartment back. It hit the glass in slanted handfuls. Somewhere on Moore, a man said ah, come on like the storm had singled him out for insult.

Lena stared at her laptop screen while the battery icon went red. The caption file she had spent six hours cleaning had exported clean at last. The invoice had not gone out. The client portal had logged her off when the router died, and the PDF sat in downloads like an accusation: 3:14:22 of rush cleanup, medical webinar, $684 due. If she sent it tonight, there was a chance the client paid before the weekend and Mrs. Alvarado did not have to slide December's rent reminder under Lena's door with that brisk pink Post-it she used when she wanted to be polite about pressure. If she did not send it, the money would move to next week, which meant the card minimum, which meant groceries with a calculator out, which meant one more stupid month of being impressed by canned beans.

The battery hit three percent.

She tried the hotspot on her phone even though the service had already started stuttering. Nothing. The login wheel spun and spun. Her charger was useless without power. Her phone was at eleven percent and dropping, because of course tonight had to be the night she had spent her last wall charge on a reheated bowl of caption revisions and not on foresight.

She looked at the black square of her window. Across the street, half the block was dark. The deli still glowed at the corner, dirty yellow and miraculous. One block south, the lights on the cross street were still burning. June's block. Of course.

Her first thought was practical and old enough to live below thought: go to June.

The second thought came after it, slower and meaner. After the shore. After the phrase overlap. After the trailer, with June talking about money like she was picking at a scab with a thumbnail. Lena had not said any of that aloud. She had let suspicion sit in her like bad shellfish and told herself that suspicion was not proof, that grief and embarrassment could make patterns out of steam. But now, in the dark, with the unpaid invoice on her dead machine and rain needling the window, her body still reached for the oldest route. June had her spare keys. June had once bicycled to a Rite Aid at midnight for tampons and a heating pad when Lena was nineteen and folded up with cramps on a bathroom floor. June was where practical emergency went.

Muscle memory beat doubt by half a second. Half a second was enough.

Lena grabbed her phone, the charger, the printed caption notes she had been using as backup, and went downstairs. The tailor shop gate was half-pulled already, Mr. Velez bent under it with a flashlight in his teeth, cursing softly in Spanish. On the sidewalk the rain smelled like wet brick, gutter metal, and old fryer grease washing loose from the corner. Water ran black along the curb. Her sneakers took it in at once.

June buzzed her in before Lena had to text twice.

"You're alive," June called from below. "Come down before the stairs decide they're a waterslide."

The basement apartment was cooler than the street in a way that felt wrong in late November, damp-cold instead of weather-cold. It smelled like coffee gone stale in the pot, nicotine that had seeped into fabric years ago and was never fully leaving, and the hot plastic breath of too many surge protectors under one desk. Cords looped from the folding table to the wall like black vines. Two old laptops sat stacked beside a cracked monitor. A sneaker tray by the door held June's work boots, a pair of Mateo's beat-up black-and-white trainers, and three loose screws for no reason Lena could see. June herself was in gray sweats and a Sixers hoodie with one cuff dark from rain. A bodega receipt was tucked under the elastic of her watchband, safe there like she had run out of pockets halfway through the day.

"Only my block's out," Lena said. Her hair was already sticking damp to the back of her neck. "I just need ten minutes. I've got to send an invoice before I turn into a pumpkin or a delinquent."

"Those are the same thing in this neighborhood." June shut the door with her heel. "Use the silver one. Personal, not the work brick. Power hiccupped and the work brick started timing out on the VPN, so I dumped half my browser shit onto it. Don't open anything minimized unless you want to read property managers crying about package lockers. If you want coffee, don't. It's disgusting."

She said it lightly, fast the way she always did when she was making a mess sound temporary. Still, Lena heard the tiredness under it. June's face had that flat, unreadable look it got when she had not slept enough and had no intention of letting anyone name it.

"You okay?" Lena asked, and hated herself a little for how automatic it sounded.

"Landlord texted that the back window well is taking water." June rolled her eyes, already reaching for a flashlight from the kitchen counter. "So now apparently I'm the Army Corps of Engineers. Send your thing. If the internet kicks, hit the router with the side of your hand. It likes disrespect."

"That's not how routers work."

"You need me to pretend I care?"

Under another set of circumstances Lena would have smiled. She almost did, and then did not. June had already turned away, jamming her feet into the work boots without untying them. She shoved her phone into the kangaroo pocket of the hoodie, patted for the flashlight, found it, and glanced back once.

"Door sticks if you leave before I get back. Pull up, then in."

"Okay."

"And don't open the tabs I shoved down into the dock unless you want to see me getting yelled at by property managers."

"Jesus."

"I'm serious. I was moving everything around when the water text hit. It'll ruin your night in a different way."

Then she was up the short basement stairs and gone, the outer door banging once behind her. Rain thickened against the little high windows at street level. Somebody upstairs dragged something heavy over tile. The apartment settled around Lena with all June's systems still humming inside it.

She sat at the folding desk and touched the silver laptop awake.

The desktop was already open. The silver laptop still wore June's emergency reshuffle all over it. Her dead external monitor had collapsed two workspaces into one screen; one browser sat open on a KeyNest dashboard dense with maintenance tickets and reply templates, and another had been swiped down to a strip of icons at the bottom instead of logged out, as if she had meant to clean the whole thing up and then the landlord text had yanked her upstairs mid-motion. Slack bounced once in the dock. An email draft sat half-finished in June's inbox about key fob replacements and tenant abuse of package lockers. In another life it would have been funny how much of adulthood was just other people's access problems.

Lena opened a browser tab, logged into her invoicing portal, and used the printed notes in her lap to rebuild the line items she had lost when her own laptop died. Rush cleanup. Speaker IDs. Burned-in corrections after client review. She could do it blind. She had done versions of this blind, tired, bleeding, half-drunk, furious, ashamed. Work was often the only place her humiliation could not follow her because timecodes did not care what had happened to her.

She attached the PDF. She filled in the amount. She hit send.

The page thought about it long enough to annoy her. Then a green bar rolled across the top.

Invoice sent.

She had just enough time to feel the cheap, mean relief of it when a notification slid up in the corner of the screen, pale gray on black.

Manual review suggested: eli_ward / target instability spike / contradiction event unresolved.

Lena's finger stayed on the trackpad. She read it once, then again, slower. Manual review suggested. Eli Ward. Target instability spike.

The notification stayed on screen long enough to become rude.

She clicked it because there was no world in which she did not.

The minimized window opened full across the laptop. At the top left, in small clean type, the platform name sat where a Gmail tab should have been: OpenClaw Studio. Beneath it, in a darker strip, a workspace title: eli_ward. At the far right of the header, next to a round avatar with June's initials in it, a green dot glowed beside Active.

For a second Lena's mind tried to build a stupid, harmless explanation. June used OpenClaw for work. June automated things. June had probably named some internal test after Eli because Lena had been talking about him for months and June had a sick sense of humor.

Then her eyes adjusted to the rest.

Below the header, a status ribbon ran across the screen.

mode: mixed autonomy
manual escalation triggers: money / contradiction / silence

The left rail listed folders and modules in a tree:

Identity shell
Source media
Voice assets
Memory bank
Live threads
Repair loops
Payments
Manual overrides

Under Live threads, one item was expanded. lena_signal.

Under it, in smaller gray text, a freshness marker updated in real time. Last inbound: 17m ago.

Her own breath made a tiny damp mark on the screen before she leaned back. It would not have landed if she had been farther away. She had been that close already.

The main pane held a branching map in colored boxes linked by fine white lines. It looked, obscenely, like subtitle software. A timeline. Speaker tracks. Conditional branches. Only instead of names like Moderator or Q&A 2, the boxes said things Lena recognized with an animal drop in her stomach.

App meet cute
Voice note hook
Late-night exclusivity
Family legitimation
Video intimacy
Minor rescue ask
Union Station salvage / Noelle crisis
Larger emergency / payroll freeze
Shore trip repair
Suspicion management

Each box could be opened. Some were green, some yellow, one red. Little rule badges sat under them: delay window, tone pack, follow-up threshold. Eli had not been improvised one message at a time. He had been kept warm between interventions.

Lena sat very still. The folding chair dug a square edge into the backs of her thighs. Rainwater from her jeans wicked into the vinyl seat. She could hear a sump pump somewhere in the building kicking on and off with a wet, exhausted cough.

She clicked Manual overrides because it was the only label on the rail that admitted a human hand.

The log loaded with dates, timestamps, and operator initials.

09/06/27 8:12 PM - JS manual override
video intimacy. No "beautiful." She won't buy it tonight. Use forearms / knees / how she listens.

09/06/27 8:17 PM - JS manual override
If she freezes, tell her to leave the lamp on. She hates being looked for and not at.

09/14/27 6:41 PM - JS manual inject
van window branch. Resist first offer. Accept on second. Keep pride intact.

09/14/27 6:49 PM - JS manual inject
Keep it in minor-rescue range. Accept with embarrassment. Voice note after transfer.

10/03/27 5:06 PM - JS manual inject
larger ask this week. Need urgency that allows delay + shame. Payroll freeze cleaner than theft repeat.

10/03/27 5:09 PM - JS private note
DeLuca won't wait. Get there.

Lena made a sound she had never heard herself make, not a gasp and not a word. Something small being torn.

She kept reading because now stopping would have been the same as mercy, and mercy had missed its window.

10/17/27 11:22 PM - JS manual override
dog test. Do not challenge memory. Fold it into care. Make her feel seen, not corrected.

11/02/27 3:14 PM - JS manual override
shore trip blowback. Admit nothing. Let him sound tired. More static on calls.

11/18/27 9:03 PM - JS manual note
friendship suspicion rising. Remember she still comes to me for practical help. That habit is older than this.

Lena shut her eyes, but it was there too: not only the romance, not only the money, but June's written architecture of Lena's reflexes, laid out like a tradesperson measuring a doorway before bringing in a couch.

When she opened her eyes again, the room had narrowed to the desk, the laptop, the water stain spreading from the hem of her jeans, the chipped mug full of pens and one tiny screwdriver, the pale groove around her ring finger where Rosa's signet had been for years before she walked it into the pawn shop under fluorescent lights and let a man with paper skin weigh her mother in grams.

She skipped Memory bank. She already knew June had her facts. What she had not known until now was how completely June had fed them into his mouth.

Voice assets was docked beside Source media, paired tabs like bad twins. Under the header, the categories ran in a row: cleaned stems, accent training, breath beds, laughter variants.

There was a clip labeled morning-rough-02, another labeled tired-sincere-05, and a third labeled laugh-soft-no-snort. On the source side, folders opened down the left with neat names:

asher_quinn_press_2019
forearm_refs
tour_bus_broll
late_night_voice_pack
tattoo_stills
dc_loading_dock_loops

In the main pane, thumbnails populated: a young white man behind a drum kit, half-turned toward stage light; an interview clip with the audio stripped and cleaned; stills of tattooed arms from what looked like three different bodies; grainy backstage footage cropped so tight a human could become a mood. One file name carried the same drummer name Farah had found in October. Another had a note beside it: jawline useful / don't overuse left profile.

Eli's face had not even belonged to one stranger. It had been cut together out of several.

Under a subfolder called intimacy prompts sat a text file preview, visible without opening all the way.

If she gets self-conscious on camera, slow down.
Praise what is specific, not what sounds hungry.
She won't believe "gorgeous" when she's ashamed.
Try:
- I like the way your forearms look when you're bracing yourself.
- Keep the lamp. I want to see you.
- You don't have to hide your stomach from me.

Below that, one final line in June's shorthand:
If knee scar shows, ask where she got it. She likes being remembered in the body.

Lena jerked her hand back from the trackpad as if it had shocked her. The scar on her left knee was from sixth grade, from falling on church steps in a plaid skirt while June laughed first and then helped her up and picked gravel out of the skin with the corner of a tissue in the girls' bathroom. It was not bedroom data. It was not a usable trait. It was hers and June's and a stupid old afternoon and now it lived here under intimacy prompts like a button to press.

The rain hit harder. Overhead, somebody shouted for a bucket. A siren went past on Broad, blurred by water until it sounded almost underwater itself. Lena pressed her thumb into the meat of her palm hard enough to feel bone. It gave her something that was still hers.

Her phone, face down by the keyboard, began to buzz. It buzzed once, then again. It was a message from Eli.

You okay over there? Storm looks ugly. Keep your phone charged for me.

For one second she only stared at the words because they were so perfectly of him, of course they were, warm and lightly bossy and pitched exactly at the version of care that had always slid under her guard. Then the laptop chimed.

In the upper right corner of the OpenClaw window, a small activity panel expanded on its own.

Autonomy action executed: check-in / target silent 17m / thread lena_signal.
Output source: warm-concern-v3 + storm context.
Delivered.

The same sentence she held in her hand sat there in miniature, timestamped to the second.

Lena did not have to infer anything anymore. She did not have to be fair. She did not have to tell herself grief was making patterns or loneliness was making monsters or maybe June had built a model for some other idiot woman named Lena Cordero who captioned medical webinars and once showed her left knee on camera to a man who did not exist. The machine on June's desk had just spoken to her in Eli's voice while June was upstairs with a flashlight and a landlord and a flooded window well.

Payments sat in the left rail with a green dot beside it.

She opened it anyway, because if she left the money untouched some stupid merciful corner of her would keep pretending the fraud had stopped at words and skin.

It was not a ledger exactly but something worse: a set of scenarios.

minor rescue - pride maintained
public humiliation salvage
family emergency extension
payroll delay / reimbursement promise
cooldown after transfer
reactivation after silence

Each one opened into scripts, timing suggestions, and little decision trees. Some were automated conditions. Some were plainly June's hand.

For minor rescue:
Never request outright.
Signal damage, refuse once.
Accept only after target insists.
Immediate return: gratitude + masculine embarrassment + concrete future plan.

For larger emergency:
Use already-established collateral figure (Noelle).
Frame ask as temporary bridge, not need.
Known barriers: taxes 4.2k; trailer proceeds approx. 18.6k / "mom money."
Target more likely to cross protected-money line if she believes she is saving housing, not career.
Do not stack with sex. She'll feel bought.

And under that, in the right column, a section labeled target payment behavior.

first transfer / minor rescue
relief + concealment from family predicted

larger transfer / payroll delay frame
severe shame after; maintain warmth, reduce availability 5-7 days

Below it sat a note:
