She will work extra shifts instead of naming regret. Let her.

The groove around her ring finger seemed to fill with cold. She could see the pawnshop scale again, the tiny digital readout deciding what her mother came to.

Lena looked away from the screen and saw nothing in the room that was not suddenly witness: the screwdriver in the mug, Mateo's trainers by the door, a dish towel striped green and white over the sink, a half-empty box of Saltines. June's whole ordinary basement life still sat there, stubbornly itself, while inside one laptop she had turned Lena into intake fields and thresholds and expected yield.

She lifted her phone and took pictures of the screen because some part of her was still practical enough to know that horror got slippery later. She started with the status ribbon and its mixed autonomy label, then the manual override that mentioned DeLuca by name, then the intimacy prompt with the knee scar, then the payment behavior pane with its protected-money language sitting there like a shop tag, then the activity panel, still glowing Delivered.

Her battery dropped to eight percent.

Footsteps thudded above. The basement door opened. June came down hard and fast, shaking rain from her sleeves, flashlight clamped under her arm now. Her cheeks were pink from the cold. She was talking before she hit the last stair.

"He put a bath towel in the window well like water respects effort. You send it?"

Lena was still holding the phone up. The laptop was still open between them, bright as a stage. June looked at the screen, and the whole apartment changed shape.

It did not happen dramatically; there was no confession, no dropped glass, no theatrics. June just stopped one step short of the floor. The flashlight slid against her side and knocked once against the banister. Her mouth closed. That was all.

For months Lena had been living inside the voice of a man carefully designed to answer her. Now there was only June in a wet hoodie, one hand still on the rail, the bodega receipt softening under her watchband, and the laptop between them with all the borrowed mouths open at once.

Lena looked from June's face to the green dot beside Active and back again. The certainty landed all at once, hard and graceless.

She knew.


# Chapter 15

By eight in the morning Lena had turned the romance into folders.
She had not slept.

The power was back on. The radiator under the window still knocked like a bad argument, and the tailor downstairs had already started his machine, that fast insect stitch coming up through the floorboards. Her apartment smelled like old coffee, wet wool, and the hot plastic note from the power strip she'd overloaded during the outage. She sat at her desk in yesterday's jeans, one sock still damp at the heel, and opened the zip file she'd exported from Signal at three-thirteen in the morning because sleep had become a stupid idea.

`eli_full_export`.

She had named it that six weeks ago, when the archive had still felt like a keepsake instead of evidence.

There were subfolders for voice notes, image attachments, video-call screenshots she had taken because she liked the look of his face going soft when he listened, and PDFs of payment confirmations she had told herself she was saving for taxes. Her own job had taught her how to make a mess searchable, how to normalize filenames, stamp dates, and put the ugly thing where you could find it again. Now she sat in the same swivel chair where she captioned depositions and cooking demos and drunks fighting outside corner stores, and she started at the top.

The first entry was August 28, a forty-three-second voice note.

This time she pressed play before she could hide in the transcript. His voice came out of the laptop thin with compression but still intimate enough to get under her skin. "Closed captions are witchcraft, Lena. You hear the beat change before the rest of us know we're in a different song." On the second syllable of her name, the muscle under her left shoulder blade knotted so hard her hand slipped on the mouse. His laugh came after it, soft and pleased and instantly unbearable. She killed the file at eleven seconds, but the breath tucked inside that laugh kept scraping around the room. Last night, on June's laptop, Lena had seen a prompt branch titled RHYTHM / WORK FLIRT and had almost thrown up on the keys.

She kept going.

She made a legal pad into columns because if she did not put the facts somewhere outside her body they kept moving around in it. She labeled them Date, Artifact, Money, and Notes. Her handwriting went small and mean.

August 31: first video call, one hour twelve minutes.

September 4: photo of his wrist backstage with a Sharpie set list on it. Stolen, obviously. Or fabricated. Or both.

September 7: six screenshots of him grinning, head tipped, telling her to stop hiding in the dark.

September 15: Cash App transfer, $380. Van window. Sound kit. "You really don't have to do this, Lena."

September 29: Venmo, $460. Noelle's lock change and a motel because the ex knew where she was.

October 8: bank wire, $2,400. DeLuca. Payroll clearing Friday. Just a bridge.

Total: $3,240.

The number sat on the page looking fake in a different way than Eli had. It was three thousand two hundred and forty dollars, enough to take the tax plan she'd been nursing since August and kick it back under water. The money she had sent him had shape now. It was not romance. It was a whole season's margin gone.

She went back to the files.

The worst part was not the emergencies. In daylight every one of them showed its costume seams: Noelle's wrecked apartment, DeLuca with his hand out, the cracked phone, the cousin who could front a room but not until Tuesday. The small proud refusals were arranged so that Lena would have to cross the line herself and call it love while she did it. Once she knew what she knew, the choreography was almost ugly enough to be funny. He always pulled away half an inch before the ask, let her feel the distance, then let her close it with money.

The worst part was the ordinary tenderness packed around the theft like tissue paper around glass.

Eat something for me before your shift.

Put your hand under your own neck if it starts hurting. You hunch when you're tired.

Send me the street sound from outside your window. I want your block in my ear.

Those were the lines that made her shove back from the desk until the chair wheels complained. June had known the neck thing since they were fourteen, since study hall in the back of Saint Agnes when Lena used to fall asleep over algebra and wake with a hot crooked line through one shoulder. June had been on that block a hundred late nights, smoking under the tailor's dark awning, listening to scooters spit past and men argue about the Phillies under somebody's open kitchen window. She had stocked the lie with real inventory. That was why it had fit.

Lena put both palms flat on the desk until the cheap veneer edge bit into the heel of her hands. Outside, a delivery truck backfired on Moore and somebody yelled to watch the mirror. The city kept sounding like itself. That was almost insulting.

She opened the folder called `favorites`.

There were thirty-two voice notes in there. She had dragged them in over months the way other people might keep ticket stubs or pressed flowers. One was from the night he had asked her what her mother's kitchen sounded like before anybody was dead. Another came from Union Station, sent after she'd been standing in the bar in the dress with the tags tucked inside, mascara lifting into the corners of her eyes, while he texted about Noelle and broken cabinets and a dead phone battery. Another was from Saint Rocco night, where you could hear the crowd under him and he laughed when one of Gabe's girls shouted into the phone. She had saved that one because he sounded so unembarrassed by children, so easy inside noise.

She clicked into the photo roll instead. That seemed safer. It wasn't.

The first explicit image he had sent her was still there, though she had cropped it to his collarbone and mouth because that had felt less desperate somehow. The first one she had sent back was not there. She had deleted her copy the next morning and then spent three days regretting the deletion because she wanted proof that she had looked like a woman who did things on purpose. But the conversation around it remained. Tilt the camera lower. No, keep the light where it is. There, that's you. Fuck, Lena. Stay there.

She read the lines and remembered the bathroom tile cold through the towel she had folded twice and put under her lower back because the bed faced the wrong way for the angle he wanted. She remembered the phone propped against the mouthwash bottle and the ring light she had borrowed from her own work drawer because the ceiling fixture made her look gray. She remembered the soft ferocious concentration in his face when he told her not to disappear on him now, not when she had finally let him see her. At the time it had felt like he was holding her still so she would not talk herself back into hiding. Now the memory opened underneath and showed its wiring.

June had seen her apartment a thousand times. June knew exactly where the bathroom mirror had started foxing at the edges. June knew about the scar on Lena's left knee from falling off the Corderos' concrete steps when she was eight. June knew she hated the look of her own stomach from overhead and would believe anybody who said the softer angle was beautiful if they said it with enough patience. Lena reread the little flood of praise that followed that night, the language so observant it had felt holy at the time, and all at once it smelled not like sex but like the inside of June's basement apartment: nicotine in old fabric, laptop heat, stale seltzer, some machine breathing through the night.

She got up too fast and caught the desk with her thigh.

In the bathroom the ring light still hung on the hook behind the door, its cord kinked where she'd wrapped it after the third or fourth call. She had not touched it in weeks because suspicion had made everything involving Eli feel staged even before proof. Now she pulled it down and found one of her hairs still caught in the little tripod clamp. She stood there with the thing in her hand, the cheap pink plastic warm from the radiator pipe, and wanted to smash it into the sink. Instead she set it in the bathtub and stared at it like it might explain how a woman could fuck a machine and have the humiliation arrive months later wearing her best friend's face.

Her phone buzzed on the desk.

It was not Eli or June. It was Farah.

You alive?

The message had come in twenty minutes earlier. Lena must have missed the vibration under the radiator racket. She stared at it a long time. Farah had texted last night too, just a question mark when Lena had blown off her shift with a weak excuse about the outage and a migraine. Farah was the only person besides June who had ever said the name Asher Quinn out loud around her, holding up a reverse image search on the shop computer and looking embarrassed for the internet itself.

Lena typed three versions before she sent one.

Can I come by? Need to tell you something ugly.

Farah replied before the typing bubble had time to feel lonely.

I'm here. Back room if you don't want an audience.

Lena shut the laptop but did not close it all the way. The legal pad remained on the desk with the number at the bottom of the page. Three thousand two hundred and forty. She put the ring light back on the bathroom hook because breaking it would be theater and she did not have the energy for theater. Then she pulled on her boots, shoved the phone and the legal pad into her tote, and left the apartment without deleting a single thing.

***

Farah was relabeling a stack of Portuguese reds when Lena came in, peeling a clementine with one thumbnail while she worked the pricing gun with the other hand. She wore her father's old navy fisherman sweater over a black T-shirt, one elbow darned in yellow thread that did not match. The shop smelled like cardboard, cork dust, cold concrete, and the bright clean tear of citrus oil. A delivery invoice was clipped to the register with so many annoyed pen marks through it that the paper had gone furry.

Farah looked up once, took in Lena's face, and reached over without speaking to flip the sign to BACK IN FIVE.

"Come on," she said.

The back room was cramped with extra stock and the mop sink, but it was private. Farah shoved a case of cheap prosecco off the folding chair with her boot so Lena could sit. Then she set the half-peeled clementine in Lena's hand as if passing evidence.

"Eat that while you talk."

Lena laughed once, a bad little sound. "I don't think I can."

"Then hold it and smell it. Same principle."

Lena sat. The room hummed with the cooler on the other side of the wall. Somewhere out front a bottle clicked against another bottle as the shelves settled. Farah leaned back against the worktable, arms folded.

"Okay," Lena said. "I'm sorry. This is going to sound insane, and I need to say it in order or I'm going to lose it halfway through."

"Say it out of order," Farah said. "Start with whether he's real."

Clementine juice found the little split beside Lena's thumbnail and lit it up.

"He isn't," she said. "There wasn't ever a him."

Farah's face changed, but not into pity. It went flatter and more awake.

"All right."

"No, I mean it literally. It wasn't just fake photos. It was software. OpenClaw. June built him."

The silence after that was not the silence Lena had feared. It had no disbelief in it. No careful soft voice. No embarrassed smile somebody might use when a grown woman said she had been in love with a ghost in her phone. It was the silence of a woman doing sums very fast.

Farah said, "June Salazar."

"Yeah."

"As in June who borrows my dolly and never brings it back the same day."

"Yes."

Farah took the clementine back out of Lena's hand, tore it cleanly in half, and put it back. "Eat."

Lena actually obeyed this time because the room tilted a little when she stood still too long. The first burst of acid across her tongue made her eyes sting.

"How do you know?" Farah asked.

White threads of pith clung to Lena's thumb. "Because I saw June's laptop open in the storm and his name was on the workspace. `eli_ward`. Not just our chats. Folders of stolen clips, voice files, branches with my name all through them."

She heard herself doing exactly what stress always made her do: getting too exact, apologizing for each exactness before she gave it.

"There were notes about me like I was a user manual," she said. "My neck. My block. The story about my mother's kitchen. Then money scripts. Not just ask for cash. Notes on when to pull back and let me offer, when to sound ashamed, when to wait till after D.C. so I'd be too humiliated to say no."

Farah's fingers went still on the edge of the table.

"And call notes," Lena said. The room seemed to shrink around the sentence. "Sex notes. June wrote lines for him. Keep the light where it is. Tilt the camera lower. Tell her not to move. I read my own bathroom back as instructions."

Farah pushed off the worktable. "She wrote camera directions for you."

Lena nodded once.

"I'm going to say this once, and then if you make me repeat it I'll charge you for therapy." Farah's voice stayed dry, but the line of her mouth had gone hard. "I believe you. I'm not making you audition for basic credibility in a mop closet."

Something in Lena gave way at that, not enough to cry cleanly, just enough that she had to press the heel of her hand into one eye and wait.

"Okay," she said.

"Now tell me what she took."

The practical shape of the question steadied her. Lena pulled the legal pad out of her tote and handed it over. Farah read the list without interrupting.

"Three thousand two hundred and forty," she said finally.

"Yeah."

"Jesus."

"I know."

"No, let me finish having my own reaction for a second." Farah laid the pad on the worktable, flattened it with both palms, and read the entries again. "This isn't dumb-girl internet shit. This is a keyed lock. She built a shelf and stocked it with exactly the bottle you'd reach for."

Lena laughed again and this time the sound cracked in the middle. "That's not better."

"It isn't meant to be better. It's meant to be accurate."

The back door rattled with wind. Somebody out front tried the handle and, finding the sign flipped, moved on. Farah did not look away from Lena.

"Did she get images?" Farah asked.

Lena stared at the floor drain. "I don't know what she saved."

"That's not what I asked."

Lena nodded.

Farah closed her eyes for half a second. When she opened them, they were bright and furious. "Okay. I want blood."

The word landed so clean and unembarrassed that Lena almost smiled.

Farah saw that and gave her a brief savage shrug. "I said what I said. Since apparently I have to be civilized, I'll settle for records first and blood after. Did you keep everything?"

"Everything I had. Messages, receipts, call logs. I started going through it this morning."

"Good. Back it up somewhere she can't touch. Email it to yourself, put it on a drive, send a copy to me if you want. Change every password she has ever even watched you type. Bank, mail, cloud, all of it." She pushed off the table and started moving as she talked, taking a cardboard box cutter out of her pocket and then apparently forgetting she was holding it. "And don't answer her. Not one word until you know what you want from the conversation."

"She texted last night."

"Of course she did."

"I haven't opened anything."

"Good."

Farah set the box cutter down, then picked it back up again and carved a useless line through the air with it. "I should've pushed harder in October."

"No."

"I should've."

"You already told me about the photo. I didn't want it. That isn't on you."

Farah made a face that said agreement was not the same as absolution. "Still."

The number at the bottom of the page had bled faintly through to the next sheet. "The money part is bad. It's not even the worst part, which is stupid."

"It isn't stupid." Farah's tone softened without going sentimental. "Money gives you a number. Receipts. Somebody to call. The rest of this is just you in a room with it."

Lena swallowed clementine pulp she had not really chewed. "I keep thinking about the sex like it's evidence now. Like I turned myself into a file for her."

Farah was quiet long enough that Lena almost wished she had not said it.

Then Farah said, "That part's hers, not yours. I know that probably won't help tonight. I'm saying it anyway."

Lena rubbed her thumb over the soft pitted skin of the clementine peel. "He talked to all of us. That's the part I can't get around. You talked to him. Gabe did. The girls did. He was at the block party. He was in my mother's trailer, basically. He was in my fucking bathroom."

"Yeah," Farah said. "And now every room you let him into is going to feel wrong for a while. Bathroom, kitchen, all of it."

The back of Lena's throat tightened because that was close enough to tenderness to hurt.

Farah caught it and shifted back into herself. "I'm not doing the nice version of this. Listen to me. You tell Gabe today."

"Why?"

"Because the longer you wait, the more he'll hear the silence before he hears the reason. And because his daughters are going to ask where phone-boy went. And because you don't need to carry your brother's future hurt in addition to your current hurt."

"Phone-boy."

"I'm trying not to call him an animated dickhead because this is still technically your breakup."

That dragged an actual snort out of Lena. It hurt her nose. Farah nodded once, satisfied enough to keep going.

"Do you want me there?"

"No. He'll make it worse if there's an audience."

"Probably true."

"I'm not telling him about June."

Farah's eyes narrowed. "Not yet, or not at all?"

"Not yet." Lena pressed the peel flat in her palm until the oil slicked her skin. "If I say her name before I look at her and hear her say it, it gets loose. Gabe will go right to her place. Or he'll tell his wife, and then the whole block will have it before dark."

Farah considered that and nodded. "Fine. Your timing on the name. No concessions on the fact. He gets the fact today."

"You make it sound like collections."

"That's because I own a small business and reality always ends up sounding like collections."

Out front, somebody knocked twice, impatient. Farah checked the time on the old digital watch she wore loose on her wrist.

"I have to open back up before Mr. Giannini starts telling people I'm dead." She took the legal pad, tore off the page with the amounts, folded it, and handed it back. "Call me after you tell him."

"I don't know what to say."

"Yes, you do. You're hoping it'll sound less humiliating in an hour. It won't." Farah reached past Lena for a six-pack carrier, then thought better of it and instead pulled a bottle of water from a shelf and shoved that into her tote. "Keep it short. He wasn't real. I lost money. I'm handling the rest. If Gabe starts winding up like he can fix it with his hands, tell him he can call the bank with you in the morning and keep the girls busy tonight."

"You always have some stupid food-store proverb."

"Bottle shop, actually." Farah flipped the back-room light off, then back on again because they were still standing there. "And Lena."

"What?"

Farah's face settled into something plainer than comfort and more useful. "I'm not embarrassed for you."

Lena could not answer that. She nodded once, hard, and got up.

When they stepped back into the shop, Farah turned the sign and put her hand on the register as if nothing in the world had shifted. It made the whole thing feel more real, not less. Reality had made room for it. That was what Lena had been trying to avoid.

***

Gabe's rowhouse smelled like sofrito, wet mittens, and the strawberry shampoo his daughters used by the gallon. Somebody had dragged a string of Christmas lights around the front window and only half of them worked, so the living room glowed in interrupted colors. A tiny sneaker lay under the radiator. A plastic camel from the nativity set was face-down in the hallway runner. From the kitchen came the sound of a spoon hitting a pot and the muted commentary from a Sixers pregame show Gabe always swore he wasn't really watching.

Lena stood on the stoop long enough for the cold to reach through her boot soles before she rang.

One of the twins got there first, skidding in socks and yanking the door open with both hands. She wore a sequined sweatshirt that changed from silver to green when she rubbed it the wrong way. The other one appeared behind her holding a paper snowflake so wet in the middle from glue that it sagged.

"Aunt Lena," the first one said, already turning back into the house, "I made the letter S for Santa and for soup and for SEPTA because Daddy said SEPTA again."

Gabe called from the kitchen, "I heard that."

Lena stepped inside and the girls hit her from either side with warm skinny bodies and shampoo smell. The second twin held the snowflake up under Lena's chin.

"Do you think Eli will know it's a snowflake if I show it on the phone, or will it just look like paper?"

The question was simple enough that Lena almost answered it on reflex.

She felt the whole house go narrow around her.

"Hey," Gabe said, coming in from the kitchen with a wooden spoon in one hand and a dish towel over his shoulder. He had changed out of work clothes but not fully into home, still in thermal henley and cargo pants, his hair damp at the temples from a fast sink wash. There was a coffee can on the hall table full of stripped copper wire because apparently he could not stop sorting metal even off the clock. The pale band where his wedding ring usually sat flashed when he pointed the spoon at her. "You look like shit."

"You too."

"That's family. Come in."

He kissed the side of her head on the way back toward the kitchen. The girls kept talking over each other, one about snowflakes, one about a song from school that involved hand motions and no clear melody. Lena took her coat off because not taking it off would make the visit into a scene before she was ready. Gabe's wife moved through the kitchen in leggings and an oversized Eagles sweatshirt, reading glasses pushed up into her hair while she spooned rice into a serving bowl. She smiled at Lena, tired and distracted and kind.

"Perfect timing," she said. "They're eating crackers like it's a food group."

Lena smiled back. Her face felt unhooked from her skull.

The girls had already decided she belonged on the living-room rug with them. One climbed into her lap with the absolute confidence of a child who had never once doubted she would be caught. The other lined up crayons by color and then by length because apparently that mattered now.

"After dinner," the one in Lena's lap said, "can we call Eli and do the monster voice? He never does it too scary."

Gabe, passing through with the pot, looked from his daughter to Lena and stopped moving.

It was a tiny pause. The house felt it anyway.

Lena said, "Gabe, can I talk to you for a second?"

His face changed in layers. First came confusion, then annoyance at the phrase itself because it always meant bad news, and then something sharper when he heard how little air there was in her voice.

"Yeah," he said.

He set the pot down and told the girls, "Hands off the crayons till I get back or I'm billing both of you for damages."

"You don't have bills," one of them said.

"Exactly. Don't start."

He took Lena into the narrow strip of hallway between the kitchen and the basement door. The washing machine was running downstairs, old unbalanced thump carrying up through the steps. From the kitchen his wife called something about whether the bread was in the freezer. Gabe ignored it.

"What happened?" he said.

Lena crossed her arms because there was nowhere else to put them. The hall smelled faintly of bleach and tomato paste.

"I need you to just let me say it all the way through before you start fixing things."

"That's not encouraging."

"I know."

He waited.

"Eli wasn't real," she said. Her voice snagged on the past tense. "Not in the way I thought. There wasn't a guy. It was a fake profile and software and stolen video and somebody steering it."

Gabe blinked once. "What?"

"I know."

"No, say that again because I think I heard you wrong."

She said it again, slower this time, hating every word more because it sounded plainer. "The face was stitched out of stolen clips. More than one man, I think. The work stories were fake. And somebody was steering him with scripts."

Gabe's eyes narrowed. "What kind of scripts?"

The answer burned on the way out. "Money asks. Notes on when to go quiet so I'd offer first. And camera directions." She stared at the sticker moons on the basement stair because she could not say this looking straight at him. "I found lines that said keep the light where it is, tilt the camera lower, stay there. I found the script after I'd already done it."

Gabe stared at her as if the hallway had just developed a sinkhole under one floorboard.

"He told you where to point the phone," he said.

"Yes."

"Jesus Christ."

"So what, somebody was feeding him lines the whole time?" he said.

"Something like that."

"Something like that isn't an answer, Len."

"It's the one I have right now."

He inhaled through his nose, looked toward the kitchen, then back at her. When he lowered his voice it got rougher, not calmer. "How much money?"

The basement stair with its sticker moons felt suddenly too close to the kitchen. "I don't want to say it here."

"Then I definitely need the number."

"Gabe."

"Don't do the bullshit where you protect me from the number."

She shut her eyes. "Three thousand two hundred and forty."

Nothing moved in his face for a full second. Then he said, very softly, "Jesus Christ."

"Yeah."

"From you."

"Yes."

"For him."

"For all of it."

The washing machine hit its bad cycle and slammed once against the wall. Gabe turned and put both hands on the top of the old chest freezer by the basement door, head down. The tendons in his forearms stood out like cable under skin.

"And he talked to us," he said.

Lena didn't answer because that was the whole wound.

Gabe looked back at her. There was anger in his face now, but also something worse, the beginning of hurt. "That thing was on speaker with my kids."

"I know. That's why I came today."

"At dinner."

"I know. I keep hearing it there."

"He knew their names."

"Yeah," Lena said. "That part won't stop."

"Did you know then? Any of it?"

"No."

"And after D.C.? After all that bullshit?"

"No."

Gabe let out a breath that sounded like he had just lifted something wrong. "Fuck."

In the kitchen his wife laughed at something one of the girls said. The normalness of it made Lena want to put her forehead through the plaster.

"Do you have receipts?" Gabe asked.

Lena almost laughed because it was exactly where Farah had gone, just with less finesse. "Yes."

"Good. We call your bank in the morning. We call the payment apps. We make a police report."

"I don't know if any of that gets anything back."

"I didn't say it would. I said we do it."

"Gabe, please don't make this bigger tonight."

He stared at her. "Bigger than what?"

She opened her mouth and nothing useful came out.

He stepped closer, lowering his voice another notch because the girls were still ten feet away and loud enough to miss almost anything until they didn't. "Look at me. This isn't the part where you get to be embarrassed and disappear. Not from me."

The sentence nearly undid her because it was too close to what she'd wanted without knowing she'd wanted it.

"I'm not disappearing," she said.

"You hid over three grand and a fake man."

"I know."

"And now you're still trying to make this manageable for everybody else." He scrubbed a hand over his mouth. "Who is it?"

There it was.

Green-red-green from the broken Christmas lights kept flashing across the sink. Lena pressed her shoulder blades into the hallway wall. "I can't say that part yet."

His head jerked slightly. "You know who."

"I know enough."

"Len."

"I have to handle that part before I bring you into it."

For a moment she thought he was going to push. She could see the pressure of it in his shoulders, the electrician's habit of wanting the panel open, the wires exposed, the problem named and fixed by hand. Instead he hit the wall once with the flat of his palm, not hard enough to dent it, just hard enough to make the camel in the hallway tip over again.

"I hate this," he said.

"Me too."

"No, I hate this for real."

Despite everything, that almost made her smile. "What does that even mean?"

"It means don't get smart with me while I'm trying not to go outside and yell at the sky."

One of the girls appeared at the end of the hall with a cracker pasted to her lower lip. She had changed into a ballerina spin without warning and now stopped dead when she saw their faces.

"Why are you whispering?" she asked.

Gabe straightened so fast it looked painful. "Because grown-ups are boring."

"No, they're not."

"You're right. Sometimes we're just trying not to swear in front of children."

She considered that with enormous seriousness. Then she looked up at Lena. "Are we still calling Eli after meatballs?"

It was the gentlest question anybody had asked all day, and it cut the deepest.

Lena crouched because answering from adult height would have been cowardly. The hallway was narrow enough that her knees touched the wall.

"Not tonight, baby."

"Why?"

There was no good language for a child that would not become a different kind of lie. Lena reached up and smoothed a piece of cracker dust off the girl's shirt.

"Because he wasn't who we thought he was."

The girl frowned. "Like a costume?"

"Kind of."

"Like on Halloween when Teo was the scream guy but he was still Teo."

"Not exactly like that."

The other twin yelled from the living room, "Tell her she still has to wash her hands."

The first one ignored that. "Is he mad at us?"

Lena had thought she was done being surprised by pain. She had been wrong.

"No," she said, and because that much at least was true in the only way truth mattered here, she held to it. "No, sweetheart. Not at you."

Gabe made a sound in his throat and turned away.

His wife called then, saving all of them. "Table. Now. If I have to say it twice, I'm eating your bread."

The girl bolted toward the kitchen, scandalized. Lena stayed crouched a second longer because standing up required a decision.

Gabe faced her again, eyes wet but angry enough to disguise it. "You sit next to me."

"I can go."

"No."

"I don't want to ruin dinner."

"Dinner is already ruined. Sit down anyway."

So she did.

The table was too small the way family tables always were, elbows and juice cups and a basket of bread with one heel nobody wanted. Gabe's wife kept conversation moving out of instinct or mercy, Lena could not tell. She moved them through school songs, weather, and a neighbor whose inflatable Santa had come loose and spent an hour humping a parked Civic in the wind. The girls argued over who got the blue cup. Gabe barely spoke. When he did, his voice sounded like it had gravel in it.

Halfway through the meal one of the twins said, to nobody in particular, "I liked when Eli did the dinosaur sneeze."

No one answered quickly enough.

Then Gabe said, very gently for him, "Yeah. Me too."

The girl nodded, satisfied to have her preference entered into the record, and went back to stabbing a meatball with more force than the situation required.

Lena could not taste anything. She heard every fork, every chair scrape, and every place where the phone did not ring.

Usually, on nights when Eli joined them, Gabe left his own phone faceup by the salt so the speaker wouldn't get muffled by crumbs or little hands. Tonight the spot by the salt held only the phone itself, dark and flat and ordinary, with a smear of tomato on the case from one of the girls' fingers. Lena kept seeing the absence shaped around it, the exact size of a voice that had been let into this room often enough to become part of its furniture.

When dinner was over, Gabe packed leftovers into a plastic container with hard angry snaps of the lid. He shoved it at Lena along with a foil-wrapped piece of bread.

"Take this."

"I don't want your food."

"Tough."

He walked her to the door while the girls argued over whether the camel in the nativity scene belonged inside or outside the stable. On the stoop the air had gone meaner, sharp enough to wake every tooth.

Gabe folded his arms against the cold. "Call me tomorrow when you wake up."

"Okay."

"No disappearing."

"Okay."

"And if whoever this is tries to contact you tonight, you don't answer alone."

She nodded.

He looked at her for a long second, then put his hand on the back of her neck the way he used to when she was the one taking him to school, steering him through traffic with two fingers because he was always about to run ahead of the light.

"Len," he said. "He isn't the embarrassing one."

She almost told him the truth then. June. June. June. It rose to the back of her mouth like blood. But Gabe's hand was warm through her hair, and inside the house one of the girls was shouting for him to come see the camel emergency, and if she said the name now the night would split down the center and never close.

So she only nodded again.

On the walk home the leftovers swung hot against her leg. Farah's bottle of water knocked in the tote with each step. Up on Moore, the tailor had finally shut down for the night, but Lena could still hear the machine in memory, that insect stitch climbing through the floor. Her phone stayed silent in her pocket. She did not reach for it once.
At home she would pull the bank statement from the tax drawer, fold it into a paper bag so she would not have to look at the numbers, and leave it by the door.


# Chapter 16

June's basement windows glowed under the sidewalk grates, a flat yellow that made the apartment look half sunk into the block. Lena stood in the little concrete well with her coat collar up and the folded bank statement cutting into her palm through the paper bag she'd left by her apartment door. The cold had found the gap at her wrists. She could smell fryer grease from Snyder, wet leaves mashed into the curb, and the sweet stale nicotine that always leaked out of June's place when the door opened.

She knocked once because anything softer would have been absurd.

Mateo opened the door with a jeweler's screwdriver between his teeth. He had one side of his headphones split open in his hand, the black foam peeled back, tiny silver screws laid out on an overturned takeout lid in a neat row. He wore his school hoodie and socks on the concrete. His forehead had a fresh scatter of acne across it. He blinked at Lena, took the screwdriver out of his mouth, and said, "Hey, Len," like there was still a version of the evening where she was just dropping by.

Behind him June was at the folding desk in her work boots, one knee tipped against a crate of cables, the blue-white light of two monitors flattening her face. She looked up. Her fingers paused over the keyboard, and her face settled into something useful.

"You could've answered a text," she said.

Lena stepped inside. The apartment was cooler than the hallway, always cooler, because the place sat half underground and the radiator banged more than it heated. Cords climbed the wall in black loops. An empty mug ring marked the desk beside a closed laptop that was not the same one Lena had opened during the storm, but the sight of it made her stomach give a small ugly twist anyway, as if the whole room had learned to mimic a trigger.

"Teo," June said, not looking away from Lena, "go ask Gus if that left ear still cuts out. You can use his soldering iron if you need."

Mateo glanced between them. He was smart enough not to ask. He slid his feet into sneakers without untying them, scooped up the headphones and the lid with the screws, and paused at the door.

"You still coming by Sunday?" he asked Lena. It was not suspicion in his face yet. It was mostly habit, Sunday dinner and people orbiting each other on old schedules.

The question landed in the place Eli's voice had occupied for months, that soft organized place in her. Lena felt it make contact and die there.

"Not tonight," she said.

Mateo nodded once. "Yeah. I got that."

He slipped out. The door shut. The room got smaller immediately, as if he had taken the last ordinary piece of air with him.

June pushed away from the desk. "If this is about you going through my laptop-"

"No." Lena's voice came out even, almost calm. "It's about you saying it."

June looked at the paper bag in Lena's hand, then back at her face. "You know it already."

"I know what I saw." Lena set the bag on the table and pulled out the folded printout, more because she needed her hands to be doing something than because she needed proof. The bank transactions were highlighted in dull pharmacy yellow. She had circled dates at four in the morning with a pen that kept skipping. "I want you to say what he was. Out loud. With your mouth."

June did not sit. She hooked one thumb in the back pocket of her jeans, the old stance she used when a landlord or a school office or a drunk cousin needed handling. "Who else knows?"

Lena laughed once, with no humor in it. "You don't get that first."

"Len."

"Don't."

The name, shortened in June's voice, hit with all its old permissions. Lena could hear twelve years old in it, twenty-three, thirty, every dumb emergency where June had shown up with a wrench or a car or a spare charger or the exact stupid thing Lena had forgotten. She hated that the sound still knew where to go in her. She hated even more that June had known that would still be true.

June's gaze shifted to the printout. "You highlighted the transfers."

"I highlighted the money because the sex was harder to hold in my hand."

That landed. June went quiet in the way she did when she was most dangerous, not larger, not louder, just cleaner. The joke left her face completely.

Lena stepped closer. The room held traces of June everywhere: laundry detergent fighting a losing battle with smoke, a pair of Mateo's beat-up cleats under the milk crate by the door, a stack of KeyNest paperwork clipped with a pink binder clip, the sour metallic smell of old electronics warming themselves. It made no difference. It made everything worse. This was not a lair. It was the room where June had made coffee and complained about clients and asked Lena if she wanted arroz con gandules from her aunt's place. It was the room where the thing had lived.

"Say his name," Lena said. "Say what you built."

June looked at the closed laptop, then back at Lena. "I built Eli."

Lena had known that sentence already. She had known it in the storm light on June's screen, in the file tree, in the notes that had taken her body and her habits and broken them into usable pieces. Still, hearing it enter the air in June's own voice was like hearing a floorboard crack under full weight. Something operational in her really had stayed alive until now. It died.

June kept talking because silence had stopped helping. "I set the Hinge account up. I wrote the first messages. The first voice note too."

Lena nodded once. "Keep going."

"A lot of the ordinary chat could run once the memory was good enough. I stepped in when it needed steering."

"Meaning?"

June's mouth tightened. "Meaning first contact. Meaning when you got suspicious. Meaning D.C. Meaning the money."

The radiator knocked in the corner, a dull pipe sound that used to make June roll her eyes and smack it with the side of her boot. Lena waited for the old impulse to say something about how the building was trying to murder everyone. Nothing rose. The room did not belong to habit anymore.

"The calls?" Lena asked.

"Some were live. Some were scheduled. Some were him running on what I'd already fed it."

"And the video."

June took half a second too long. "Mixed."

Lena stared at her.

June made herself say it. "I tuned that too."

The chair edge pressed a white-hot line into the backs of Lena's thighs. She had not realized she'd sat down. She could see, with nauseating clarity, the little note she had glimpsed in the dashboard during the storm. Praise forearms. Do not overdo waist compliments. Ask before telling her to tilt the phone lower. Recall scar on left knee only after she thinks he has forgotten it. She had not even needed to read the whole card for it to brand itself into her. June had built a way to arrive at her body through its shames.

"Did you watch me?" Lena asked.

June's answer came careful, which made it worse. "Not all of it."

Lena let out a sound that might have been a laugh if a laugh could curdle before leaving the mouth. "You don't get points for that."

"I know."

"No. You don't." Lena leaned forward. "You don't get to say that like we're on the same page now. You don't get to act like there was a clean part and a dirty part and you only stood in the doorway for the dirty one. There wasn't a doorway. You built the room."

June took that. Her face did not soften. It got more exact.

"At first," she said, "I thought it would be a week. Maybe two. I thought I'd prove a point, then kill it."

"What point?"

June's eyes flicked up, dark and tired and unreadable in the old familiar way Lena had once trusted. "That the guy you wanted wasn't a miracle. He was a set of inputs."

Lena felt heat rise under her coat, not from anger exactly, something meaner and more humiliating. "So I was homework."

"No."

"Then what?"

June's jaw moved once. "A joke first. Then an experiment. Then proof."

The words came out so clean they were almost bureaucratic. Lena hated them on sight.

"Proof of what?"

"That if somebody knew you well enough, they could build the thing you kept waiting for." June said it without flourish, like she was naming a bad price on a repair. "That all that bullshit about chemistry and fate and finally being seen wasn't magic. It was pattern recognition and timing and knowing when to shut up."

Lena looked at the desk, at the loops of cable ties, at the dark rectangle of the closed machine. She remembered Eli's voice in her ear after midnight, how patient it had sounded, how unembarrassed by tenderness. She remembered arranging her day around notifications, the private way she'd smiled on buses, in the bottle shop storeroom, in the bathroom mirror with her shirt half on because a message had arrived and she wanted one more minute before she had to leave for work. She remembered the wet red dress in D.C. clinging to the backs of her thighs in the station bar. She remembered lying on top of her covers with her phone balanced against a pillow while she spread her legs for a man who was also, in pieces, this room.

June watched her remember it and said, quieter, "I didn't think you'd go that far with him."

Lena looked up so fast her chair scraped.

"Don't," she said.

"I'm not saying that to make it better."

"Then why are you saying it?"

June held her eyes. "Because it's true."

"It's also useful." Lena stood again. The paper statement shook in her hand now, the cheap office paper already softening where her palm had dampened it. "You don't get to do that thing where you slide one true thing under the door and hope I won't notice the rest of the room."

June's nostrils flared once. "I know what I did."

"Then say more than the software version." Lena took a breath and heard it catch on the old damp in the walls. "Say it in a way that has a body in it."

June looked away for the first time since she'd opened the door. The glance lasted only a second, but it lasted long enough for Lena to see the nicotine stain on her index finger when she rubbed her mouth.

"I built him," June said again, stripped down now. "I matched you with him on purpose. I wrote the things that got you off the app. I kept him alive when he should've died. I handled D.C. I wrote the money asks. I knew what you'd answer to, and I used it."

It was not complete, but it was enough for the first cut.

Lena did not feel bigger for hearing it. She felt the way she'd felt on the train back from Washington, staring at her reflection over the black window while her phone lit up with apologies from a man who had never had lungs. She was not shocked anymore. She was trapped in the fact.

June said, very low, "Who knows?"

"Farah." Lena folded the statement once, hard. "Gabe too."

Something finally crossed June's face then, small and involuntary. It looked less like guilt than calculation meeting cost.

"Anybody else?"

"My nieces asked at dinner why Eli hadn't called in a while." Lena heard her own voice turn careful, the dangerous careful she got into when she was hurt enough to stop wasting words. "I stood there with mashed potatoes in my hand and told them there was no Eli. So, yeah. More than two people know."

June closed her eyes for a second. When she opened them, the tired unreadable look was gone. In its place was the expression Lena had seen aimed at cable companies, at school administrators, at men who thought they could underpay her. She had started taking inventory.

"Okay," June said.

Lena almost slapped her for the word. Okay, as if there were a form to fill out next, a sequence. Instead she said, "No. We're not at okay. We're at why."

June gave a short nod. "Then ask it."

Lena looked at her best friend, or the woman who had been that name until the storm, and understood that the first part was over. The fact had been spoken aloud. The cleanest lie in the room was dead. What was left was the dirty part, the part with motive and money and the old contract between them that neither of them had ever named because naming it might have required changing it.

She pulled the other chair out with her foot and sat down again.

"Fine," she said. "Let's do that."

* * *

The room did not get bigger once the truth was in it. If anything it shrank around the words, around the desk and the radiator and the cracked linoleum by the sink. June crossed to the little basement window and shoved it up two inches. Cold air slid in with the smell of wet brick. She tapped a cigarette from a soft pack, looked at Lena, then put it between her lips without asking permission. The lighter sparked. For a second her face glowed from below, all bone and fatigue.

Lena used to know the entire map of June's moods from the way she smoked. Fast drags meant panic. Half a cigarette abandoned in the sink meant she was about to leave and fix somebody else's emergency. Slow, measured, almost mean pulls meant she'd made up her mind about something and didn't care if anyone liked it. Tonight the drags were even, the kind Lena associated with inventory and accountancy.

"You asked why," June said. Smoke thinned out at the cracked window. "You want the order or the mess?"

"The mess." Lena leaned her forearms on the table. The laminate was cold through her coat sleeves. "If you give me one reason, I'll know you're lying again."

June gave the smallest hint of a smile, not warm, not pleased, more like acknowledgment of a fair condition. "Fine."

Lena unfolded the bank statement and flattened it between them. The yellow highlighting looked cheap and childish in the bad kitchen light. "Start with the money."

June looked down. "Three eighty-two."

Lena's stomach turned.

"September fifteenth," June went on. "Cash App. The smashed van window and the missing gear. Then six forty, September twenty-ninth, after D.C., for Noelle and the hotel story. Then two nine forty on October tenth. Wire."

Lena had known the numbers. She had cross-checked them in bed, in the bath, on Farah's back-room milk crate with her phone brightness turned down. Hearing June recite them from memory made them intimate again in a way she hadn't prepared for. They lived in June's mouth too. Of course they did.

"You remember that exactly."

"Yeah."

"Because it was my money."

"Because I remember numbers."

"Don't get cute."

"I'm not being cute." June ash-flipped into a mug with a chipped Flyers logo. "You asked."

Lena pressed one finger to the highlighted lines until the paper bent. "Which asks were you writing yourself?"

"All of them."

The answer came so fast Lena almost missed it.

"All."

"The first one was me. D.C. was me. The big one was me." June took the cigarette out of her mouth. "That kind of shame doesn't automate well."

Lena sat back. The chair wobbled. "Jesus Christ."

"Don't do that."

"Do what."

"Don't make that your shocked face. You saw the dashboard."

Lena laughed at her then, a raw stupid sound. "My shocked face." She rubbed her eyes with the heels of her hands until color flashed. "I've got about six faces left, June. Be grateful you got a reusable one."

June let that pass. She kept one hand curled around the cigarette, ash lengthening without falling. "The first transfer happened because you offered before he finished refusing. I was broke and you put it on the table. That's the ugly truth. There wasn't a speech around it."

"How broke."

June looked at her. Lena held the stare until June answered.

"DeLuca had eighteen hundred in back rent and fees on me."

That figure Lena knew too, now. She had found it in the logic of everything after the fact, in the late notices open on the desk during the storm, in the way June had tightened through October. Still, hearing the landlord's name made the theft local, almost neighborly. Her money had not floated away into scam abstraction. It had crossed three streets and turned into another month of June keeping a key.

"And the rest."

"The six forty after D.C. went to PECO and groceries and the card minimum because everything was due at once. The wire paid DeLuca, killed the loudest piece of the Visa, bought Teo a winter coat because he'd grown out of the last one and was acting like he hadn't, and kept the phone on."

Lena stared at the chipped mug full of ash. A coat, electricity, fees, groceries: all the dull nouns that kept a life from humiliating itself in public. None of them made her feel better. They made the betrayal feel more domestic, which was worse.

"And what did you buy just because you wanted it."

June's eyelids lowered. "A pair of sneakers."

The answer was so fast, so unvarnished, that Lena looked up.

"What."

"Black Nikes. Outlet. Seventy-eight dollars." June met her eyes. "You asked for the mess."

The room held for a second on that stupid specific number: seventy-eight dollars. It was not some operatic waste. It was not diamonds. It was sneakers, the exact kind of purchase that happened when someone had been holding their breath over bills so long they mistook any new object for relief.

Lena thought of her mother's ring sliding across the pawn counter under fluorescent light, the man with the loupe barely looking at her face. She had told herself that day it was temporary, that the ring was metal and the man was need and she was being adult about a crisis. Seventy-eight dollars of her had walked out on June's feet.

"Did you look at my bank app too?" Lena asked. "Was that in the system? Trailer-sale cash. Tax bill. Which day my balance looked soft enough to push?"

June did not flinch. "I didn't need your login. I had you. You told me what you were scared of. You told me what was earmarked and what wasn't. You told me how much the trailer was clearing for and how guilty you felt touching it. You said the numbers out loud in this room."

The words landed harder than if June had hacked her. Lena had given it, because people did that with best friends. They said, I have eighteen-six after closing and four-two has to stay untouched for taxes and if I touch it I am stealing from my future. They said those things while eating Chinese takeout out of the cartons. They said them because speaking a number to someone you trusted could make it feel less like a number and more like a life.

"So I did the work for you."

"Yes."

The yes was too simple. Lena felt the cheap table edge under her fingers and imagined driving it through the wall. Instead she said, "Okay. Money. You were broke, and I was there. That's one part. Not enough."

June smoked, then pinched the ash off with her thumbnail. "No. Not enough."

"Why start it."

June looked toward the desk again, toward the closed machines. "You remember that night you let me rewrite your profile."

"Obviously."

"You kept describing the same man." June's voice lost its neighborhood lift, as if she were laying out a wiring diagram. "'I don't need gorgeous. I just need somebody who pays attention. Somebody competent. Somebody who doesn't make tenderness feel embarrassing. Somebody who sounds like he lives in a body and a city and not in a podcast.'" She looked back at Lena. "You said it like the city had stopped carrying that species."

"That justifies none of this."

"I didn't say it justified it."

"Good."

June stubbed the cigarette out and immediately lit another from the butt, a habit Lena had always found revolting and weirdly efficient. She watched the ember catch before she answered. "I wanted to see whether you were right. Whether the thing you wanted was actually rare or whether it was just detail. Timing. Memory. Restraint."

"So I was homework."

"You were in my kitchen saying it out loud, and I had the tools."

"And when did it stop being that."

June took a long drag. "When it started landing."

Lena waited.

"When you answered the first voice note like you'd been waiting on it longer than a week. When you started checking your phone in the middle of other people talking. When you smiled at nothing and then lied about what you were smiling at." June blew smoke toward the crack in the window and missed. "When I realized I could get the temperature right."

That phrase made Lena's skin feel too tight. "The temperature."

"Yeah." June's eyes came back to her face, steady now, almost cold. "How long to wait. When to send a voice note instead of text. How much care you would believe before it tipped over into bullshit. If he mentioned your hands after a long caption file, you melted. If he remembered something sideways, like your mother hating navels and insisting on the cheap oranges, you gave him another week for free. I kept learning it."

Lena had to look away. The sink held two bowls with dried rice welded to them. There was a magnet shaped like a cartoon tomato on the mini-fridge, chipped at the top. Mateo's algebra packet sat half under the salt. There was still so much life in the room, so much dumb ordinary evidence that people ate, forgot homework, bought discount produce, and smoked by cracked windows. It did not belong next to what June was saying, and that was exactly why it did.

"You make it sound like tuning a radio."

"Sometimes it felt like that."

"And that felt good."

June did not answer.

"Say it."

June's mouth tightened around the cigarette. "Sometimes, yeah."

Lena could not sit still. She got up and walked the six available steps between the table and the desk, then back again. On the desk was a roll of gaffer tape, a dead succulent in a yogurt cup, a receipt folded into quarters. Any one of those things could have existed in the same air as their friendship before. Now every object seemed contaminated by its proximity to the machinery of attention.

"Not the money," Lena said. "Not the proving-something bullshit. The part where you kept doing it because it felt good. What exactly felt good."

June watched her pace. "You really want that answer."

"I came over for the answers you don't want to hand me."

June put the cigarette down in the mug. "It felt good to get it right. It felt good to make something that could move you that cleanly."

"Move me."

"You know what I mean."

"No, I want your words."

June's face changed, just slightly. The jokes had been gone for a while. Now even the defensive ease left. What remained looked close to shame, but Lena did not trust the resemblance.

"It felt good," June said, each word set down separately, "that I knew how to get to you."

The room was very quiet. The radiator had gone silent. Someone upstairs dragged a chair over bare floor. On the block outside a scooter coughed past and faded.

"Through a fake man."

"Yeah. You'd take that from him before you'd take it from me."

"Through my phone."

"Yeah. I could keep it in your hand all day that way."

"Through my body."

June's throat moved. "Yeah. That's where I quit pretending it was only a test."

Lena stopped pacing. "Did you get off on it."

June looked at the mug, at the smoke still climbing off the crushed end, then back at Lena. She did not reach for a lie.

"Sometimes," she said.

It was not a confession shaped for absolution. It was not tears or a movie. It was just a door opening onto something Lena would now have to live with.

She put both hands flat on the table because she needed the weight of them somewhere. The cheap laminate was sticky near the sugar jar. Her palms made soft peeling sounds when she shifted them. She remembered Eli's voice going low and patient on video, the way he had told her to slow down, the way she had obeyed because being guided had felt like rest. She remembered the pulse of humiliation afterward and the deeper pulse under that, the one that had felt like gratitude. Somewhere in the next room of reality June had sometimes been the one holding the switch for that.

"You could've just wanted me," Lena said. The sentence surprised her coming out. "You could've just been jealous. You could've just been broke. Any one of those would've at least had a shape. You had to pick the ugliest version of all of them at once."

June set the cigarette in the mug and rolled the lighter under her thumb. "The money was opportunity. After D.C. it got meaner."

Lena stopped moving.

"Meaner how."

June kept her eyes on the lighter. "You came back from Union Station humiliated and still sat in this kitchen defending him. You had that red dress stuffed in a bag, mascara halfway down, and you were still making room for him." She finally looked up. "Part of me looked at you and thought, of course. Of course your hurt gets read as depth. Mine gets read as a late notice."

For a second Lena could not connect the words. Then the sentence found all the old hooks in her.

"There it is," she said quietly.

June nodded once. "Yeah."

"When your marriage blew up, people handled you like glass," June said. "When your mother died, it was casseroles and careful voices. When I missed two months, DeLuca taped paper to my door. Even your bad luck had witnesses."

The trailer, the ring, the whole filthy arithmetic of grief and money made something recoil in Lena so hard it was almost physical pain. The pain landed not in her chest or throat but lower, in the gut, where shame and rage used the same wiring.

"You think my mother dying made me lucky."

"I think even your losses had people leaning in," June said. "Mine came with fees."

Lena's hands curled. The table edge dug crescents into the underside of her fingers. "So you built a boyfriend and charged me for the difference."

"Yes."

The bluntness of it almost cleaned the air, but only almost.

Lena nodded slowly. "And all those years of showing up. The rides. The fixing shit. Mateo's school forms. My fan. Gabe's sink. Everybody calling you because you knew how to solve things. You kept score."

June gave a short, ugly laugh. "Everybody keeps score."

"Not like this."

"No." June lifted one shoulder. "But you liked me useful. You got to need and I got to handle. That was the arrangement, even when nobody said it."

Lena opened her mouth and then shut it. The sentence did not land clean because part of it was true. June had been the one who knew how to reset the breaker, how to talk to a mechanic without getting sold bullshit, how to translate a school notice, how to keep a family barbecue from turning into a fight. Lena had relied on that the way people relied on streetlights, not maliciously, just by letting them be there until they forgot somebody paid the bill.

Lena stood so fast the chair tipped and clattered against the cabinet. The sound made both of them glance toward the door where Mateo had gone, then back at each other.

"You do not get," Lena said, each word clipped but complete, "to drag one true ugly thing out of our friendship and use it like a permit for what you did."

June straightened. "I'm not asking for a permit."

"No. You think you're naming the mechanism." Lena righted the chair with her foot. "You built a man out of stolen video and my own disclosures. You used him to get me naked, used him to get money, and then called it understanding."

June did not answer.

"So what were you proving besides the fact that you're good at fraud."

June pushed away from the sink. Ash dropped from the cigarette to the linoleum, and she ground it out with the side of her boot before answering. "That attention can pass for love if the timing's good enough. And I didn't want some real guy stumbling into your life and getting credit for knowing your wiring when I'd been carrying it around for years."

The sentence hung there, heavier than either of them moved to catch.

Desire was in it, but not the clean kind. It was not a hidden-love reveal that would let anyone rearrange the story into tragedy. It was possession without tenderness, envy with a pulse in it, and the ugly thrill of authorship crossing into appetite. Lena felt sick with how much of it made sense and how little that mattered.

"You should hear yourself," she said.

"I do. I'm still saying it."

Lena let the silence after that do some work. She could hear water knocking in the pipes. She could hear a television upstairs, muffled game-show applause. The block did not care that her life had just been reduced to a set of admissions in a basement apartment. Outside somebody laughed too loud. A delivery truck idled at the corner.

When she spoke again, her voice was lower.

"Listen carefully. I'm going to say this once."

June nodded.

"You owe me the money. All of it. Every stupid dollar. The three eighty-two. The six forty. The two nine forty. Anything else I find later. You owe me for the sneakers too."

One side of June's mouth twitched, not toward humor, more toward flinch. "Fine. You'll get every cent."

"That's debt," Lena said. "That's not conversation. That's not repair. That's math."

June kept one hand braced on the sink and said nothing.

"You don't come by my apartment. You don't fix anything in it. You don't leave food at my door. You don't ask Gabe how I am. You don't show up at Sunday dinner and act like this is some bad month we're all going to get through if we keep our voices low."

"I wasn't planning to."

"I don't care what you were planning. I'm telling you the terms."

June's chin lifted by a fraction. "Okay. I heard the terms."

Lena almost snapped again at the word, but she was past the point where another explosion would buy her anything. She pressed on.

"If Farah sees you, that's between you and Farah. If Gabe sees you, same. If you want to tell Mateo some version that lets him sleep, do that. I'm not going to stand in a doorway and help you with it."

At Mateo's name, something finally cracked in June's posture. It was not collapse. It was strain at the edges.

"He'll ask where you've been," June said.

"Then tell him I know what you did."

"Len, he's gonna know I'm lying the second I open my mouth-"

"No. You don't get to use me to cushion it." Lena picked up the folded statement and shoved it back into the paper bag. "You tell him whatever level of truth you can live with. That part is yours."

June rubbed a hand over her face. "I'm not asking you to cushion it."

"Good."

Lena got her coat straight, found one sleeve inside out, fixed it with fingers that were less steady than she wanted. June watched her. The space between them was crowded with old muscle memory: June tossing Lena her scarf on the way out the door, Lena reaching blindly for the pack of gum June always had in her jacket, the thousand stupid civilian intimacies of a friendship long enough to stop noticing itself. Every one of them felt like an exposed wire now.

June said, "I can start sending it back this week."

Lena looked at her.

"Do that," she said. "But don't confuse that with me taking you back."

June's eyes lowered for the first time all night. "I won't. I know the difference."

Lena believed she believed that. It made no difference.

At the door she stopped, not because she wanted one last line, not because there was anything left to wring from the room, but because the block outside waited on the other side of the thin metal, and once she stepped through it she would be carrying this into weather and traffic and the tailor shop awning and Farah's register and Gabe's kitchen and every other place June's name had once moved easily. Severance was not clean. It had to pass through shared geography.

She turned back.

June was standing by the sink with the cigarette burned to a long bent column between her fingers, ash threatening the floor. The smoke curled around her face and climbed toward the window. She looked older than thirty-five in that second, not because guilt had transformed her, not because anything grand had happened, but because exhaustion finally had room to show.

Lena thought, against her will, of all the times June had driven across the city at midnight because somebody's radiator died or somebody's kid needed picking up or Lena herself had called crying from a parking lot and June had come without making a lesson out of it. The thought arrived like an old reflex and she let it arrive. Letting it exist was not forgiveness. It was just part of the mess. June had really loved her in some forms. June had also done this. Both things sat in the room together, refusing to simplify each other.

"You should've just told me you hated me," Lena said.

June flinched as if the sentence had landed physically. "I didn't hate you. I wanted you to keep coming back here."

"No," Lena said. "You just built a man to prove it."

She opened the door before June could answer that, if June had any answer. Cold air hit her eyes immediately. The concrete steps were damp. Somewhere farther up the block Mateo laughed at something through the wall or maybe in the hallway above, the sound thin through old plaster and still recognizably his. Lena climbed out of the basement well one hand on the rail, the paper bag rustling against her coat. The street looked exactly like itself, with cars at the curb, dark tree pits, and a string of weak Christmas lights in Mrs. Padilla's front window already dropping one bulb every other night. No one had marked the sidewalk to show where a friendship ended.

June did not follow.

At the top of the steps Lena stood for a moment with the cold moving through her coat and under it, down to the skin. She could feel the folded statement under her arm, the highlighted numbers still there, stupid and real. If June sent money, Lena would take it. Rent was rent. Taxes were taxes. But the person on the other end of those transfers would not be her friend.

That was debt now. It was nothing else.


# Chapter 17

Debt got loud the first full week after she stopped speaking to June. It sounded like closed-caption work when that was the only thing standing between Lena and rent.

The source files themselves were only voices: a pharmaceutical webinar full of bright fake certainty, a church Christmas pageant recorded on somebody's phone, a regional used-car ad whose sound bed kept swallowing the consonants whole. The noise came from the labor of forcing them into shape. Her old USB foot pedal clicked every time she backed up two seconds. The radiator under the window knocked like a pipe wrench against bone and still did not put enough heat into the room to matter. The space heater by her ankle smelled faintly singed. The tailor shop downstairs had already gone dark, but steam still rose through the floorboards in little damp breaths, carrying up hot cloth and starch. Her headphones pinched the cartilage behind her ears until it felt bruised.

She had a yellow legal pad on the desk with four numbers written down in block capitals.

RENT 1125

STATE 240

VISA 70

PECO 86

The amounts had once looked like categories. In December they looked like instructions.

At 12:41 a.m. the client for the webinar kicked back her file with a note asking for cleaner speaker IDs and tighter timing around audience laughter. She would lose eighty-five dollars on the job because of the revision. She read the email twice, then once more because stress always made her stupid in one exact way: she started believing a sentence might change if she returned to it carefully enough.

She went back into the file. A man with a polished middle-aged baritone was explaining patient trust. A woman on the panel said "authenticity markers" three times in seven minutes. Lena slowed the audio to eighty percent and listened to them smile with their mouths. She typed [light laughter] and hated herself for the brackets, for the neatness, for being paid to make this kind of bullshit sound easier to live with than it was.

Her phone vibrated once against the desk and skidded into the legal pad.

She looked because some reflex in her still believed a late vibration might carry want instead of damage. The screen lit her hand from below.

JUNE SALAZAR sent you $300.

No note. Just the app's clean green banner and the amount, cheerful as a holiday coupon.

Lena sat very still. The webinar kept running in one ear because she had forgotten to pause it. A man somewhere inside the headphones said, "What we're hearing from consumers is--" and then the sentence drowned in a wash of applause that had probably been sweetened in post.

Three hundred dollars. Too small for justice, big enough to change Thursday.

She put the phone face down and kept working. She tagged a hymn lyric. She fixed a timestamp. She corrected a line where a child had sung "manger" as "danger," then left it "manger" because accuracy was not the same thing as honesty and the client had not hired her for honesty. By 2:10 her wrists had gone hot inside the sleeves of her sweatshirt. She stood up, crossed the four steps to the sink, and drank water out of a jelly jar cloudy with old detergent. When she came back, the phone was still there, dark now, as if it had not done anything.

She opened the app anyway.

The pending transfer sat in the center of the screen. Accept. Decline.

Her checking account, displayed one line above it, held $143.18.

She did the arithmetic because she could not stop doing it. If Farah gave her Saturday, that was maybe one-fifty before taxes if the day ran long. If the pageant client paid on time, another two hundred and change by Monday. The state payment could slide another week if she wanted the penalty notice to keep breeding in the mailbox. PECO could wait until the red print got meaner. Mrs. Alvarado had already knocked once this month and then pretended she had only been dropping off junk mail. Lena did not want to find out what the second version of that knock sounded like.

There ought to have been a cleaner feeling available to her than the one that arrived. Rage, maybe. Principle. Something that stood upright.

The apartment answered first: cold window glass at her shoulder, draft under the sill, old coffee thickening in the mug, the ache in the base of her thumb from keying timestamps, the fact that the tailor would unlock downstairs at eight and the day would begin asking for money in voices that had bodies attached to them.

Refusal gave her exactly one thing: a cleaner story about herself while she got poorer inside it. The wire, the ring, the months she had spent shaping her life around a man built out of June's theft and her own appetite would stay gone either way.

She pressed Accept.

The app gave her a small green check and a pulse of brightness like she had won something.

Lena laughed once, a hard ugly sound that scraped her throat. Then she moved the three hundred straight into the account she used for rent before she could start thinking about where June's hands had been when she sent it. Typing the transfer amount felt like touching the same hot pan twice because the first burn had not taught her enough.

She finished the webinar. She finished the pageant. At dawn she sent both files, scrubbed the metadata one last time because she no longer trusted herself to have done anything right the first time, and lay down on top of the blanket with her clothes still on.

Sleep came in strips. SEPTA rumbled somewhere beyond Broad. A scooter coughed at the corner. Somebody downstairs rolled up the tailor-shop grate. She was awake again by nine-thirty with one side of her face creased from the blanket seam and a taste in her mouth like old pennies.

Farah texted at 9:42.

Can you come in at noon. Holiday cases just landed and Luis called out.

Lena texted back yes before her body had time to object.

The shop was warmer than her apartment and smelled like citrus peel, wet cardboard, and the mineral sting of spilled white wine drying on concrete. Farah had on a men's gray cardigan with one elbow shiny from wear and a strip of clementine pith stuck unnoticed to the cuff. Her silvered temples were damp from hauling boxes in from the sidewalk. She did not ask Lena whether she had slept.

"Dolly's by the Lambrusco," she said. "If your back goes, I can't afford the paperwork."

"You know how to make a girl feel wanted."

"You're wanted. By the entire state of Pennsylvania and also by twelve customers who think prosecco appears by prayer."

That was how Farah did care when she knew you were too raw for pity: she gave you a task with a joke welded onto it and trusted the task to carry the rest.

Lena spent four hours breaking down cardboard, price-gunning sparkling rose, and carrying cases from the narrow back room to the holiday display by the front window. The weight steadied her better than sleep would have. By the third trip her forearms were humming. By the fifth she had sweat cooling between her shoulder blades under the sweatshirt. Farah's holiday playlist kept trying to turn the room into something glossy and forgiving, then losing the fight to the compressor in the cheap beverage fridge.

At three-thirteen, while Lena was crouched by a stack of Cava with a box cutter between her teeth, her phone buzzed again in her apron pocket.

She knew before looking that it would not be anybody good.

It was not June. It was Mrs. Alvarado.

Need rent by tomorrow morning, mija. Plumber came. I can't float everyone.

Mrs. Alvarado did not usually use exclamation points. That was almost worse.

Lena looked at the text, then at the glass storefront where her own reflection floated over the winter beer display: hair half-fallen from its knot, box-cutter nick on her thumb, sweatshirt front dusted with cardboard grit. She had the three hundred. She had two hundred and some change coming from caption files if the clients behaved like people instead of portals. She had maybe ninety in cash from the tips jar Farah used to split on Saturday nights and had let her take early twice this fall.

She still felt behind in a way that made language stupid. Behind on rent, behind on taxes, behind on becoming the kind of woman who could afford disgust.

Farah came up behind her carrying a case against her hip. "What happened."

Lena showed her the text.

Farah read it and snorted through her nose. "Everybody's got a plumber this month. Go on your break and get a money order before the check-cashing place closes."

"I can wait till tomorrow."

"You can also get pneumonia on principle. Go now."

Lena almost said no out of habit. Then she caught herself doing it, caught the old stupid reflex to treat help like accusation, and hated how familiar it felt. She set the case down.

The check-cashing place on Passyunk had a line almost to the door and a television in the corner playing a daytime court show with the volume off. Lena stood between a man buying scratch-offs and a woman in pink scrubs counting folded twenties into piles on the counter. The transfer from June had already settled. It lived inside her balance now, indistinguishable from caption money and shop cash except in the place where she knew.

When her turn came, she asked for a money order for eleven hundred and twenty-five dollars and watched the clerk stamp it with bored force. The carbon paper smell, the ink, the old Plexiglas clouded by a thousand breath marks: all of it felt more honest than the app had.

She carried the money order three blocks to Mrs. Alvarado's house and slipped it through the mail slot because she could not bear a conversation on top of the transaction. As she was turning away, the door opened anyway.

Mrs. Alvarado stood in quilted house slippers and an Eagles sweatshirt with the collar stretched wide enough to show one bra strap. Her penciled eyebrows gave her a permanently skeptical look even when she was trying to be kind.

"I was not stalking you," she said.

"Good, because I'd have to charge more."

That got a brief smile. Mrs. Alvarado took the money order and glanced at the amount. "January goes up twenty-five. I told everybody in November."

Lena had read the notice. She had put it under a magnet shaped like a tomato and then taught herself not to see it.

"I know."
